Answer to the First Objection: Knowledge Does Multiply
Author presents two responses to the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's two-pronged response to the objection that knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns.

Core Idea
Knowledge either corresponds to knowns or relates to them; in both cases it multiplies.

The opponent argues that because knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns, it cannot change when they change. The author counters with two strong arguments. First, he rejects the premise that knowledge does not multiply by showing that knowledge, whether a form or a relation, must differ as knowns differ. Second, he shows that even if we accept the premise, the conclusion does not follow. Pay close attention to how he distinguishes between form and relation, and how he identifies a flawed analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the two 'وجوه' or aspects the author presents.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The objection claims that because knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns, it must not change when they change. Grasp this before seeing the response.
3
Analyze the first response (rejection of premise)
The author argues that knowledge does multiply, whether it is a form corresponding to the known or a specific relation. Both entail multiplicity.
The argument uses dichotomy: either form or relation.
4
Analyze the second response (concession without entailment)
Even if we grant the premise, the conclusion does not follow. The opponent's reasoning is an invalid analogy lacking a common property. The author's own proof shows change is necessary.
This is a logical move: accepting the premise but denying the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الجواب عن الوجه الأول: وهو أنه لما لم يتكثر العلم بتكثر المعلومات وجب أن لا يتغير بتغيرها، فهو من وجهين الأول: إنا لا نسلم أن العلم لا يتكثر بتكثر المعلومات والدليل عليه: أن العلم إما أن يكون صورة مطابقة للمعلوم، وإما أن تكون نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم. فإن كان الأول وجب تكثر المعلوم عند تكثر المعلومات، لأن الأشياء المطابقة للماهيات المختلفة، يجب كونها مختلفة. وإن كان الثاني فكذلك أيضاً لما بينا أن النسبة إلى الشيء مغايرة للنسبة إلى غيره. بدليل: أنه يصح تعقل إحدى النسبتين حال الذهول عن الأخرى. والثاني: هب أنا سلمنا أن العلم لا يجب أن يتكثر عند تكثر المعلومات. فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم أن لا يتغير عند تغير المعلومات؟ فإن ذلك قياس من غير جامع. والدليل القاهر الذي ذكرناه يدل على أنه يجب أن يتغير عند تغير المعلومات.
As for the answer to the first objection: that because knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns, it must not change with their change. It is from two aspects: The first: we do not accept that knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns. The evidence for this is that knowledge is either a form corresponding to the known, or a specific relation between the knower and the known. If it is the first, then knowledge must multiply when knowns multiply, because things that correspond to different quiddities must be different. And if it is the second, likewise, because we have shown that the relation to one thing is different from the relation to another. The proof is that it is valid to conceive one relation while being oblivious to the other. The second aspect: even if we concede that knowledge does not necessarily multiply with the multitude of knowns, why do you say that it necessarily does not change when the knowns change? For that is an analogy without a common property. The compelling proof we have mentioned indicates that it must change when the knowns change.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The author addresses the claim that because knowledge does not multiply with the multitude of knowns, it must not change when they change. He provides two responses. First, he rejects the premise that knowledge does not multiply; he argues that if knowledge is a form corresponding to the known, then multiplication of knowns requires multiplication of knowledge. And if knowledge is a relation, then each relation is distinct. Second, even if we accept the premise, it does not follow that knowledge does not change; the opponent's reasoning is an invalid analogy. The author's own proof shows that knowledge must change with changes in knowns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a single indivisible quality and thus cannot multiply or change.
Correction: The author shows that knowledge can be understood as forms or relations, which do multiply and change with their objects.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the first objection, what is the author's second aspect (الوجه الثاني)?
A) He proves that knowledge must multiply
B) He concedes the premise but denies the conclusion
C) He rejects the evidence
D) He argues from analogy

Key Takeaway
Knowledge must differ or multiply as its objects differ, because knowledge is either a form corresponding to the known or a relation to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter new evidence that challenges a long-held religious understanding, do you instinctively treat your knowledge as a fixed, unchangeable form, or do you allow it to adapt and multiply? What would it cost you to be open to change?
?
The author argues that knowledge must differ when its objects differ. Are there areas of your faith—like your understanding of Allah's names, or the nature of the Hereafter—where you have settled for a single, static image rather than letting your knowledge grow as you learn more? What holds you back from deeper study?
?
If knowledge were a relation, why do you often relate to Allah's commands as if they are distant facts rather than personal, living demands? How might your worship change if you saw each act of obedience as a distinct relation that shapes your knowledge of Him?

Knowledge as Disclosure: Answering the Second Doubt
The author argues that knowledge is identical to disclosure, refuting a quality beyond it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that knowledge is identical to disclosure and that any supposed additional quality beyond disclosure fails to be knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the disclosure itself, not a separate quality behind it.

The opponent claims knowledge is a special quality that reveals realities. The author counters by reaffirming that knowledge is nothing but the disclosure. He then systematically examines whether a quality distinct from disclosure could be called knowledge, showing that both possibilities lead to the conclusion that knowledge changes when disclosure changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's method of refutation: he first states his own position, then considers the opponent's view and tests its implications.
The argument uses a conditional structure: 'if this, then that'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author examines two possibilities if knowledge were a separate quality: (1) it does not necessitate the specific relation, (2) it does. Find these two cases in the text.
Look for 'إن كان يوجبه' and 'أما إن قلنا'.
3
Trace the conclusion of each scenario
For case (1), the quality fails to be knowledge because knowledge is defined as disclosure. For case (2), the cessation of the relation (disclosure) implies the cessation of the quality via the logical rule of concomitant.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
The author uses the principle that if a necessary concomitant (lazim) ceases, the accompanied (malzum) must also cease. This is a key reasoning tool in kalam.
This argument reinforces that knowledge is not an unchanging entity but is tied to disclosure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: وهي قولهم: إن العلم صفة مخصوصة تنكشف بها حقائق المعلومات. فنقول: قد ذكرنا في كتاب العلم أن العلم لا معنى له إلا هذا الانكشاف، وإلا هذا التبيين المخصوص، فأما إثبات صفة وراء هذه النسبة المخصوصة، وسوى هذه الإضافة المخصوصة، فقد ذكرنا في كتاب العلم أن الدليل لم يدل على ثبوته البتة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنه لما دل الدليل على أن هذه النسبة المخصوصة قد تغيرت. كان ذلك حكماً بأن العلم قد تغير. ثم نقول: سلمنا أن العلم أمر مغاير لهذه النسبة المخصوصة، إلا أنا نقول: إن ذلك الشيء المخصوص. هل يوجب النسبة المخصوصة إلى المعلوم المخصوص أو لا يوجيه؟ فإن كان يوجبه فهو ليس بعلم. لأن العلم عبارة عن هذا الانكشاف، وهذا التجلي. ولا شك أن الانكشاف والتجلي حالة نسبية بين العلم وبين المعلوم. فإذا فرضنا صفة ليست هي نفس هذا التجلي ولا موجبة لهذا التجلي، امتنع كونه علماً. وأما إن قلنا: إن تلك الصفة المخصوصة موجبة لهذه النسبة المخصوصة، ولهذه الإضافة المخصوصة، فعند زوال هذه النسبة المخصوصة قد زالت لازمة ذلك العلم. وزوال اللازم يدل على زوال الملزوم. فههنا يجب القطع أيضاً بزوال ذلك العلم
As for the answer to the second doubt: which is their statement that knowledge is a specific quality by which the realities of known things are disclosed. So we say: We have mentioned in the Book of Knowledge that knowledge has no meaning except this disclosure and this specific manifestation. As for establishing a quality beyond this specific relation and other than this specific attribution, we have mentioned in the Book of Knowledge that the proof has not indicated its existence at all. And when this is established, we say: Since the proof has indicated that this specific relation has changed, that is a judgment that knowledge has changed. Then we say: Suppose we concede that knowledge is something other than this specific relation, but we say: That specific thing, does it necessitate the specific relation to the specific known or not? If it necessitates it, then it is not knowledge, because knowledge is nothing but this disclosure and this manifestation. There is no doubt that disclosure and manifestation are a relative state between knowledge and the known. So if we suppose a quality that is neither this manifestation itself nor necessitating this manifestation, it is impossible for it to be knowledge. And if we say that that specific quality necessitates this specific relation and this specific attribution, then when this specific relation ceases, the necessary concomitant of that knowledge has ceased. And the cessation of the necessary concomitant indicates the cessation of the thing it necessarily accompanies. Here also one must necessarily judge the cessation of that knowledge.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a doubt. The opponent claims that knowledge is a specific quality by which realities are disclosed. The author responds by first reiterating his position from earlier that knowledge is nothing but disclosure itself, and there is no quality beyond that disclosure. Then he argues that since the disclosure (which is a specific relation) has changed, it follows that knowledge has changed. He then considers the possibility that knowledge is something other than the relation. He examines two cases: (1) If that other thing does not necessitate the relation, then it is not knowledge; (2) If it does necessitate the relation, then the cessation of the relation necessitates the cessation of that thing, proving again that the knowledge has changed.

اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a quality that exists independently of the disclosure of the known.
Correction: The author shows that any such quality either fails to be knowledge (if it doesn't necessitate disclosure) or is subject to change along with disclosure (if it does necessitate it).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if we suppose a quality that is not itself the manifestation and does not necessitate the manifestation, what is its status?
A) It is knowledge because it is a quality
B) It is impossible for it to be knowledge
C) It is the same as disclosure
D) It is a necessary concomitant of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not a separate static quality but is identical to the dynamic disclosure of the known, so any change in disclosure directly implies a change in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge is identical to the active disclosure of truth in your heart, then consider a core Islamic belief you claim to know (e.g., 'Allah is watching me'), but you have not actively disclosed it to your heart today. Is that belief truly knowledge at this moment, or is it just a memory? What concrete action can you take right now to re-disclose that truth to yourself?
?
The author argues that there is no proof for a quality beyond disclosure. In your relationship with Allah, do you ever rely on a sense of 'having knowledge' as a static possession, without actually engaging your heart with the reality of what you claim to know? What would it mean for you to live as if knowledge is nothing but the moment-by-moment disclosure of truth?
?
The lesson concludes that if the disclosure relation changes, knowledge itself changes. Reflect on a time when you felt your certainty about an Islamic truth weaken—did your knowledge actually change? Or was it never genuine disclosure? How does this challenge your understanding of what it means to 'know' your faith?

Knowledge as Relational Disclosure
The author argues that knowledge is the specific disclosure and must change when the disclosure changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical argument that knowledge is not a static attribute but a relational disclosure, and that any change in the disclosure necessarily implies a change in knowledge itself.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the disclosure itself; when the disclosure changes, knowledge changes.

This passage is a tightly reasoned argument about the nature of knowledge (al-ʿilm). The author begins by recalling that knowledge is nothing but the specific disclosure of realities. He then systematically shows that if the disclosure relation changes, knowledge must change as well—even if one concedes that knowledge is something distinct from that relation. This is a foundational kalām argument that ties the definition of knowledge to the problem of change and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Note the logical connectives like 'if', 'then', 'because'.
The passage includes footnote markers; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the author's starting claim
Find where the author states that knowledge has no meaning other than disclosure. This is the foundation of the whole argument.
He also says that proving an additional attribute beyond disclosure has no evidence.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
The author presents a concession: suppose knowledge is different from the relation. Then it either necessitates the relation or not. Trace both branches and see how both lead to the same conclusion that knowledge must change.
Notice the principle: if the necessary (lāzim) vanishes, the necessitated (malzūm) must also vanish.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why the author goes through both branches. How does this argument strengthen the claim that knowledge is essentially relational?
This is a typical kalām method of exhaustive case analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخصوصة تنكشف بها حقائق المعلومات . فنقول : قد ذكرنا في كتاب ه العلم ، : ه : ( أن العلم ( (١) : لا معنى له إلا هذا الانكشاف ، وإلا هذا التبيين المخصوص ، فأما إثبات صفة وراء هذه النسبة المخصوصة ، وسوى هذه الإضافة المخصوصة ، فقد ذكرنا في كتاب ( العلم ، : أن الدليل لم يدل على ثبوته البتة . ( وإذا ثبت هذا ) (۲) فنقول : إنه لما دل الدليل على أن هذه النسبة المخصوصة قد تغيرت . كان ذلك حكماً بأن العلم قد تغير . ثم نقول : سلمنا : أن العلم أمر (۳) مغاير لهذه النسبة المخصوصة ، إلا أنا نقول : إن ذلك الشيء المخصوص . هل يوجب النسبة المخصوصة إلى المعلوم المخصوص أو لا يوجيه ؟ ( فإن كان يوجبه ) (٤) فهو ليس بعلم . لأن العلم عبارة عن هذا الانكشاف ، وهذا التجلي . ولا شك أن الانكشاف والتجلي حالة نسبية بين العلم وبين المعلوم. فإذا فرضنا صفة ليست هي نفس هذا التجلي ولا موجبة لهذا التجلي ، امتنع كونه علماً . وأما إن قلنا : إن تلك الصفة المخصوصة موجبة لهذه النسبة المخصوصة ، ولهذه الإضافة المخصوصة ، فعند زوال هذه النسبة المخصوصة قد زالت لازمة ذلك العلم . وزوال اللازم يدل على زوال الملزوم . فههنا يجب القطع أيضاً بزوال ذلك العلم
Specific [disclosure] by which the realities of known things are disclosed. We say: We have mentioned in the Book of Knowledge that knowledge has no meaning other than this disclosure, other than this specific clarification. As for establishing an attribute beyond this specific relation and other than this specific addition, we have mentioned in the Book of Knowledge that the evidence has not indicated its existence at all. (And when this is established) we say: Because the evidence has indicated that this specific relation has changed, that was a ruling that knowledge has changed. Then we say: We grant that knowledge is something different from this specific relation, but we say: That specific thing – does it necessitate the specific relation to the specific known or does it not necessitate it? (If it necessitates it) then it is not knowledge, because knowledge is this disclosure and this manifestation. There is no doubt that disclosure and manifestation are a relative state between the knower and the known. So if we suppose an attribute that is neither this manifestation itself nor necessitating this manifestation, its being knowledge is impossible. And if we say that that specific attribute necessitates this specific relation and this specific addition, then at the vanishing of this specific relation, the necessary of that knowledge has vanished. And the vanishing of the necessary indicates the vanishing of the necessitated. So here also one must decide on the vanishing of that knowledge.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and its change. The author begins by recalling that knowledge is nothing but the specific disclosure (al-inkishāf) or clarification (al-tabyīn) of realities. He then states that establishing an additional attribute beyond this relational disclosure has no proof. Next, he argues that if the evidence shows that the specific relation (al-nisba al-makhṣūṣa) has changed, then knowledge itself has changed. He then considers a concession: suppose knowledge is something distinct from this relation. That distinct thing either necessitates the relation to the known or not. If it does not necessitate it, it cannot be knowledge because knowledge is precisely that disclosure. If it does necessitate the relation, then when the relation vanishes, that which necessitates it must also vanish (by the principle that the necessary follows the necessitated). Thus knowledge must cease. The argument proceeds in a step-by-step logical fashion, typical of kalām reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge is a static attribute that does not change even when the known object changes.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge is essentially a relational disclosure; if the disclosure changes, knowledge necessarily changes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between the specific relation (al-nisba al-makhṣūṣa) and knowledge (al-ʿilm)?
A) The relation is an accidental property that can change without affecting knowledge.
B) The relation is identical to knowledge; if the relation changes, knowledge changes.
C) The relation is a separate entity that persists even when knowledge changes.
D) The relation is a condition for the existence of knowledge but not part of its essence.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is the specific disclosure of realities; any change in that disclosure necessitates a change in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge is the specific disclosure of realities, then what reality have you been avoiding having disclosed to you? What would it cost you to truly know it, and why haven't you paid that price? Today, consider one step you can take to seek that disclosure.
?
Recall a time when your understanding of something changed dramatically. Did you resist that change because you thought your 'knowledge' was fixed? How might this lesson's argument—that knowledge must change when disclosure changes—free you to embrace new truths about yourself, others, or your faith?
?
The author says evidence indicates the specific relation has changed. Is there an area of your life where the evidence is clear that your current 'knowledge' is no longer aligned with reality, yet you cling to it? What would it look like to let that knowledge disappear and seek a new disclosure?

Refutation of the Third Objection
Sanusi refutes the third objection on persisting belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun refute the claim that persistence of belief yields knowledge of future events.

Core Idea
Persistence of belief is impossible; even if possible, it doesn't guarantee knowledge.

This lesson examines the third objection against the position that belief changes with disclosure. The objectors argue that if a belief persists until the event, then the believer becomes knowledgeable. Imam Sanusi counters by denying the possibility of such persistence and shows that even if granted, the scenario fails to produce knowledge. We will work through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: If a belief persists until the predicted event occurs, does the believer become knowledgeable?
The objectors assume persistence is possible.
2
First answer: Impossibility of persistence
Most mutakallimun say persistence on the known is impossible; belief is an accident that occurs anew each moment.
This is a key doctrine in kalam.
3
Second answer: The hypothetical unknown
Even if persistence were possible, how do the objectors know what would happen? They have no basis for their claim.
4
Third answer: The condition of time awareness
Even if the belief persisted, without awareness of the day's arrival, knowledge is not generated.
This shows the objectors' scenario is incomplete.
5
Conclusion: The objection supports the author
The objectors' own example actually proves the author's point that knowledge changes with disclosure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة (() : وهي قولهم : إنا إذا فرضنا بقاء اعتقاد : أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً إلى وقت حصول الدخول فإنه بتعين ذلك عالماً بأنه حصل ذلك الدخول . فنقول : الجواب عنه أن نقول : العلم يصير. أكثر المتكلمين يقولون : البقاء على المعلوم ممتنع ، بل إنه عرض (3) يحدث فحالاً . وإذا كان هذا البقاء ممتنعاً ، فكيف عرفوا : أنه لو حصل هذا الممتنع فإن الأمر يكون كذا وكذا ؟ ومن الذي أخبرهم بأن الأمر كما يقولون ؟ ثم نقول : إنا قد بينا أنه لو بقي هذا الاعتقاد إلا أنه لم يعلم أن النهار قد حضر ، فإنه لا يصير (١) بسبب ذلك الاعتقاد عالماً بأنه قد دخل البلد في هذه الساعة ، فيثبت أن الصورة التي ذكروها من أدل الدلائل عا . صحة قولنا .
As for the answer to the third objection, which is their statement: 'If we suppose the persistence of the belief that Zayd will enter the town tomorrow until the time of entry occurs, then he becomes knowledgeable that that entry has occurred.' We say: The answer is that we say: Knowledge becomes... Most of the mutakallimun say: Persistence on the known is impossible; rather it is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs anew. And if this persistence is impossible, how did they know that if this impossible thing occurred the matter would be such and such? And who told them that the matter is as they say? Then we say: We have shown that if this belief persisted but he did not know that the day had arrived, he would not become by that belief knowledgeable that he entered the town at this hour. So it is established that the picture they mentioned is among the strongest proofs of the correctness of our statement.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objection (shubha) states: Suppose a belief persists until the predicted event occurs; then the believer becomes knowledgeable of the event at the moment it happens. The author answers: Most mutakallimun hold that persistence (baqāʾ) on a known (maʿlūm) is impossible; it is a new accident (ʿaraḍ) each moment. Even if we grant its possibility, how do the objectors know what would happen in that impossible scenario? Furthermore, the author argues that even if the belief persisted but the person lacked awareness of time, he would not automatically become knowledgeable. Hence the example actually supports the author's own position that knowledge changes with disclosure.

Key Terms
شبهة
بقاء
اعتقاد
العلم
عرض
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief can persist unchanged until the known event occurs, and that persistence alone yields knowledge.
Correction: Mutakallimun hold that belief is an accident that ceases instantly; it does not persist. Even if it did, knowledge requires awareness of the present moment.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of persistence (baqāʾ) on the known?
A) It is possible but rare.
B) It is impossible; belief is a renewed accident.
C) It is necessary for knowledge.
D) It occurs only for divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The objectors' scenario fails because persistence of belief is impossible, and even if it were possible, knowledge requires additional conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief about your own character that you have held for years—perhaps that you are patient or generous. Have you ever actually examined whether that belief is true, or do you simply assume it persists? What would it mean to truly know yourself rather than just believe?
?
When you recite 'I know that Allah is One' in your prayer, is that a persistent belief you've carried since childhood, or is it a living knowledge that discloses itself anew each time? What would happen if you could not rely on yesterday's certainty?
?
If your most deeply held conviction about your faith were challenged tomorrow by an event that seems to contradict it, would you discover that your belief was merely persistent habit or genuine knowledge anchored in disclosure? What would your response reveal about the state of your heart?

The First Proof Against the Second Group and Its Objection
The later theologians present their first proof of infinite regress against the second group, followed by an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the later mutakallimun used the requirement of a knowing agent to argue that the origination of knowledge leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires a knowing agent, leading to an infinite regress that is impossible.

The later mutakallimun present their first proof against the second group's view that knowledge can originate without prior knowledge. They argue that any originated knowledge must have an originator who is knowledgeable, which would require an infinite chain of knowledges. This passage states the proof and begins an objection against it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's structure
Follow the logical chain: originated knowledge → requires an originator → originator must be knowledgeable → that knowledge itself is originated → leads to infinite regress. Recognize the conclusion that this regress is impossible.
The proof assumes that a sound action (fiʿl muḥkam) requires the agent to know what he is doing.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on محدث (originator), فاعل (agent), فعل محكم (sound action), عالم (knowledgeable), and تسلسل (infinite regress). Understand how each term functions in the argument.
These terms are foundational for kalam discussions on causation.
3
Engage with the objection
Note that the passage begins an objection: 'someone might say this proof is weak because...' but the text cuts off. Consider what weakness could be pointed out—perhaps the assumption that the originator must be knowledgeable is not always true?
The objection is incomplete; we only have its beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتكلمون المتأخرون : فقد استدلوا على فساد هذا القول بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : قالوا : هذا العلم لما حدث بعد أن لم يكن فلا بد له من محدث وفاعل ومحدث ذلك العلم وفاعله لا بد ) (۳) وأن يكون عالماً به . لما بينا أن صدور الفعل المحكم عن الفاعل مشروط بكون ذلك الفاعل عالماً . فيكون إحداث هذا العلم مشروطاً بحصول علم آخر قبله ، والكلام في ذلك العلم السابق ، كالكلام في هذا العلم وذلك يوجب التسلسل وهو محال · ولقائل أن يقول : هذه الحجة ضعيفة لأنكم رتبتم هذا الكلام على أن هذا
As for the later mutakallimun, they argued for the corruption of this statement with proofs: The first proof: They said: This knowledge, when it occurred after not having been, must have an originator and agent (muḥdith wa fāʿil). And the originator of that knowledge and its agent must be knowledgeable of it, because we have shown that the issuing of a sound action from the agent is conditioned by that agent being knowledgeable. So the origination of this knowledge is conditioned by the existence of another knowledge before it. And the discussion concerning that prior knowledge is like the discussion concerning this knowledge, and that necessitates infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. And someone might say: This proof is weak because you have arranged this discourse on the basis that...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) of the later mutakallimun against the second group's view. They argue: When knowledge comes into existence after non-existence, it requires an originator (muḥdith) and agent (fāʿil). That agent must be knowledgeable of the knowledge it creates, because producing a sound, well-ordered action (fiʿl muḥkam) requires the agent to be knowledgeable. Thus, the creation of this knowledge is conditioned upon the prior existence of another knowledge. The same reasoning applies to that prior knowledge, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Then the text begins an objection: someone might say this proof is weak because 'you have arranged this discourse on the basis that...' – but the text cuts off, so the full objection and any answer are not included in this window.

Misconception: The proof denies that knowledge can originate at all.
Correction: The proof does not deny origination; it argues that if knowledge originates, it leads to infinite regress, which the second group must avoid.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first proof against the second group?

B) The origination of knowledge leads to infinite regress, which is impossible.
C) Knowledge cannot be originated because the agent does not need to be knowledgeable.
D) The second group's view is correct because knowledge can arise without a prior knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that the origination of knowledge leads to infinite regress because the originator must already possess knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Was your action truly 'sound'—grounded in genuine knowledge, or driven by ignorance or haste? How can you ensure that your actions are built on firm knowledge, especially in matters of faith?
?
This proof shows that the chain of knowledge cannot regress infinitely; it must end in a First Knower. Consider the source of your own knowledge about God: does it trace back to a reliable origin, or is it based on assumptions or inherited beliefs? What are you willing to do to verify that foundation today?

If the originator of an action must possess knowledge of that action, what does that imply for your own accountability before God? Every deliberate act of yours is witnessed by the All-Knowing. Are you acting as if you are aware of that? What change in your daily conduct does this awareness demand?

Three Proofs That Knowledge of Knowledge Is Distinct from Knowledge
Three proofs for the distinctness of meta-knowledge from first-order knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational arguments proving that knowing that you know something is a distinct cognitive state from simply knowing it, and why this distinction is essential for avoiding an infinite regress in epistemology.

Core Idea
Knowing that you know is not the same as knowing — three proofs from difference of objects, awareness, and entailment.

The author now defends the key assumption that knowledge of knowledge (meta-knowledge) is genuinely distinct from first-order knowledge. An objector challenges this distinction, and three proofs are offered based on the difference between knowledge and its object, the possibility of being aware of something while unaware of being aware, and the absurd consequence of identifying them. This lesson unpacks each proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author lists three arguments labeled 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'. Make a mental note of the structure.
The objector's statement is included for context, but focus on the three proofs.
2
Understand Proof 1: Difference of objects
Proof 1 argues that since the known thing (ma'lum) is different from knowledge (ilm), the knowledge of each must also be different. So knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge are distinct because their objects differ.
This is a simple syllogism: if A and B are different, then knowledge of A and knowledge of B are different.
3
Understand Proof 2: Unawareness
Proof 2 points out that we can know a thing (e.g., the sky is blue) while being unaware (ghafil) that we know it. At that moment, the knowledge of the thing exists, but the knowledge of that knowledge is absent (non-existent). Since existence and non-existence are different, the two knowledge states are different.
This relies on the intuitive experience of knowing without reflection.
4
Understand Proof 3: Absurd consequence of identity
Proof 3 uses a reductio: if knowledge of a thing were identical to knowledge of that knowledge, then all higher-order levels (knowing that you know that you know...) would be identical and simultaneously present. But we know from immediate experience that this is not the case, so they must be distinct.
This shows that identifying them leads to an infinite actual simultaneous presence, which is absurd.
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
These three proofs together establish that meta-knowledge is genuinely distinct from first-order knowledge. This distinction is crucial for the earlier argument that an infinite regress of knowledges would entail an infinite regress of causes and effects.
Without this distinction, the regress argument would collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال هذا بناء على أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بالشيء، فما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك؟ ثم نقول: المعلوم لا يكون علة الحصول العلم به، فلم يلزم من حصول هذا المعلوم حصول علل ومعلولات غير متناهية؟ ونجيب عن الأول. فنقول: الذي يدل على أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للشيء وجوه: الأول: إن المعلوم مغاير للعلم فوجب أن يكون العلم بأحدهما مغاير للعلم بالآخر. والثاني: إنه يمكننا أن نعلم الشيء حال ما نكون غافلين عن العلم بالعلم به، والموجود مغاير للمعدوم. فوجب أن يكون العلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بالعلم به. الثالث: وهو أنه لو كان العلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالعلم به، فيلزم أن تكون هذه المرتبة الثالثة نفس المرتبة الثالثة، وكذا القول في سائر المراتب. فعند العلم بالشيء، يلزم أن تكون كل المراتب حاضرة بالفعل، ولما علمنا بالضرورة أنه ليس الأمر كذلك علمنا أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بذلك الشيء. وأما السؤال الثاني: وهو قوله: المعلوم لا يكون علة للعلم به، فلم يلزم من حصول مراتب لا نهاية لها في هذه العلوم حصول علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها دفعة؟ فنقول: الجواب عنه من وجهين: الأول: إن الذات الموصوفة موجبة للعلم وكل مرتبة من تلك المراتب فإنها شرط لتأثير الذات المخصوصة في المرتبة الثانية، وشرط العلة يجري مجرى نفس العلة وحينئذ يعود السؤال المذكور. والثاني: وهو أن كل مرتبة متقدمة من هذه العلوم فإنها مستلزمة للمرتبة المتأخرة. فالدليل الدال على بطلان علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها يكون بعينه قائماً في هذه الصورة. والله أعلم.
It should not be said that this is based on the fact that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of the thing, so what is the proof that it is so? Then we say: the known thing is not a cause for the occurrence of knowledge of it, so it does not follow from the occurrence of this known thing the occurrence of infinite causes and effects? And we answer the first. We say: what indicates that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from the thing are aspects: First: The known thing is different from knowledge, so it must be that knowledge of one of them is different from knowledge of the other. Second: We can know a thing while we are unaware of knowledge of knowledge of it, and the existent is different from the non-existent. So it must be that knowledge of the thing is different from knowledge of knowledge of it. Third: That if knowledge of the thing were the same as knowledge of knowledge of it, then it would entail that this third rank is the same as the third rank, and likewise for all ranks. So upon knowledge of the thing, it would entail that all ranks are present actually. And since we know necessarily that it is not so, we know that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of that thing. As for the second question, which is his statement: the known thing is not a cause for knowledge of it, so why does it follow from the occurrence of infinite ranks in these knowledges the occurrence of infinite causes and effects at once? We say: the answer to it is from two aspects: First: The described Essence is necessitating of knowledge, and each rank of those ranks is a condition for the influence of the particular Essence in the second rank, and the condition of a cause runs in the course of the cause itself, and then the mentioned question returns. Second: That each preceding rank of these knowledges entails the subsequent rank. So the proof that indicates the falsity of infinite causes and effects applies exactly in this case. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (suʼāl wa-jawāb) that defends the earlier infinite regress argument against two challenges. The objector first challenges the assumption that knowledge of knowledge (al-ʻilm bi al-ʻilm bi al-shayʼ) is distinct from knowledge itself, and then questions whether the known thing is a cause of knowledge (so infinite regress would not entail infinite causes).

The response gives three proofs for distinctness:
1. The known (maʻlūm) is different from knowledge (ʻilm), so knowledge of one cannot be the same as knowledge of the other.
2. We can know a thing while being unaware of knowing that we know it (ghāfil), so the two states are different (existent vs. non-existent).
3. If they were identical, then knowing one object would entail all higher levels of self-knowledge (e.g., knowing, knowing that you know, etc.) being simultaneously present, which is contrary to necessity (ḍarūrah).

For the second question, the answer has two aspects:
1. Although the known is not a direct cause, each level is a condition (sharṭ) for the next, and a condition has the same effect as a cause in producing regress.
2. Each higher level entails (mustalzim) the lower, so the same proof against infinite causal chains applies to these entailing relations.

Thus the objection is refuted, and the original argument stands.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
مغاير
غافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge are the same cognitive state.
Correction: The author provides three proofs showing they are distinct: different objects, possibility of unawareness, and absurdity of identity.

Checkpoint
Which proof relies on the fact that we can know something without being aware that we know it?
A) Proof 1: Difference of objects
B) Proof 2: Unawareness
C) Proof 3: Reductio of identity
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three proofs establish unequivocally that meta-knowledge is distinct from first-order knowledge, a necessary premise for the argument against infinite regress of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second proof shows that you can know something while being unaware of that knowledge. Consider your faith: are there truths about Allah that you profess but rarely bring to conscious reflection? How would your worship change if you deliberately called those truths to mind today?
?
The third proof warns that if knowledge and meta-knowledge were identical, all ranks would collapse. In your spiritual life, do you ever treat your knowledge of Islamic principles as though it were the same as deep, reflective understanding? What is one area where you need to move from mere knowing to knowing that you know?

Answering the Objection: Does Knowledge Depend on the Known as a Cause?
Two responses to the objection that infinite knowledge levels do not entail infinite causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the infinite regress argument against the objection that the known thing is not a cause of knowledge, using the concepts of condition (shart) and entailment (iltizam).

Core Idea
Even if the known is not a cause, the regress still holds because conditions and entailments act like causes.

A second objection is raised: if the known thing is not a cause of knowledge, then the infinite regress of knowledges does not entail infinite causes. The author offers two responses. First, each level is a condition for the next, and conditions function like causes. Second, each level entails the next, so the same proof against infinite causal chains applies. This lesson examines these responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two responses
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objection: the known is not a cause, so why would infinite knowledge levels cause infinite causes? Then locate the two responses labeled 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
The objection is phrased as 'فلم يلزم من حصول مراتب لا نهاية لها في هذه العلوم حصول علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها دفعة؟'
2
Understand the first response: condition (shart)
The first response says that the Essence (God) is the cause of knowledge, but each rank is a condition (shart) for the influence of that Essence on the next rank. Since conditions run in the course of the cause, the same problem of infinite regress of causes returns.
A condition is something that must be present for a cause to produce its effect. Here, each knowledge level is a condition for the next, so they are causally relevant.
3
Understand the second response: entailment (iltizam)
The second response states that each preceding rank entails (mustalzim) the subsequent rank. This means there is a necessary connection. Therefore, the proof that infinite causes are impossible applies exactly to this chain of entailments.
Entailment is a logical relation that mirrors causality in producing an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the significance
Together, these two responses show that even if the known itself is not a cause, the regress of knowledge levels involves causal-like relations (conditions and entailments) that generate the same problem of infinite regress. Thus the objection fails.
This preserves the original argument that an infinite regress of knowledges would imply an infinite regress of causes.

Source Text

Will vs. Natural Inclination: Two Examples
Two examples show will is distinct from natural inclination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational distinction between will (irada) and natural inclination (mayl al-tabi'a) as demonstrated by two concrete examples.

Core Idea
Will and natural inclination are independent: one can exist without the other.

This lesson presents two vivid examples from everyday experience that help us separate two often-confused concepts: the will to act and the natural inclination or desire. The first example shows will operating without any apparent benefit or harm, while the second shows desire without will and will without desire. By examining these cases, the text establishes that will is a distinct faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the examples speak for themselves.
Notice the clear structure: first example, second example, conclusion.
2
Identify the first example
Find the example of choosing between equal options (two cups or two loaves). Understand why this shows will without benefit or harm.
The key phrase: 'لا لمرجح' (without a preference factor).
3
Identify the second example
This example has two parts: the patient who craves fruit but refrains, and the ascetic who wills worship without desire. Note how each part illustrates a different combination.
One shows natural inclination without will; the other shows will without natural inclination.
4
Contrast the outcomes
Compare the two examples: in the first, will occurs without benefit/harm; in the second, will and desire occur independently. This shows they are not the same.
The conclusion explicitly states: 'the difference between natural inclination and will becomes clear.'
5
Reflect on the distinction
Think about why this distinction matters for understanding human action and moral responsibility. The will is not enslaved to desire.
This distinction is foundational in Islamic ethics and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : : إن المخير بين شرب القدحين ، وأكل الرغيفين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر ، لا لمرجح ، وكذلك الهارب من السبع الضاري إذا وصل إلى موضع يتشعب منه طريقان متساويان من جميع الوجوه ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني ، من غير أن يحصل بسبب ذلك الترجيح منفعة زائدة ، أو يندفع بسببه مضرة زائدة، وههنا حصلت الإرادة من غير أن يحصل معها جلب النفع ، أو دفع الضرر .
والثاني : إن المريض قد يشتهي تناول الفاكهة جداً، مع أنه لا يأكلها ويحترز عنها ، فههنا ميل الطبع قائم والإرادة غير حاصلة ، والرجل الزاهد العابد قد يريد إقامة الصلوات والعبادات مع أنه لا يشتهي الإقدام عليها ، لما فيها من المتاعب والمشاق بها . فههنا الإرادة حاصلة مع أن ميل الطبع غير حاصل . فظهر بهذا : الفرق بين ميل الطبيعة وبين الإرادة
First: Indeed, the one who has a choice between drinking two cups and eating two loaves, he chooses one over the other without a preference factor, and likewise the one fleeing from a ferocious lion when he arrives at a place from which two equally suitable paths in all aspects diverge, he chooses one without the second, without there occurring by that preponderance an additional benefit or being repelled by it an additional harm, and here the will (al-irada) occurs without occurring with it attraction of benefit or repulsion of harm. Second: Indeed, the patient may strongly desire to eat fruit, yet he does not eat it and refrains, so here the natural inclination (mayl al-tab') is present and the will is not present. And the ascetic worshiper may intend (yurid) to perform prayers and acts of worship while he does not desire (yashtahi) to undertake them because of the hardships and difficulties in them. So here the will is present while the natural inclination is not present. So by this, the difference between natural inclination (mayl al-tabi'a) and will (al-irada) becomes clear.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage provides two empirical examples to differentiate between natural inclination (mayl al-tabi'a) and will (irada). The first example: a person given a choice between two equal options (cups or loaves) picks one without any additional benefit or harm, showing that will operates independently of obvious advantage. The second example presents two scenarios: a patient craves fruit but refrains (desire without will), and an ascetic wills worship without desire (will without desire). The conclusion explicitly states that by these examples the distinction between the two becomes manifest.

Key Terms
إرادة
ميل الطبيعة
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that will always follows desire.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that will can exist without desire (ascetic) and desire can exist without will (patient), proving they are independent.

Checkpoint
What do the two examples in this passage collectively demonstrate?
A) That will and natural inclination are identical.
B) That will can exist without natural inclination and vice versa.
C) That natural inclination always precedes will.
D) That will is a form of desire.

Key Takeaway
Will and natural inclination are independent: each can exist without the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you done something good without actually wanting to do it? How does the example of the ascetic worshiper challenge your view of the sincerity of your actions? Will you today choose one act of worship you don't feel like doing and reflect on your intention?
?
Think of a time you strongly desired something harmful but chose to refrain. What does that say about the nature of your will compared to your natural inclination? Does this realization empower you to resist temptations more consciously?
?
If will is not simply following desire, then what truly drives your choices? Are your choices free or merely reactions? In the next hour, notice when you act from mere inclination versus genuine will, and ask yourself which guides your life.

Two Philosophical Objections to the Definition of Will
Philosophers raise two objections against defining will as non-necessary preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections philosophers raise against defining will as an attribute that causes preponderance without necessity, and why they argue that such a definition is incoherent.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that no cause can be 'possibly effecting' – it either necessitates its effect or is impossible.

This passage presents the philosophers' critique of a specific theological definition of will. They challenge the idea that will can cause a preponderance without necessity, arguing that every efficient cause either necessarily produces its effect or cannot produce it at all. They also question the distinction between preponderance and origination, claiming they are the same concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and the literal translation carefully. Pause to note the structure: two objections announced at the beginning.
The philosophers begin with 'As for your saying...' and then present two problems.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues that an efficient cause must either necessitate its effect or be impossible. There is no third category of 'possible' causation. This makes the definition of will as causing preponderance without necessity incoherent.
They refer to earlier discussions about the capable agent.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection challenges the distinction between will (causing preponderance) and power (causing origination). They argue that preponderance is simply the occurrence of one side of a possible, which is origination. So the definition adds nothing.
They demand a clear distinction between preponderance and origination.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these objections undermine the standard theological definition of will. Think about why the philosophers find the concept of a non-necessary cause unintelligible.
This connects to broader debates about divine attributes and determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : أما قولكم : الإرادة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي : الممكن على الآخر من غير وجوب ، ومن غير تأثير كلام مشكل من وجهين (۱) : الأول : أن هذا الكلام يقتضي إثبات مؤثر يؤثر لا على سبيل الوجوب ، وحينئذ تعود المباحث المذكور في باب تأثير القادر. وذلك لأن المؤثر إما أن يكون مستجمعاً لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية. وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول وجب ترتب الأثر عليه، فيكون ذلك المؤثر مؤثراً على سبيل الوجوب ، لا على سبيل الصحة ، وإن كان الثاني كان ترتب الأثر عليه ممتنعاً فيكون ذلك ممتنع التأثير، لا يمكن التأثير (٢) ( فعلمنا أن الشيء إما أن يكون واجب التأثير أو ممتنع التأثير، فأما أن يكون ممكن التأثير ) (۳) فهذا غير معقول ، وتمام تقرير هذا البحث قد سبق ذكره في باب القادر ·
والثاني : هب أنا إذا عقلنا وجود مؤثر يؤثر على سبيل الصحة - إلا أن هذا - هو القدرة فيها الفرق بين القدرة وبين الإرادة ؟ أما قوله : إنها تؤثر في الترجيح لا في التكوين فنقول : هذا الكلام إنما يتم ببيان الفرق بين الترجيح وبين التكوين ، فإنا لا نعقل من الترجيح إلا وقوع أحد جانبي الممكن : بسببه ، وهذا هو التكوين. وإثبات مفهوم آخر يسمى بالترجيح والتخصيص مغاير للمفهوم الحاصل من التكوين أمر غير معقول
The philosophers said: As for your saying: 'The will (al-irada) is an attribute that necessitates preponderance (tarjih) of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without necessity (wujub) and without effect (ta'thir)' – this is a problematic statement in two respects (wajhayn): First: This statement entails affirming an efficient cause (mu'aththir) that effects not in a necessary way, and then the discussions mentioned in the chapter on the influence of the capable agent (qadir) return. That is because the efficient cause is either possessing all the matters considered in effectivity, or not. If the first, then the effect must follow from it, so that efficient cause is an efficient cause by way of necessity, not by way of possibility (sihha). If the second, then the effect's following from it is impossible, so it is impossible of effect, cannot effect. (Thus we know that a thing is either necessarily effecting or impossible to effect; as for its being possibly effecting, this is unintelligible (ghayr ma'qul).) The full exposition of this investigation has already been mentioned in the chapter on the capable agent. Second: Suppose we conceive of an efficient cause that effects by way of possibility – yet that is power (qudra), so what is the difference between power and will? As for your saying: 'It (will) effects in preponderance (tarjih), not in origination (takwin)', we say: This statement is only complete by clarifying the difference between preponderance and origination. For we do not conceive of preponderance except as the occurrence of one side of the possible due to it, and this is origination. And affirming another concept called preponderance and specification (takhsis) distinct from the concept obtained from origination is an unintelligible thing.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' two objections to a specific definition of will. The first objection argues that any efficient cause (mu'aththir) either necessitates its effect or is impossible; there is no third category of contingent causation. Thus, describing will as effecting preponderance without necessity is incoherent. The second objection challenges the distinction between will (as effecting preponderance) and power (as effecting origination). The philosophers argue that preponderance is nothing other than bringing one side into existence, which is exactly origination. Postulating an additional concept is unintelligible.

Key Terms
إرادة
ترجيح
وجوب
تأثير
قدرة
تكوين
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition of will as 'preponderance without necessity' is logically sound and distinct from power.
Correction: The philosophers show that this definition leads to the impossible concept of contingent causation and fails to differentiate preponderance from origination, making it incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the first philosophical objection to defining will as preponderance without necessity?
A) It implies that will is identical to power.
B) It requires the impossible concept of an efficient cause that is neither necessary nor impossible.
C) It confuses preponderance with origination.
D) It denies the existence of will.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that the definition of will as non-necessary preponderance is problematic because all causes are either necessary or impossible, and preponderance is indistinguishable from origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own choices—like deciding between two options—do you believe your will causes the choice without necessity? If the philosophers are right that only necessary or impossible causation exists, what does that imply about your freedom? How do you reconcile this challenge with your daily experience of choosing?
?
The philosophers claim that ‘preponderance’ is the same as ‘origination’. In other words, when you ‘choose’ to do something, is your act of choosing itself the very act of doing? Or do you sense a gap between your will and the resulting action? Examine your own experience: does the distinction hold up, or does the philosophers’ critique hit home?
?
If God's will is defined as non-necessary preponderance, and this definition is shown to be unintelligible, how does that change your understanding of divine attributes? Are you willing to accept that your conception of God's will might be flawed? What is at stake for your faith if this argument stands?

Two Rebuttals to the Philosopher's Objection on Choice
Author gives two rebuttals to the philosopher's choice objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosopher's claim that choice can occur without any benefit or harm, using two distinct arguments that reveal the hidden role of mental preponderants and general benefit-seeking.

Core Idea
Choice always involves either a specific mental preponderant or a general pursuit of benefit.

The philosopher's objection tries to show that choice can be unmotivated by citing examples like choosing between two equal loaves. The author offers two powerful rebuttals. The first argues that subtle mental factors (closeness, ease, inherent benefit) always tip the scales, creating a preponderant even when we are unaware. The second argues that even without a specific preponderant, the underlying desire to eat or drink is itself a pursuit of benefit, proving that no action is truly free from the motive of benefit or harm.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosopher's objection
Read the philosopher's claim: a person chooses one of two equal options without any specific benefit or harm. This challenges the necessity of a preponderant.
The author's response targets exactly this claim.
2
Study the first rebuttal: denial of equality
The author denies that the two options are truly equal. He explains that mental factors (closeness, ease, inherent benefit) always create a preponderant desire, preventing indifference.
Notice the distinction between occurrence, persistence, and recollection of the preponderant.
3
Study the second rebuttal: general benefit
Even if no specific preponderant exists, the act of choosing to eat/drink itself stems from a general desire for benefit and avoidance of harm.
This refocuses on the overarching motive.
4
Connect to kalam principles
Understand how these rebuttals uphold the kalām view that every act of will requires a preponderant (murjih), whether specific or general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : المخير بين ( أكل الرغيفين ) (۱) وشرب القدحين ، يختار أحدهما من غير جلب منفعة ، أو دفع مضرة يختص بها ذلك الواحد . فنقول : الكلام عليه من وجهين :
الأول : لا نسلم أنهما يتساويان في جلب (۲) المنافع ، والمصالح المتعلقة بالأبدان (۴) ، بل لا بد وأن يتخيل أن أحدهما أقرب إليه ، وأحدهما أسهل عليه ، وهو أنفع في نفسه ، وربما كان عظيم الحرص على الأكل والشرب ، فلما وقع بصره على أحدهما عظمت رغبته فيه . فصارت قوة رغبته فيه مانعة له عن تحويل النظر عنه إلى غيره ، فبقيت رغبته مقصورة عليه وغير متعدية منه إلى غيره . وبالجملة : فحصول المرجح الذهني غير، وبقاء ذلك المرجح غير . وتذكر ذلك المرجح بعد نسيانه غير. ولا يلزم من فقدان هذا الثالث فقدان الثاني والأول .
والوجه الثاني في الجواب : هب أنه يأخذ أحدهما دون الآخر لا لمرجح . إلا أن الذي لأجله صدر عنه هذا الفعل شدة رغبته في أصل الأكل والشرب وتلك الرغبة عبارة عن طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة، فلولا طلب النفع لما أقدم على أخذ أحد الرغيفين، وشرب أحد القدحين ، ولولا الفرار من دفع ضرر السبع ، لما اختار سلوك أحد الطريقين ، فيثبت أن الحامل له على هذا الفعل ليس إلا طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة . وحينئذ يعود ما ذكرناه .
And as for his saying: 'The one given a choice between eating two loaves and drinking two cups, he chooses one without attracting a benefit or repelling a harm specific to that one.' We say: Discussion on it is from two aspects:
First: We do not grant that they are equal in attracting benefits and interests related to bodies (abdān), rather he must imagine that one is closer to him, and one is easier for him, and it is more beneficial in itself, and perhaps he is extremely eager for eating and drinking. So when his eyes fall on one, his desire for it increases. So the strength of his desire for it prevents him from turning his sight from it to another, so his desire remains confined to it and not transferred to another. In summary: the occurrence of the mental (dhihnī) preponderant (murjih) is one thing, and the persistence of that preponderant is another, and the recollection of that preponderant after forgetting it is another. And the loss of this third does not necessitate the loss of the second and first.
The second aspect of the answer: Suppose he takes one without a preponderant. Yet the reason for which this act issued from him is his intense desire for the act of eating and drinking itself, and that desire is the pursuit of benefit (manfa'a) and repulsion of harm (madarra). Were it not for the pursuit of benefit, he would not have proceeded to take one of the loaves or drink one of the cups; and were it not for fleeing from the repulsion of the harm of the lion, he would not have chosen to traverse one of the two paths. So it is established that the motive behind this action is nothing but the pursuit of benefit and repulsion of harm. And then what we mentioned returns.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage contains the author's response to the philosophers' criticism that the example of choosing between two equal options demonstrates will without benefit or harm. The author offers two rebuttals. First, he denies that the two options are truly equal in terms of benefit; there are subtle mental factors (closeness, ease, inherent benefit) that produce a mental preponderant (murjih dhihnī). He distinguishes between the occurrence, persistence, and recollection of this preponderant, arguing that the absence of recollection does not entail absence of the preponderant itself. Second, even granting no specific preponderant, the underlying desire to eat or drink is itself a pursuit of benefit and avoidance of harm, showing that the action is still motivated by these general ends.

منفعة
مضرة
رغبة
تخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A choice between two equal options can be completely unmotivated (without any benefit or harm).
Correction: The author shows that either subtle mental factors create a preponderant (first rebuttal) or the general desire for benefit underlies the action (second rebuttal).

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the author's response, what prevents the person from turning his sight to the other option?
A) The absence of any preference.
B) The strength of his desire for the one he sees first.
C) The equal attraction of both options.
D) The lack of benefit in either.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that every choice, even between seemingly equal options, is ultimately driven by a preponderant—whether specific mental factors or a general pursuit of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made that you considered trivial or equal. Can you now identify the hidden preponderant (mental or general) that actually drove your choice? Are you comfortable acknowledging that even your smallest choices are never random? What would change if you lived with that awareness every moment?
?
The author argues that a general pursuit of benefit is always at play, even when we are unaware. If you honestly examine your daily habits—eating, sleeping, scrolling—how much of what you do is truly for Allah's pleasure versus your own immediate comfort? What would it take to shift that preponderant toward the akhirah?
?
Consider a time you forgot why you chose something. Does this lesson reassure you that there was still a reason (the preponderant occurred and persisted), or does it make you uneasy that you might not be fully aware of your own motives? How can you train yourself to be more conscious of the preponderants in your life, especially those that lead you away from obedience?

The Fourth Argument: Preponderance Cannot Be Necessary or Possible
The fourth argument eliminates necessity and possibility for preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why preponderance (tarjih) cannot be either necessary or possible in the context of divine actions.

Core Idea
Preponderance is either necessary or possible — both lead to contradictions.

This lesson presents the fourth argument in a series refuting the idea that preponderance can occur without a preponderator. The author succinctly eliminates both necessity and possibility as viable grounds for preponderance, pointing to necessary consequences for each.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise structure: the argument splits into two branches.
The argument is only a few lines, but it packs a logical punch.
2
Analyze the two branches
Identify the two possibilities: preponderance by necessity and by possibility. Understand the absurdity of each.
The first leads to making God's action compulsory; the second cannot produce preponderance without a preponderator.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why both options are false and what this implies about God's actions. How does this support the idea that God acts voluntarily?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن اقتضاء هذا الترجيح إما أن يكون على سبيل الوجوب ، أو على سبيل الإمكان ، والأول باطل ، وإلا لزم أن يصير موجباً بالذات ، والثاني باطل ، لأن مع الجواز والتساوي يمتنع حصول الترجيح . وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم .
The fourth argument: the implication of this preponderance is either necessarily or possibly; the first is false, otherwise it would necessitate becoming necessary by essence; and the second is false, because with possibility and equality the occurrence of preponderance is impossible. This is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (الحجة الرابعة) in a series of arguments against preponderance (ترجيح). The author analyzes the nature of the preponderance implied by the opponent's position. He states that such preponderance must be either by way of necessity (على سبيل الوجوب) or by way of possibility (على سبيل الإمكان). The first option is false because if it were necessary, it would entail that God becomes necessary by essence (موجباً بالذات), which is absurd since God's actions are voluntary. The second option is also false because if the preponderance were possible, then with equal possibility (مع الجواز والتساوي) no preponderance can occur without a preponderator. Thus both options are invalid, concluding the chapter.

Key Terms
ترجيح
وجوب
إمكان
موجباً بالذات
جواز
تساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think preponderance can be merely possible without needing a preponderator.
Correction: The author shows that with mere possibility and equality, preponderance cannot occur; a preponderator is still needed.

Checkpoint
In the fourth argument, why is the first option (preponderance by way of necessity) invalid?
A) It would make God's actions compulsory, negating His free will.
B) It would imply that God is not eternal.
C) It requires a preponderator.
D) It leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that preponderance cannot be explained by necessity or possibility, confirming that all preponderance requires a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that every preponderance in the universe has a preponderator, would that change how you see your own choices? Do you act as if your preferences are uncaused?
?
The argument shows that God's actions are not necessary but voluntary. Does that make God more real to you, or harder to understand? How does this truth affect your trust in His decisions?

The Third Proof: Vision Distinct from Impression
Al-Razi gives the third proof and concludes the discussion on sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's third proof that vision is not identical to physical impression, and why this distinction is essential for a proper theory of perception.

Core Idea
A stronger physical impression does not mean stronger vision—proving they are distinct.

Al-Razi has already given two arguments that vision is not mere physical impression. In this passage, he presents the third and perhaps most intuitive argument: consider looking at the sun. The physical impression is intense, yet vision is impaired. This simple observation shows that the state called 'sight' and the state called 'impression' are different. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage twice—once for flow, then again while following the translation. Note the key terms: إبصار (sight), رؤية (vision), انطباع (impression), تأثر (affection).
This passage concludes the entire discussion on sight.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Al-Razi sets up a conditional: if sight were identical to physical affection, then a more complete affection would mean more complete sight. This is the core logical structure.
3
Apply the counterexample
He then provides a counterexample: looking at the sun strengthens the affection but weakens sight. This disproves the conditional, so the identity claim is false.
Notice the contrast: strong impression but weak vision.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Because the consequent (stronger vision) does not follow, the antecedent (identity) is false. Thus, vision and impression are distinct states. This is the final proof.
This conclusion verifies the meaning of sight (تحقيق معنى الإبصار).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليست من باب الانكشاف والتجلي. وأما الرؤية فإنها من باب الانكشاف والتجلي فوجب أن تكون الرؤية حالة مغايرة لهذا الانطباع والتأثر . والثالث : إنه لو كانت الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والرؤية عبارة عن هذا التأثر ، لوجب أن يقال : كلما كان التأثر أكمل ، وجب أن يكون حصول الإبصار أكمل وليس الأمر كذلك. لأنا إذا نظرنا إلى قرص الشمس فإنه يقوي ذلك التأثر ويكمل ، لا لأن الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والرؤية تضعف فعلمنا أن هذا التأثر والانطباع حالة مغايرة للإبصار والإدراك . فهذا هو الكلام في تحقيق معنى الإبصار .
It is not of the category of disclosure and manifestation. As for vision, it is of the category of disclosure and manifestation, so it is necessary that vision be a state distinct from this impression and affection. And the third: if the state called sight and vision were identical to this affection, it would be necessary that whenever the affection is more complete, the occurrence of sight must be more complete, but it is not so. For when we look at the disk of the sun, it strengthens and completes that affection, not because the state called sight and vision weakens; so we know that this affection and impression is a state distinct from sight and perception. So this is the discussion concerning the verification of the meaning of sight.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on the nature of sight. The author (al-Razi) states that sight is not of the category of mere physical disclosure or manifestation (inkishaf) but rather vision (ru'ya) is itself a form of disclosure. He then gives the third proof: if sight were identical to physical impression, then a stronger impression would yield stronger vision, but looking at the sun causes a strong impression yet weakens vision. Therefore, the impression and sight are distinct. The passage wraps up the verification of the meaning of sight.

Key Terms
إبصار
