Hearing as a Distinct Faculty: The First Proof Against Physical Reduction
Al-Razi presents the immediate distinction between hearing and knowledge and the first argument against physical reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first argument that hearing is not merely a physical impression but a distinct perceptive state.

Core Idea
Hearing through a wall proves hearing is more than sound waves.

In this passage, al-Razi begins his discussion on the nature of hearing by drawing an immediate intuitive distinction between hearing and knowledge. He then presents a concrete argument: we can hear someone speaking perfectly through a dense wall, yet the sound waves cannot travel through the wall intact. This leads to a powerful refutation of the purely physical account of hearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the overall flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the initial distinction
Notice how al-Razi distinguishes between knowing the quiddity of sound and the act of hearing itself. This distinction is intuitive.
3
Understand the physical reductionist view
Some argue that hearing is just the physical impression of sound waves on the eardrum. Find the evidence they give (e.g., loud sounds tearing the eardrum).
This is the view al-Razi will refute.
4
Follow the first proof against reductionism
Al-Razi presents his core proof: we hear speech completely through a wall. But the wall is an obstacle to the sound wave. How can that be?
This is the heart of the lesson.
5
Consider the objection and refutation
The opponent objects that pores in the wall might transmit the wave (like an open door). Al-Razi refutes: the wall's density destroys the wave's form, so coherent speech cannot be transmitted through pores.
Thus hearing must be a distinct faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السماع فنقول : إنا قبل سماع الصوت قد تعلم حقيقة الصوت وماهيته. فإذا سمعنا بالأذن ، علمنا حصول تفرقة بديهية بين الحالتين : أعني حالة العلم وحالة السماع . وهذه التفرقة معلومة بالبديهية . ثم من الناس من قال : تلك التفرقة عائدة إلى أن الصوت إنما يحدث عند تموج الهواء. فإذا تأدى ذلك التموج إلى سطح الصماخ فقرع ذلك السطح فذلك القرع - وهو الحركة العنيفة - هو الذي لأجله حصل التفاوت بين الحالتين . والدليل على حصول هذا التأثر : إنه إذا قوي الصوت ظهر الأثر الشديد في الأذن ، حتى أنه ربما تمزق سطح الصماخ عند قوته ، وعند سماع أصوات البوقان ، يحس الإنسان كان دماغه يظهر فيه شيء من التمزق والتفرق ، فيثبت أن عند حصول السماع يحصل هذا الانطباع والانفعال . وعند هذا قال بعضهم : التفاوت الحاصل بين الحالتين عائد إليه . ومن الناس من قال : هذا التفاوت مسلم لا نزاع فيه ، إلا أنا ندعي حصول التفاوت بين هذين النوعين من الإدراك ، أعني النوع المسمى بالعلم ، والنوع المسمى بالسماع . والدليل عليه : أن السماع أمر مغاير لهذا النوع ، ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن الإنسان إذا تكلم من وراء الجدار ، فإنا قد نسمع كلامه بالتمام والكمال ، ومن المحال أن يتأدى ذلك التموج إلينا بكماله وتمامه . لأن الجدار الحائل بين القائل وبين السامع عائق عن ذلك وليس لهم أن يقولوا : إنه حصل في الجدار مسام ومنافذ وثقب . فلا يمتنع تأدي ذلك الهواء المتموج في تلك الثقب والمنافذ إلى سطح الصماخ . قالوا : والدليل عليه : أن الإنسان إذا جلس في بيت مظلم ولذلك البيت باب ، وتكلم إنسان خارج ذلك البيت ، فإن كان باب البيت مفتوحاً كان السمع أكمل ، وإن كان باب البيت مردوداً صار السمع أضعف وأخفى . وذلك يدل على ما قلناه فإنا نقول : هذا القدر باطل . وذلك لأن الجدار جسم كثيف فالتموج الحاصل بسبب نطق ذلك المتكلم وكلامه إذا وصل إلى ذلك الجدار فإنه لا يبقى على صورته وعلى شكله ، فإن الشكل الحاصل في الهواء اللطيف ممتنع البقاء عند وصوله إلى الجدار الكثيف ، والذي ينفذ من ذلك الهواء في ثقب الجدار ، وفي مسامه الضيقة ، لا يبقى على ذلك الشكل الذي حدث خارج ، فوجب أن يمتنع سماع ذلك الكلام على شكله ونظمه ، وحيث حصل ، علمنا أن الذي قالوه باطل
As for hearing, we say: before hearing the sound, we have known the reality and quiddity of sound. So when we hear with the ear, we know the occurrence of an immediate distinction between the two states: namely, the state of knowledge and the state of hearing. And this distinction is known intuitively. Then among people some said: that distinction goes back to the fact that sound only occurs when air vibrates. When that vibration reaches the surface of the eardrum and strikes that surface, then that striking—which is violent motion—is what causes the difference between the two states. The evidence for the occurrence of this impression: when sound is strong, a severe effect appears in the ear, so much that perhaps the surface of the eardrum is torn due to its strength, and when hearing the sounds of trumpets, a person feels as if something of tearing and splitting occurs in his brain. Thus it is established that upon hearing this impression and affection occurs. At this point some said: the difference between the two states goes back to it. And among people some said: this difference is admitted without dispute, but we claim the occurrence of a difference between these two types of perception, namely the type called knowledge and the type called hearing. The evidence for it: hearing is something distinct from this type, and it is indicated by proofs: the first: that if a person speaks from behind a wall, we may hear his speech completely and perfectly, and it is impossible for that vibration to reach us in its entirety and completeness, because the wall intervening between the speaker and the hearer is an obstacle to that. And they cannot say: there are pores, openings, and holes in the wall; so it is not impossible for that vibrating air to transmit through those holes and openings to the surface of the eardrum. They said: the evidence for it: if a person sits in a dark room and that room has a door, and a person outside that room speaks, then if the door of the room is open, the hearing is more complete, and if the door of the room is closed, the hearing becomes weaker and fainter. That indicates what we said. So we say: this much is false. Because the wall is a dense body, so the vibration occurring from the speech of that speaker, when it reaches that wall, it does not remain in its form and shape; the shape occurring in the subtle air is impossible to remain upon reaching the dense wall. And that which penetrates of that air through the holes of the wall and its narrow pores does not remain in that shape that occurred outside. So it is necessary that hearing that speech in its shape and order is impossible. And where it occurs, we know that what they said is false.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage introduces the discussion on hearing. The author notes that before hearing a sound we know its essence; upon hearing we intuitively distinguish between knowledge (of sound) and hearing itself. Some claim the difference is due to physical impression (the striking of the eardrum). The author presents a counterview: hearing is a distinct state. The first proof: we hear speech through a wall perfectly, yet the sound waves cannot pass intact through a dense wall. An objection is raised that pores in the wall could transmit waves, with the example of an open door improving hearing. The author refutes this: the wall's density destroys the wave pattern, so coherent speech cannot be transmitted through pores. Since we still hear it, the cause must be a distinct hearing state, not physical transmission.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that hearing cannot be reduced to physical impression because we hear coherent speech through a wall, which would block and disrupt the sound waves.

Checkpoint

Hearing cannot be reduced to the physical transmission of sound waves; it is a distinct faculty that perceives sound even when the wave is disrupted.

?
The wall example shows that physical obstacles cannot block true hearing. What 'walls' do you build—distractions, biases, pride—that prevent you from truly hearing the truth when it is spoken to you? What will you do today to tear down one such wall?

Second Proof: Hearing Direction Shows Distinctness
Al-Razi proves hearing is not mere ear-impression because we perceive the direction of sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ability to perceive the direction of sound proves that hearing is not merely a physical impression on the eardrum but a distinct cognitive state.

Core Idea
Hearing direction reveals that hearing is more than just the ear-drum's vibration.

Al-Razi presents a second argument to demonstrate that hearing is a distinct perception separate from the physical impact on the ear. He appeals to a common experience: when we hear a sound, we know its direction. This simple fact, he argues, cannot be explained if hearing were only the ear-drum's impression. In this lesson, you'll follow the logical steps of his proof and see how he uses an analogy with touch to strengthen his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Let the original flow before analyzing.
The passage is a short but dense argument.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break the passage into premise, hypothesis, consequence, analogy, and conclusion. Notice how each part builds on the previous.
Use the guided_breakdown as a tool.
3
Reflect on the analogy with touch
Compare the author's use of the tactile sense: it feels contact but not direction. Why does this support his conclusion?
Think about why direction discrimination implies a distinct faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهو أنا إذا سمعنا الصوت، فإنا نعلم أنا سمعناه من الجانب الأيمن أو الجانب الأيسر. وهذا يدل على أنا سمعنا الصوت في مكانه الذي حدث فيه. إذ لو قلنا : بأن المسموع ليس إلا القرع الذي حدث في داخل الصماخ فقط، فإنه يجب أن لا يعرف بحسب القوة السامعة مكان ذلك الصوت وجهته، كما أن القوة اللامسة لما لم تُحس إلا بالكيفية المدموسة حال وصولها إلى العضو فإنها لا تميز بين الجهة التي منها جاءت تلك الكيفية المحسوسة وبين غيرها . فيثبت بهذا أن الإدراك المسمى بالسماع حالة مغايرة لتأثر الصماخ وانفصاله من التموج الواصل إليه
The second proof: it is that when we hear the sound, we know that we heard it from the right side or the left side. This indicates that we heard the sound in its place where it occurred. For if we say that the heard is nothing but the striking that occurred inside the eardrum only, then it would be necessary that the hearing faculty does not know the place and direction of that sound, just as the tactile faculty, since it only perceives the quality of touch upon its arrival at the organ, does not distinguish between the direction from which that perceived quality came and another. Thus it is established by this that the perception called hearing is a state distinct from the impression of the eardrum and its excitation from the vibration reaching it.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that hearing is a distinct state. The author argues that when we hear a sound, we can tell its direction (right or left), meaning we perceive the sound at its original location. If hearing were merely the effect of a physical strike on the eardrum, we would only sense the strike itself, not its origin. By analogy, the sense of touch feels a quality on the skin but cannot tell the direction from which the object came. Since hearing does discriminate direction, it must be a different cognitive state beyond the physical impression.

Misconception: One might think direction is inferred from differences in sound intensity, not directly perceived.
Correction: The author argues that direction is directly known by the hearing faculty, not inferred, because if it were only a physical impression, we would not have direction information at all.

Checkpoint

A) Because an ear-impression cannot have a direction.
B) Because the tactile sense can also tell direction.
C) Because if hearing were only the strike on the eardrum, we would only feel the strike, not its source.
D) Because sound always comes from one direction.

Key Takeaway
The ability to hear direction proves that hearing is a distinct cognitive state, not just an eardrum impression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider that every sound you hear—including the voices of loved ones, the recitation of scripture, or the sounds of nature—carries with it an inherent sense of direction. This proof shows that your hearing is not just a passive physical process but an active perception from the soul. Is there a sound today that you have been ignoring or taking for granted? How will you listen more attentively as an act of worship?
?
This argument distinguishes between a physical event (eardrum vibration) and a cognitive state (hearing). In your daily life, do you often reduce your experiences to mere physical sensations, forgetting that there is a deeper perceiver within? What would change if you started seeing every sensory experience as a direct interaction between your soul and the world?

Proof That Divine Attributes Cannot Be Possible
Proof via reductio that attributes are not possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that divine attributes cannot be possible in themselves, and why possibility implies a cause that leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Possibility requires a cause; self-causation or eternal origination is absurd.

This passage presents a concise but powerful argument that the divine attributes cannot be merely 'possible' (mumkin). The author uses a reductio ad absurdum, showing that if the attributes were possible, they would need a cause—leading to contradictions that force us to conclude they are necessary. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the unique ontology of the Divine Essence and Its attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: first the claim, then the two absurd consequences.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Main Claim
The author states that every possible-in-itself (mumkin li-dhatihi) must have a cause. Here, the only candidate cause is the divine essence itself.
This sets up the reductio.
3
Understand the Two Absurdities
The passage lists two reasons why the essence cannot be the cause of its own attributes. First, that would make the essence both agent and recipient simultaneously. Second, need for a cause only arises at the time of origination, otherwise the thing would be eternally produced.
Each absurdity is a separate logical objection.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
If the attributes caused by another, they would be originated (hadith), which is impossible. Therefore, they cannot be caused by the essence either. The only remaining option is that the attributes are necessary (wajib), not possible.
This conclusion preserves the eternity and necessity of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها ممكنة للدواعها ، فهو أن كل ممكن لذاته ، لا بد له من سبب . وذلك السبب ليس إلا تلك الذات، وذلك محال من وجهين : الأول : إنه إذا كان المؤثر هو تلك الذات والقابل أيضاً تلك الذات فيلزم كون الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً ، وهو محال . والثاني : هو أن الاحتياج لا يحصل إلا عند زمان الحدوث، وإلا لزم تكوين الكائن ، وهو محال ، فلو كانت هذه الصفات معللة بالغير، لكانت حادثة ، لكن كونها حادثة محال ، فيمتنع كونها معللة بالذات
As for the explanation that it is impossible for them to be possible due to their causes [or essences], it is that every possible in itself necessarily has a cause. That cause is none other than that essence, and that is absurd from two aspects: First: If the effecter is that essence and the recipient is also that essence, then it follows that one thing is both recipient and effecter, which is absurd. Second: Need only occurs at the time of origination; otherwise, the coming-to-be of the existent would be necessitated, which is absurd. So if these attributes were caused by another, they would be originated, but their being originated is absurd; therefore, it is impossible for them to be caused by the essence.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine attributes cannot be possible (mumkin) in themselves, but must be necessary (wajib). The author argues that if attributes were possible, they would require a cause (sabab). The only candidate for that cause is the divine essence itself. But this leads to two absurdities: (1) the same thing would be both agent (mu'aththir) and recipient (qabil), which is impossible; (2) need for a cause only arises at the time of origination (huduth), otherwise the thing would have been eternally produced, which is also impossible. Therefore, the attributes cannot be caused by the essence; they must be necessary and uncaused.

Key Terms
ممكن لذاته
واجب لذاته
سبب
مؤثر
قابل
فاعل
حدوث
معللة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument denies the reality of divine attributes altogether.
Correction: The proof only denies that attributes are possible (mumkin), not that they exist. It establishes their necessity and eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can't the divine essence be the cause of its own possible attributes?
A) Because the essence is simple and cannot have parts.
B) Because it would lead to the essence being both agent and recipient, or to eternal production.
C) Because attributes are separate entities.
D) Because the essence is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes cannot be possible because possibility implies a cause, and the only candidate cause leads to logical absurdities; thus the attributes are necessary and uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are necessary and uncaused, do you truly rely on His unchanging mercy and knowledge, or do you act as if His attributes depend on your actions? How will you trust His attributes more this week?
?
The proof shows God cannot be both agent and recipient of attributes. Do you sometimes fragment God in your mind, imagining a 'part' of Him that acts and a 'part' that receives? How does this affect your worship of His absolute oneness?
?
Possibility implies need. Where in your life are you treating God's attributes as 'possible'—as if His forgiveness or help might not be there until you earn it? What would change if you truly believed His attributes are necessary and always present?

The Three Rational Judgments: Necessary, Impossible, Possible
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and their intrinsic natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three rational judgments: necessary by itself, impossible by itself, and possible by itself.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the building blocks of all theological reasoning.

Sanusi begins by laying the conceptual groundwork for all kalam arguments. He defines the three categories that every rational mind can affirm before even considering revelation. This passage gives the precise meaning of each judgment, which is essential for the discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage defining wājib, mumtaniʿ, and mumkin. Notice how each is defined in relation to itself (li-dhātihi).
The phrase 'li-dhātihi' emphasizes that the judgment is intrinsic, not caused.
2
Identify the contrast
See how necessary is 'pure truth' and impossible is 'pure falsehood'. Possible is neither—it depends on an external cause.
3
Reflect on the classification
Ask yourself why these three categories are exhaustive. Every conceivable thing falls into one of them.
This is the foundation for the later argument about Allah's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الواجب لذاته فإنه حق محض لذاته. وأما الممتنع لذاته فهو باطل محض لذاته. وأما الممكن لذاته فإنه لا يترجح وجوده على عدمه، إلا بإيجاد موجد، ولا يترجح عدمه على وجوده، إلا بإعدامه معدم. فعلى هذا: الممكن إذا أخذ من حيث هو هو، فإنه يكون معدوماً، بمعنى أنه ليس له استحقاق الوجود والعدم من ذاته. وإذا كان كذلك، فكل ممكن فهو من حيث إنه هو باطل، وهالك. ولهذا قال سبحانه: {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه}. ولما كان كل ممكن فإنه يكون ممكناً أبداً، وكل ممكن فإنه من حيث هو هو يكون باطلاً وهالكاً لزم أن ما سوى الحق سبحانه فهو هالك أبداً. ولهذا السبب قالوا: لا موجود في الحقيقة إلا الله. وأيضاً: فكل ممكن فهو إنما يكون موجوداً بالنظر إلى تكوين واجب الوجود، فواجب الوجود هو الذي يؤثر في جعل ما سواه حقاً. ولهذا قال: {ويحق الله الحق بكلماته}. فهو سبحانه حق لذاته، ويحق الحق بكلماته. ولما ثبت أنه سبحانه حق لذاته، كان اعتقاد وجوده واعتقاد كونه موصوفاً بصفات العلو والعظمة أحق الاعتقادات، لأن المعتقد لما كان ممتنع التغير كان ذلك الاعتقاد أيضاً ممتنع التغير، وكذا القول في الإقرار به، فهو سبحانه أحق الحقائق بأن يكون حقاً، ومعرفته أحق الحقائق بأن تكون حقاً، والإقرار به أحق الأقوال بأن يكون حقاً.
As for the necessary by itself, it is pure truth by itself. As for the impossible by itself, it is pure falsehood by itself. As for the possible by itself, its existence is not preponderant over its non-existence, except by the bringing-into-existence of a bringer-into-existence, and its non-existence is not preponderant over its existence, except by the annihilating of an annihilator. On this basis: the possible, when taken in itself, is non-existent, meaning that it does not have the right to existence or non-existence from itself. And if this is so, then every possible is, in respect of itself, false and perishing. For this reason He—glory be to Him—said: {Everything is perishing except His Face}. And since every possible is always possible, and every possible is in respect of itself false and perishing, it follows that whatever is other than the Truth—glory be to Him—is always perishing. For this reason they said: There is no existent in reality except God. Also: every possible only exists by consideration of the bringing-into-existence of the Necessary Existent; so the Necessary Existent is the one who effects making what is other than Him true. For this reason He said: {And God establishes the truth by His words}. So He—glory be to Him—is truth by Himself, and He establishes the truth by His words. And since it is established that He—glory be to Him—is truth by Himself, the belief in His existence and the belief that He is described with the attributes of loftiness and greatness are the most worthy of beliefs, because when the believed object is impossible of change, that belief is also impossible of change. And likewise the statement concerning acknowledgment of Him: so He—glory be to Him—is the most worthy of realities to be truth, and knowledge of Him is the most worthy of realities to be truth, and acknowledgment of Him is the most worthy of statements to be truth.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible. The author begins by defining each: the Necessary by itself is pure truth; the Impossible by itself is pure falsehood; the Possible by itself does not have existence or non-existence inherently but requires an external cause. From this he argues that every possible, considered in itself, is not existent but rather false and perishing, citing the Quranic verse {Everything is perishing except His Face}. Since possibles are always possible, they are always perishing, so only the Necessary Being truly exists. He then adds that a possible only exists by being brought into existence by the Necessary, so the Necessary is the one who makes other things true, citing {And God establishes the truth by His words}. Finally, he concludes that because Allah is truth by Himself, belief in Him and acknowledgment of Him are the most worthy to be true, as they correspond to an unchanging object.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممتنع لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'possible' means it can exist on its own without a cause.
Correction: The passage explicitly says a possible has no intrinsic existence or non-existence; it needs an external cause to exist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does a possible by itself (mumkin li-dhātihi) relate to existence?
A) It is necessarily existent.
B) It is necessarily non-existent.
C) It has no intrinsic preponderance for existence or non-existence.
D) It is both existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, and possible—are defined by their intrinsic relation to existence: necessary must exist, impossible cannot exist, and possible depends on an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since you are a possible being, your existence at this very moment is a gift renewed by God. Do you live as if every breath is a cause from Him, or do you take it for granted?
?
The impossible is pure falsehood. Reflect on a belief you hold that contradicts God's oneness. Is it truly impossible? If so, why do you still entertain it?
?
The necessary being alone is self-subsisting. How do your daily actions—your worry, your pride—show that you are not self-sufficient?

Only Allah Truly Exists: The Argument from Possibility
Sanusi argues that only Allah truly exists and that belief in Him is most worthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only Allah is considered truly existent and why belief in Him is most worthy.

Core Idea
Everything other than Allah is perishing in itself; only Allah is pure truth.

Having defined the three rational judgments, Sanusi now draws a profound conclusion: every possible thing, considered in itself, is non-existent, false, and perishing. This leads to the Quranic truth that only Allah's 'Face' endures. He then builds the case that belief in Allah is the most worthy of all beliefs because it corresponds to an unchanging reality.

Guided Path
1
Follow the logical chain
Notice how Sanusi moves from the definition of possible to the conclusion that possibles are perishing. He cites Quran 28:88.
The verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' is central.
2
Understand the ontological claim
A possible, in itself, has no reality. Its existence is borrowed from the Necessary Being. Thus, only the Necessary truly exists.
This is a key tenet of tawḥīd.
3
See the implication for belief
Since Allah is unchanging truth, belief in Him is also unchanging and most worthy. This gives certainty to faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الواجب لذاته فإنه حق محض لذاته. وأما الممتنع لذاته فهو باطل محض لذاته. وأما الممكن لذاته فإنه لا يترجح وجوده على عدمه، إلا بإيجاد موجد، ولا يترجح عدمه على وجوده، إلا بإعدامه معدم. فعلى هذا: الممكن إذا أخذ من حيث هو هو، فإنه يكون معدوماً، بمعنى أنه ليس له استحقاق الوجود والعدم من ذاته. وإذا كان كذلك، فكل ممكن فهو من حيث إنه هو باطل، وهالك. ولهذا قال سبحانه: {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه}. ولما كان كل ممكن فإنه يكون ممكناً أبداً، وكل ممكن فإنه من حيث هو هو يكون باطلاً وهالكاً لزم أن ما سوى الحق سبحانه فهو هالك أبداً. ولهذا السبب قالوا: لا موجود في الحقيقة إلا الله. وأيضاً: فكل ممكن فهو إنما يكون موجوداً بالنظر إلى تكوين واجب الوجود، فواجب الوجود هو الذي يؤثر في جعل ما سواه حقاً. ولهذا قال: {ويحق الله الحق بكلماته}. فهو سبحانه حق لذاته، ويحق الحق بكلماته. ولما ثبت أنه سبحانه حق لذاته، كان اعتقاد وجوده واعتقاد كونه موصوفاً بصفات العلو والعظمة أحق الاعتقادات، لأن المعتقد لما كان ممتنع التغير كان ذلك الاعتقاد أيضاً ممتنع التغير، وكذا القول في الإقرار به، فهو سبحانه أحق الحقائق بأن يكون حقاً، ومعرفته أحق الحقائق بأن تكون حقاً، والإقرار به أحق الأقوال بأن يكون حقاً.
As for the necessary by itself, it is pure truth by itself. As for the impossible by itself, it is pure falsehood by itself. As for the possible by itself, its existence is not preponderant over its non-existence, except by the bringing-into-existence of a bringer-into-existence, and its non-existence is not preponderant over its existence, except by the annihilating of an annihilator. On this basis: the possible, when taken in itself, is non-existent, meaning that it does not have the right to existence or non-existence from itself. And if this is so, then every possible is, in respect of itself, false and perishing. For this reason He—glory be to Him—said: {Everything is perishing except His Face}. And since every possible is always possible, and every possible is in respect of itself false and perishing, it follows that whatever is other than the Truth—glory be to Him—is always perishing. For this reason they said: There is no existent in reality except God. Also: every possible only exists by consideration of the bringing-into-existence of the Necessary Existent; so the Necessary Existent is the one who effects making what is other than Him true. For this reason He said: {And God establishes the truth by His words}. So He—glory be to Him—is truth by Himself, and He establishes the truth by His words. And since it is established that He—glory be to Him—is truth by Himself, the belief in His existence and the belief that He is described with the attributes of loftiness and greatness are the most worthy of beliefs, because when the believed object is impossible of change, that belief is also impossible of change. And likewise the statement concerning acknowledgment of Him: so He—glory be to Him—is the most worthy of realities to be truth, and knowledge of Him is the most worthy of realities to be truth, and acknowledgment of Him is the most worthy of statements to be truth.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible. The author begins by defining each: the Necessary by itself is pure truth; the Impossible by itself is pure falsehood; the Possible by itself does not have existence or non-existence inherently but requires an external cause. From this he argues that every possible, considered in itself, is not existent but rather false and perishing, citing the Quranic verse {Everything is perishing except His Face}. Since possibles are always possible, they are always perishing, so only the Necessary Being truly exists. He then adds that a possible only exists by being brought into existence by the Necessary, so the Necessary is the one who makes other things true, citing {And God establishes the truth by His words}. Finally, he concludes that because Allah is truth by Himself, belief in Him and acknowledgment of Him are the most worthy to be true, as they correspond to an unchanging object.

Key Terms
حق
باطل
هالك
حق محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'only Allah exists' means the universe is an illusion or that God is everything (pantheism).
Correction: The passage means that things other than Allah have no independent reality; their existence is dependent on Allah's creation. They are not illusory but ontologically contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is belief in Allah the most worthy of beliefs?
A) Because it is based on scripture.
B) Because its object (Allah) is unchanging, so the belief is also unchanging.
C) Because it is widely held.
D) Because it gives comfort.

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is truly existent and unchanging; therefore belief, knowledge, and acknowledgment of Him are the most worthy of their kinds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than Allah is perishing in itself, what in your life are you clinging to as if it were permanent? How does this passage change your attachment to worldly things? Commit to one concrete action this week that reminds you that only Allah truly exists.
?
The passage says belief in Allah is most worthy because its object is unchanging. Have you ever felt your faith shaken by doubts or circumstances? How does this argument strengthen your certainty? What step will you take to ground your belief in this truth, rather than in your fluctuating feelings?
?
The passage concludes that acknowledgment of Allah (الإقرار به) is the most worthy of statements. Examine your own speech: do you use Allah's name casually or fail to acknowledge Him in your daily conversations? What change can you make today to ensure your words truly honor Him as the only reality?

The Mu'tazilite Proof: God's Knowledge Prevents Evil
Mu'tazila present two versions of their proof that God's knowledge of evil prevents Him from doing it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God's knowledge of an act's ugliness necessarily prevents Him from committing it, as presented in two versions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of evil necessarily prevents Him from doing it.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God knows an act is evil, that knowledge alone is sufficient to deter Him from doing it. They offered two formulations: one based on rational analogy, the other on necessary knowledge. This passage presents both versions, clarifying the logical structure of their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two versions of the proof.
The first version uses analogy (qiyās); the second claims necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī).
2
Identify the two versions
Find where the first version ends and the second begins. Note the keywords: 'فثبت' for the first, 'والوجه الثاني' for the second.
3
Understand the role of incitement (dāʿiya)
In the second version, the Mu'tazila acknowledge that human knowledge of ugliness can be overcome by appetite. But they argue this is impossible for God.
This is the crux of their argument for divine immunity from evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت أن العلم بالقبح يوجب الصرف عن فعل القبيح، ولما حصل هذا العلم في حق الله تعالى، وجب أن يؤثر في صرفه عن القبيح. والوجه الثاني: هو أن نترك هذا القياس، وندعي العلم الضروري بأن العلم بكون الفعل قبيحاً يوجب الانصراف عنه، اللهم إلا إذا صار هذا الصارف معارضاً بداعية الشهوة والحاجة. لكنا بينا أن هذه الداعية في حق الله تعالى ممتنعة، فكان علمه تعالى بقبح الفعل صارفاً له عن هذا الفعل، ولم يوجد في حقه ما يعارضه، وهو داعية الشهوة، فوجب أن يمتنع الله من فعل القبيح، فهذا تقرير دليل المعتزلة
Thus it is established that knowledge of ugliness (qubh) necessitates turning away from the ugly act. Since this knowledge exists with respect to God Most High, it must effect His turning away from the ugly. The second aspect: that we abandon this analogy and claim necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) that knowledge that an act is ugly necessitates turning away from it, unless this deterrent (ṣārif) is opposed by an incitement of appetite (shahwa) and need (ḥāja). But we have shown that such incitement is impossible with respect to God Most High. Thus His knowledge of the act's ugliness is a deterrent for Him from this act, and there is nothing in His case to oppose it—namely incitement of appetite—so it is necessary that God refrains from the ugly act. This is the presentation of the Muʿtazilite proof.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the Muʿtazilite proof that God's knowledge of an act's ugliness (qubh) necessarily prevents Him from committing that act. The author presents two versions of the proof. The first version is based on rational inference (qiyās): it is established that knowledge of ugliness necessitates turning away from the ugly act. Since this knowledge exists in God, it must have its effect of turning Him away from evil. The second version avoids the analogical argument and claims that it is a matter of necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) that knowledge of ugliness causes one to turn away, unless there is a countervailing incitement (dāʿiya) of appetite or need. Since God cannot have such incitements, His knowledge of ugliness alone suffices to prevent Him from evil. The passage concludes by stating this as the formulation of the Muʿtazilite proof.

Key Terms
علم القبح
صارف
داعية الشهوة
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God has the power to do evil, implying impotence.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that God's knowledge makes evil impossible for Him, not that He lacks power. He is all-powerful but His knowledge prevents the act.

Checkpoint
What is the second version of the Mu'tazilite proof based on?
A) Rational inference (qiyās)
B) Necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) that knowledge of ugliness prevents the act
C) Scriptural texts
D) Consensus of the scholars

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof that God's knowledge of evil prevents Him from doing it rests on either rational analogy or necessary knowledge, both of which rule out any countervailing incitement in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of evil necessarily prevents Him from doing it, what does your own knowledge of your sins demand of you? Are you using that knowledge to turn away, or do you allow desire to override it? Make a concrete decision today to act on one truth you know but have ignored.
?
The Mu'tazila argue that God cannot do evil because He knows it is ugly. Do you live as though God's knowledge of your life makes Him trustworthy? Or do you secretly fear that He might act against you? Write down one area where you doubt God's goodness in your life, and ask Him to show you how His knowledge assures His perfect justice.
?
The second version of the proof claims that knowledge of ugliness is a necessary deterrent unless incited by appetite. What 'incitements of appetite' in your life have you allowed to override your knowledge of right and wrong? Confess one such pattern and commit to removing that incitement this week.

The Schools and the Chosen View on Rational Good and Evil
Author states schools' positions and the chosen view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the major theological schools on rational moral judgment and the Ash'ari chosen view that it applies to humans but not to Allah.

Core Idea
Rational moral judgment is valid for humans but not for Allah.

This lesson introduces the central debate on whether reason alone can determine good and evil. The author lists the affirming and denying schools, then clarifies the nuanced Ash'ari position that while humans can rationally perceive moral values, Allah's actions are not bound by them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the OCR marks like [?] but the meaning is clear from context.
2
Identify the two opposing camps
The passage first mentions two groups: the Mu'tazila and Karamiyya who affirm rational judgment, and the Philosophers and Jabriyya who deny it.
Memorize these schools for later recognition.
3
Study the chosen view
The author then presents the Ash'ari position: rational judgment is valid for human beings but not for Allah. Understand the distinction.
This is a key nuance that prevents misattributing moral obligations to Allah.
4
Ask: why does this matter?
Reflect on why the Asharis make this distinction. How does it relate to divine justice and human responsibility?
The commentary will help clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع
في
أن تحسين العقل وتقييم [؟] هل هو معقب [؟] أم لا؟؟
أطبقت المعتزلة والكرامية على إثبات تحسين العقل وتقبيحه . وأطبقت الفلاسفة والجبرية على إنكاره ، والمختار عندنا : أن تحسين العقل وتقبيحه بالنسبة إلى العباد معتبر ، وأما بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى فهو باطل
أما إثباته في حق العباد فيدل عليه وجوه :
.
الأول : إنا نرى أن العقلاء قبل علمهم بالشرائع والنبوات مطبقين على حسن مدح المحسن ، وحسن ذم المسيء . فإن من أحسن إلى محتاج ، فإن ذلك المحتاج يجد من صريح عقله ، حسن مدحه وذكره بالخير ، ولو أساء رجل إليه فإنه يجد من صريح عقله حسن ذمه . وهذا الحكم حاصل ، سواء كان ذلك الإنسان مؤمناً يصدق بالأنبياء أو لم يكن كذلك . فعلمنا أن هذا الحسن مقرر في عقولهم
Chapter Seven: On whether the rational judgment of good and evil is valid or not. The Mu'tazila and the Karamiyya concur on affirming the rational judgment of good and evil. The Philosophers and the Jabriyya concur on denying it. The chosen view with us is that the rational judgment of good and evil is valid with respect to servants, but with respect to Allah the Exalted, it is false. As for its affirmation with respect to servants, it is indicated by proofs: First: We observe that rational people, before their knowledge of religious laws and prophethoods, are in agreement on the goodness of praising the benefactor and the goodness of blaming the wrongdoer. For if someone does good to a needy person, that needy person finds in his clear reason the goodness of praising him and mentioning him with good, and if a man wrongs him, he finds in his clear reason the goodness of blaming him. And this judgment occurs whether that person is a believer who believes in the prophets or not. So we know that this goodness is firmly established in their intellects.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the chapter on the rational judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa-taqbīḥuh). The author first states the positions of the major schools: the Mu'tazila and Karamiyya affirm rational moral valuation, while the Philosophers and Jabriyya deny it. Then he presents the chosen view (al-mukhtār) of the Ash'ari school: rational judgment of good and evil is valid for human beings (al-ʿibād), but not for Allah, because Allah's actions are not subject to external moral norms. After stating this position, he begins to provide proofs for the affirmative part (for humans). He mentions that there are several proofs (wujūh), and then gives the first proof: the universal, pre-revelational agreement of rational people on two specific judgments: it is good to praise the benefactor and it is good to blame the wrongdoer. He supports this with a concrete example: if someone helps a needy person, that needy person naturally feels that praising the helper is good, and if someone harms him, he naturally feels that blaming the harmer is good. This judgment is independent of religious belief; it is found in everyone, whether they believe in prophets or not. Thus, the author concludes that this sense of goodness (ḥusn) is firmly established in their intellects (ʿuqūlihim), meaning it is a rational, not merely a revealed, judgment.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
معتزلة
كرامية
فلاسفة
جبرية
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris completely reject rational judgment of good and evil.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm it for humans but deny it for Allah, as stated in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two schools affirm rational judgment of good and evil?
A) Mu'tazila and Karamiyya
B) Philosophers and Jabriyya
C) Mu'tazila and Philosophers
D) Karamiyya and Jabriyya

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position distinguishes between human rational moral judgment (valid) and divine actions (not subject to rational norms).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy or injustice, do you ever find yourself questioning Allah's wisdom based on your own sense of right and wrong? How does this lesson's distinction between human and divine moral judgment challenge you to instead submit to His absolute wisdom?
?
Are there areas in your life where you secretly believe your moral reasoning is superior to what Allah has commanded? Take a moment to identify one command you struggle with—does your rational judgment of it as 'unfair' or 'outdated' reflect the very error this chapter exposes?
?
The Ash'ari view affirms rational judgment for humans but denies it for Allah. This means you are responsible to use your reason to discern good and evil, yet you must never judge Allah by that same reason. How comfortable are you with that tension? Will you truly live by it, or do you tend to hold Allah to your own standards while excusing your own moral failings?

The First Proof: Universal Human Agreement on Praise and Blame
Author presents the first proof for rational judgment in humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for rational moral judgment: the universal, pre-revelational human tendency to praise benefactors and blame wrongdoers.

Core Idea
Before any religion, all rational people naturally approve of praising the good and blaming the bad.

This lesson presents the first proof for rational moral judgment. The author argues that even before knowing any religious law, rational people universally agree on the goodness of praising the benefactor and blaming the wrongdoer, as shown by a concrete example of a needy person's natural reaction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Focus on the example.
This passage is a single proof with an example.
2
Identify the claim
The author claims that rational people, before revelation, universally agree that it is good to praise the benefactor and blame the wrongdoer.
These are two specific judgments.
3
Understand the example
The example of a needy person illustrates the natural, immediate reaction of praising someone who helps and blaming someone who harms, independent of religious belief.
The example is key to the proof.
4
Note the conclusion
The author concludes that this sense of goodness (ḥusn) is firmly established in their intellects, proving rational judgment exists.
Notice the phrase 'mutaqarrir fī ʿuqūlihim'.

After stating this position, he begins to provide proofs for the affirmative part (for humans). He mentions that there are several proofs (wujūh), and then gives the first proof: the universal, pre-revelational agreement of rational people on two specific judgments: it is good to praise the benefactor and it is good to blame the wrongdoer. He supports this with a concrete example: if someone helps a needy person, that needy person naturally feels that praising the helper is good, and if someone harms him, he naturally feels that blaming the harmer is good. This judgment is independent of religious belief; it is found in everyone, whether they believe in prophets or not. Thus, the author concludes that this sense of goodness (ḥusn) is firmly established in their intellects (ʿuqūlihim), meaning it is a rational, not merely a revealed, judgment.

النبوات

صريح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof relies on religious belief.
Correction: The author explicitly states the judgment occurs whether the person believes in prophets or not.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to illustrate the natural moral judgment?
A) A king judging his subjects
B) A needy person praising a benefactor and blaming a wrongdoer
C) A prophet delivering a revelation
D) A philosopher debating ethics

Key Takeaway
The universal human reaction of praising benefactors and blaming wrongdoers, even without revelation, shows that moral judgments are rooted in reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific time you praised someone for helping you. Did you feel that response came from your own reason, or from social training? If from reason, what does that tell you about your Creator's design for knowing good?
?
When you see injustice, your heart rises in blame. If you deny that this judgment is rooted in your own clear reason, what do you base your moral outrage on? How does that affect your accountability before God?
?
You have learned that even without revelation, reason knows the goodness of praising good and blaming evil. On what basis do you excuse your own sins? Do you rely on God's mercy while ignoring the voice of your own reason that condemns your wrong? What one step will you take today to listen to that voice?

Rational Good and Bad: Positions and Chosen View
Author states positions on rational good/bad and the chosen view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on rational good and bad, and why the Ash'ari view distinguishes between human and divine applicability.

Core Idea
Reason judges good and bad for humans, but not for Allah.

The question of whether human reason can independently determine moral value—good and bad—has been a major dispute in Islamic theology. This passage presents the two opposing camps and then states the chosen Ash'ari position, which makes a crucial distinction between the human and divine realms. Let's examine the text to see how the author sets up this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the clear structure: title, two opposing positions, chosen view.
2
Identify the groups and their positions
Who affirms rational good and bad? Who denies it? Find the four groups mentioned and which side each takes.
Mu'tazila and Karramiyya affirm; philosophers and Jabriyya deny.
3
Understand the Ash'ari chosen view
The author says 'the chosen view with us'—this is the Ash'ari position. Notice how it splits the issue: good and bad are valid for human beings but not for Allah.
This distinction is key to the Ash'ari reconciliation between reason and revelation.
4
Connect to the misconception
Some think Ash'aris totally reject rational good and bad. The passage shows they only reject it for Allah. Think about why this matters for theology.
This sets up the need to prove both parts of the position.

Source Text

The philosophers and Jabriyya have agreed on denying it. The chosen view with us: that reason's approval and disapproval with respect to servants are considered, but with respect to Allah Almighty it is invalid.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the doctrinal positions and the author's chosen view. The author first reports that the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya affirm rational good and bad, while the philosophers and Jabriyya deny them. Then he states the position of his own school (the Ash'aris): rational judgments of good and bad are valid for human beings ('ibad), but they do not apply to Allah. This sets up the need to prove both parts of this position.

The Universal Rational Consensus on Praising Good and Blaming Evil
Author presents first proof: universal agreement of rational people on praising good and blaming evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof in the Matalib uses universal rational agreement to establish that moral judgments (praising good, blaming evil) are known by reason alone, prior to revelation.

Core Idea
All rational people, even without religion, agree: good-doers deserve praise, evil-doers deserve blame.

The author begins by pointing to a simple but profound observation: everywhere and always, rational human beings—whether believers or not—feel that someone who does good should be thanked and praised, and someone who does harm should be blamed. This universal agreement, he argues, shows that moral judgments are not learned from prophets or scriptures but are rooted in our very reason. In this lesson, we will examine this proof step by step and see how it grounds the case for prophetic missions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to hear the author's tone, then read the literal translation. Notice that the author uses a concrete example to make his point vivid.
The example of the needy person is key—it shows the judgment is spontaneous and universal.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into its parts: (a) premise of universal agreement, (b) illustration with the needy person, (c) conclusion that this goodness is ingrained in reason.
Observe that the author tests the judgment across believers and non-believers to prove it is independent of revelation.
3
Reflect on the objection anticipated
The author hints that this judgment is not merely social convention—it is found in all rational people, even those without faith. Ask yourself: could there be a society where people do not feel this way?
This anticipation strengthens the argument for rational objectivity.
4
Take the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your grasp of the main claim. Only the passage's explicit statement will count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا نرى أن العقلاء قبل علمهم بالشرائع والنبوات مطبقين على حسن مدح المحسن ، وحسن ذم المسيء . فإن من أحسن إلى محتاج ، فإن ذلك المحتاج يجد من صريح عقله ، حسن مدحه وذكره بالخير ، ولو أساء رجل إليه فإنه يجد من صريح عقله حسن ذمه . وهذا الحكم حاصل ، سواء كان ذلك الإنسان مؤمناً يصدق بالأنبياء أو لم يكن كذلك . فعلمنا أن هذا الحسن مقرر في عقولهم
First: We see that rational people, before their knowledge of revealed laws and prophecies, are in agreement upon the goodness of praising the good-doer and the goodness of blaming the evil-doer. For if someone does good to a needy person, that needy person finds by his clear reason the goodness of praising him and mentioning him with good; and if a man does evil to him, he finds by his clear reason the goodness of blaming him. And this judgment obtains whether that person is a believer who affirms the prophets or not. So we know that this goodness is established in their minds.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is the first proof for rational good and bad regarding human actions. The author points to a universal empirical observation: rational people, even without knowledge of divine revelation, consistently agree that it is good to praise someone who does good and good to blame someone who does evil. He illustrates with a concrete example: a needy person spontaneously feels by pure reason that his benefactor deserves praise and his malefactor deserves blame. This judgment is independent of religious belief, as it is found in believers and non-believers alike. Thus, the author concludes, this sense of goodness is ingrained in human reason itself.

Correction: The author argues that the agreement is found even in non-believers and before revelation, indicating it is a rational, innate judgment.

Key Takeaway
Rational people universally and spontaneously judge that good-doers deserve praise and evil-doers deserve blame, proving that basic moral judgments are known by reason alone, prior to revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If moral judgments are universally known by reason, then on the Day of Judgment, you cannot claim ignorance of basic good and evil. What does this lesson reveal about your accountability for the moral choices you make every day?

The Infinite Regress Argument for Rational Judgment
Sanusi argues that rational judgment must precede legal command to avoid infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of infinite regress proves that reason must judge it bad to expose oneself to punishment, establishing rational bad.

Core Idea
Rational judgment must precede legal command, or else we get an infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a logical argument that rational bad is prior to legal bad. If reason does not already deem it bad to incur punishment, revelation would have to command avoidance, leading to an infinite chain. Thus, reason must already have a judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of legal bad
Read the author's definition of legal bad: it is simply a statement by the law that an act leads to punishment.
2
Consider the role of reason
Ask: does reason judge that avoiding punishment is obligatory? If yes, rational good/bad exist. If no, we face infinite regress.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If reason does not judge, then the law would need to command avoidance, but that command itself requires a prior rational judgment, and so on.
This is impossible.
4
Conclude the proof
Therefore, reason must already judge that avoiding punishment is obligatory, proving rational bad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنه لا معنى للقبح الشرعي إلا أن الشرع يقول له : إنك إن فعلت الفعل الفلاني صرت معاقباً عليه ، فيقول عقله : هل نقضي بوجوب الاحتراز عن العقاب أو لا نقضي بذلك ؟ فإن قضي بذلك ، فالحسن والقبح العقليان قد ثبتا ، وإن لم يقض عقله بذلك فحينئذ يحتاج إلى أن يوجب الشرع عليه الاحتراز عن العقاب . والكلام فيه كما في الأول ، فيلزم التسلسل ، وهو محال .
والثالث : إنه لا شك أن عندنا مطلوباً أو مكروهاً . ولا يجوز أن نقول : إن كل مطلوب أو مكروه إنما كان مطلوباً أو مكروهاً لأجل شيء آخر لزم التسلسل أو الدور ، فلا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء يكون مطلوباً لذاته ، وبوجود شيء يكون مكروهاً لذاته . ثم لما تأملنا علمنا : أن اللذة والسرور مطلوبتان بالذات . وأن الألم والغم مكروهان بالذات . فهذا الحكم ثابت في محض العقول ، سواء حصلت الشريعة أو لم تحصل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العقل يقضي بحسن بعض الأشياء وبقبح بعضها . فهذه الوجوه دالة على أن الحسن والقبح بمقتضى العقل في حق العباد معتبر
Second: There is no meaning to 'legally bad' except that the law says to him: 'If you do such-and-such act, you become punishable for it.' Then his reason says: 'Do we rule that it is obligatory to avoid punishment or do we not rule that?' If it rules that, then rational good and bad have been established. And if his reason does not rule that, then the law would need to make avoiding punishment obligatory for him. And the discussion is the same as the first, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible.
Third: There is no doubt that we have something desired or something hated. And it is not permissible to say that every desired or hated thing is desired or hated only because of something else, for that would lead to infinite regress or circularity. So one must acknowledge the existence of something that is desired for itself, and something that is hated for itself. Then when we reflect, we know that pleasure and delight are desired for themselves, and pain and sorrow are hated for themselves. This judgment is established in pure reason, whether the law exists or not. So it is established by what we have mentioned that reason judges some things as good and some as bad. So these proofs indicate that good and bad by reason are considered with respect to the servants.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage contains two proofs for rational good and bad in human actions. The second proof is a logical argument: The meaning of 'legally bad' is that revelation declares an act punishable. If reason does not already deem it bad to expose oneself to punishment, then revelation would need to command avoiding punishment. But that command itself would require reason to deem avoidance obligatory, otherwise infinite regress. Therefore, reason must already judge that it is bad to expose oneself to punishment, establishing rational bad. The third proof observes that we naturally desire some things and hate others. If every desire/hate were for the sake of something else, we would have infinite regress or circularity. Thus, there must be things desired/hated for themselves. Reflection shows that pleasure and delight are desired for themselves, and pain and sorrow hated for themselves. This judgment is purely rational, independent of revelation. Hence, reason does judge some things good and some bad. The author concludes that these proofs establish rational good and bad for humans.

Key Terms
القبح الشرعي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational good and bad are derived solely from revelation.
Correction: This proof shows rational judgment is prior and necessary for revelation to be meaningful.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof argue would happen if reason does not judge it bad to avoid punishment?
A) Revelation would become unnecessary
B) There would be infinite regress
C) Humans would never sin
D) Reason would be perfect

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress proves that reason must already judge avoiding punishment as obligatory, establishing rational bad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you actively use your reason to judge what is good and bad before turning to scripture? Do you trust that Allah has given you a rational capacity to discern right from wrong? What would it mean if you never used reason to judge—would you be trapped in an infinite regress of commands? How does this argument challenge your reliance on habit or culture instead of reasoned reflection?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that if reason does not judge, we would need an endless chain of commands. Think of a time when you needed to make a moral decision and you kept seeking external guidance without trusting your own rational assessment. Did that lead to confusion or paralysis? How can you now, after understanding this argument, trust that your reason is capable of judging good and bad, as a gift from Allah, and act accordingly?

The Argument from Inherent Desires for Rational Good and Bad
Sanusi argues that inherent desires for pleasure and aversion to pain prove rational good and bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why there must be things desired and hated for themselves, leading to rational judgments of good and bad independent of revelation.

Core Idea
Pleasure is desired for itself; pain is hated for itself – a rational judgment before any law.

In this passage, Sanusi uses our natural inclinations toward pleasure and away from pain to prove that reason makes judgments of good and bad. Unless some things are desired or hated for themselves, we would have infinite regress or circularity. Reflection shows pleasure and pain are such things.

Guided Path
1
Observe natural desires and aversions
We all have things we desire and things we hate. This is indubitable.
2
Consider the source of desire
If every desire were for the sake of something else, we would have infinite regress or circularity. So some things must be desired for themselves.
Similarly for hatred.
3
Identify the ultimate objects
Through reflection, we discover that pleasure and delight are desired for themselves, and pain and sorrow are hated for themselves.
This is self-evident.
4
Conclude rational judgment
Since this judgment is purely rational and exists whether or not revelation is present, reason judges some things good (pleasure) and some bad (pain).

Source Text

?

Are you treating them as having that innate moral sense?

Why Rational Good and Bad Do Not Apply to Allah
Author argues rational good/bad cannot apply to Allah and introduces Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that rational concepts of good and bad are based on benefit and harm, which do not apply to Allah, and you will see the initial Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond human categories of benefit and harm.

The author tackles a central theological debate: can we describe Allah's actions as 'good' or 'bad' in a rational sense? He argues that these categories are tied to benefit and harm, which are inapplicable to the Transcendent Creator. The Mu'tazila object, pointing out that good and bad are not identical to benefit and harm. In this lesson, we examine the author's proof and the beginning of their counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the definition of good and bad and the conclusion drawn.
The passage ends mid-sentence with the Mu'tazila objection.
2
Understand the author's definition
Identify how the author defines 'good' (benefit) and 'bad' (harm). Note that this is a rational definition, not a revelatory one.
3
Follow the deduction
The author argues that because benefit and harm only apply to beings that can be affected, and Allah is such that He cannot be affected, rational good/bad do not apply to Him.
This is based on the impossibility of benefit/harm for Allah.
4
Consider the Mu'tazila objection
Read the beginning of the objection: good/bad are distinct from benefit/harm. The author will likely respond later.
This objection challenges the author's definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثباتهما في حق الله تعالى ، فنقول : هذا محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن الذي عقلناه من معنى الحسن ما يكون نفعاً ، أو مؤدياً إليه ، والذي عقلناه من معنى القبح ، ما يكون ضرراً أو مؤدياً إليه . والرغبة في المنفعة ، والرهبة عن المضرة إنما يعقل حصولهما في حق من يصح عليه النفع والضرر . ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى محالاً ، كان القول بثبوت الحسن والقبح في حق الله محالاً .
قالت المعتزلة : كون الشيء حسناً أو قبيحاً . أمر مغاير لكونه منشئاً للمنفعة والمضرة . والدليل عليه : أن النافع قد يكون قبيحاً ، والضار قد يكون حسناً . أما أن النافع قد يكون قبيحاً ، فلأن الظلم نافع في حق الظالم مع أنه
As for establishing them with respect to Allah Almighty, we say: that is impossible. And proofs indicate it: The first proof: What we have understood of the meaning of 'good' is that which is benefit or leads to benefit, and what we have understood of the meaning of 'bad' is that which is harm or leads to harm. And desire for benefit and fear of harm are only rationally conceivable with respect to one to whom benefit and harm apply. And since that is impossible with respect to Allah Almighty, saying that good and bad are established with respect to Allah is impossible. The Mu'tazila said: The fact that a thing is good or bad is something other than it being productive of benefit or harm. The proof of this is that beneficial may be bad, and harmful may be good. For example, oppression is beneficial to the oppressor and yet it is bad.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an argument against applying rational good and bad to Allah, followed by a Mu'tazila objection. The author first defines good as benefit or what leads to benefit, and bad as harm or what leads to harm. Benefit and harm are only relevant to beings capable of benefiting or being harmed. Since Allah is free from such contingencies, good and bad cannot be attributed to Him. The Mu'tazila then object: good/bad are distinct from benefit/harm; a beneficial action (like oppression for the oppressor) can be bad, and a harmful action (like a painful medical treatment) can be good. Therefore, the definitions are different. The argument cuts off at the end of the page, but the objection challenges the author's definition. This sets up a refutation likely continued elsewhere.

الضرر
الظلم
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That because Allah is all-powerful, He can do what is 'bad' according to human reason, making Him unjust.
Correction: The author argues that 'bad' as a rational category does not apply to Allah at all. His actions are beyond such human concepts. The Ash'ari position is that good and bad are defined by divine command, not by human reason.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of 'good'?
A) What is commanded by revelation
B) What is benefit or leads to benefit
C) What is approved by society
D) What is beautiful in nature

Key Takeaway
Rational categories of good and bad, defined by benefit and harm, cannot be applied to Allah, who is beyond such contingent relationships.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or do good deeds, do you ever catch yourself doing it for the benefit it brings you—peace of mind, reward, protection? This lesson shows that Allah is beyond benefit and harm. This week, try to perform one act of worship purely because He deserves it, not because it benefits you. How does it feel?
?
Have you ever judged an event in your life as 'bad' because it harmed you? This lesson says rational 'bad' is defined by harm. But Allah is not subject to that. Can you trust that what seems harmful to you may be good in Allah's plan? Identify one recent difficulty and consciously say: 'My definition of bad does not apply to this, because it comes from Allah who is above my categories.'
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that oppression is good for the oppressor but still bad. Do you ever excuse your own sins because they bring you some benefit? Repent from that logic and commit to judging actions solely by Allah's command, not by your own calculation of benefit.

The Third Argument for Rational Good and Evil
Third proof that reason judges good and evil based on intrinsic desire and aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that reason alone can judge good and evil, based on intrinsic desire for pleasure and aversion to pain.

Core Idea
Pleasure is desired for its own sake; pain is disliked for its own sake—this is known by pure reason.

This passage presents a powerful argument that reason can recognize good and evil without relying on revelation. It starts with our undeniable experiences of desire and aversion, then uses a logical regress proof to show that some things must be desired or disliked intrinsically. By reflecting, we identify pleasure and pain as such intrinsic values, establishing that rational judgments of good and evil are valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from experience to regress argument to conclusion.
The passage begins mid-sentence with 'والثالث' (The third).
2
Understand the regress argument
Identify why infinite regress or circularity is impossible for desires and aversions. This forces the existence of something intrinsically desired/disliked.
3
Identify the intrinsic values
Find where the passage states that pleasure and joy are desired in themselves, and pain and grief are disliked in themselves.
These are given as examples, but the argument establishes the category.
4
Connect to rational good and evil
See how the conclusion follows: reason judges some things good (pleasure) and others evil (pain) independently of divine law.
This refutes the idea that good and evil depend solely on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنه لا شك أن عندنا مطلوباً أو مكروهاً . ولا يجوز أن نقول : إن كل مطلوب أو مكروه إنما كان مطلوباً أو مكروهاً لأجل شيء آخر لزم التسلسل أو الدور ، فلا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء يكون مطلوباً لذاته ، وبوجود شيء يكون مكروهاً لذاته . ثم لما تأملنا علمنا : أن اللذة والسرور مطلوبتان بالذات . وأن الألم والغم مكروهان بالذات . فهذا الحكم ثابت في محض العقول ، سواء حصلت الشريعة أو لم تحصل فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العقل يقضي بحسن بعض الأشياء وبقبح بعضها . فهذه الوجوه دالة على أن الحسن والقبح بمقتضى العقل في حق العباد معتبر
The third: There is no doubt that we have something desired or something disliked. And it is not permissible for us to say: Every desired or disliked thing is only desired or disliked because of something else, for then there would be infinite regress or circularity. So one must acknowledge the existence of something that is desired for its own sake, and the existence of something that is disliked for its own sake. Then when we reflected, we knew: that pleasure and joy are desired in themselves, and that pain and grief are disliked in themselves. So this judgment is established in pure intellects, whether the Law comes or not. So it is established by what we have mentioned that reason judges the goodness of some things and the evil of some things. So these arguments indicate that good and evil based on reason are considered in relation to servants.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the validity of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyān) in human actions. It begins by stating an undeniable fact: we experience things that are desired (maṭlūb) and things that are disliked (makrūh). The argument then uses a regress proof: if every desired thing were desired only for the sake of something else, an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) would result. Therefore, there must exist something that is desired for its own sake (maṭlūb li-dhātihi) and something disliked for its own sake. Through reflection, we discover that pleasure (al-ladhdha) and joy (al-surūr) are intrinsically desired, while pain (al-alam) and grief (al-ghamm) are intrinsically disliked. This judgment is purely intellectual—it holds whether or not divine law (sharī'a) is present. Thus reason itself pronounces some things good and others evil, establishing that rational good and evil are valid criteria for human actions ('ibād).

Key Terms

المكروه لذاته
الحسن والقبح العقليان
اللذة
الألم
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that good and evil are determined solely by revelation and reason has no role.
Correction: This argument shows that reason alone can recognize intrinsic good (pleasure) and evil (pain) independently of divine law.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why must there be something desired for its own sake?
A) Because all desires are based on social convention
B) Because otherwise an infinite regress or circularity would result
C) Because revelation commands it
D) Because desire is always for pleasure

Key Takeaway
The third argument establishes that reason alone can judge good and evil by identifying pleasure and pain as intrinsically desired and disliked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If pleasure and pain are intrinsically desired and disliked by your very nature, what does that reveal about your deepest motivations? Are you pursuing pleasure and avoiding pain only for themselves, or have you allowed your desires to become disconnected from what is truly good in the sight of Allah? This lesson forces you to ask: Is your life oriented toward the ultimate intrinsic good, or are you caught in an infinite regress of temporary satisfactions? Commit today to reflect on your daily choices and ask which of them are valued for Allah's sake alone.
?
The argument shows that reason alone, without revelation, can recognize some things as good and evil. Do you rely on your intellect to discern right from wrong, or do you only follow divine commands out of habit? If your reason tells you that injustice is evil and compassion is good, how does that affect your obedience to Allah? Is your faith merely submission, or is it also an intellectual acknowledgment of the intrinsic goodness of His commands? Challenge yourself: identify one moral truth you hold that you could justify to a non-Muslim using reason alone.
?
The infinite regress argument suggests that if every desire were for something else, you would have no ultimate purpose. What is the one thing in your life that you desire for its own sake, not as a means to something else? If you cannot name it, you might be living without an ultimate goal. Let this question pierce your heart: Are you living for Allah alone as the intrinsic end, or are your desires scattered among countless temporary aims? Resolve to make your ultimate desire (maṭlūb li-dhātihi) the pleasure of Allah, which is the only intrinsic good that brings lasting peace.

The Proof That Rational Good and Evil Are Invalid for God
Author presents a proof that rational moral categories do not apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that rational judgments of good and evil cannot be applied to God, and how the unanimity of religious communities supports this.

Core Idea
If rational ugliness applied to God, He would be a futile actor, contradicting all faiths.

In this passage, the author presents a concise proof against applying rational moral categories to God. He uses a simple scenario to show the absurd consequences, then appeals to the consensus of believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional argument and the appeal to consensus.
2
Understand the scenario
The author gives an example of someone taking a dinar and giving it back. Why would that be futile? Relate this to the concept of favor (ni'mah).
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow how the author moves from the hypothetical to the actual: if rational ugliness were valid, God would be futile, but all religions affirm He is wise and beneficent.
This refutes the rationalist position.
