Objection: Worship's Benefits Accrue to the Worshiper
Objection with three arguments that worship benefits the worshiper.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and content of a key objection against the claim that rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God's actions.

Core Idea
If worship benefits humans, then God's actions could be judged by human reason.

The author has argued that God's actions are not subject to human notions of good and evil. But an opponent raises a powerful objection: perhaps the obligations are good precisely because they benefit the worshiper. This lesson presents that objection in full, along with three supporting arguments drawn from reason, theology, and philosophical psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: a central claim followed by three distinct arguments.
The passage begins with 'Then it is said' (فإن قيل), signaling a counterargument.
2
Identify the three arguments
Locate the three arguments marked by 'First', 'Second', and 'Third'. Each attempts to show that obligations are good because they benefit humans.
The first argument uses rational good/evil; the second uses comparative delight; the third uses a psychological model.
3
Examine the psychological argument
The third argument from the philosophers (حكماء) explains how worship purifies the soul by reducing attachment to physical pleasures and increasing spiritual inclinations. This makes the transition to the afterlife pleasant.
This argument is more elaborate than the first two.
4
Recognize this as a counterargument
Remember that this entire passage is not the author's own view but a strong objection he will later refute. Consider why it might be convincing.
The author's refutation will likely challenge the human judgment of good/evil in God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما حسن تلك التكاليف لمنفعة عائلة إلى الشاكر والمطيع ؟ وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن تعظيم من لا يستحق التعظيم قبيح في العقول . فهو سبحانه إنما كلفهم حتى يصيروا بسبب إتيانهم بتلك الطاعات مستحقين للتعظيم ، فحينئذ يحسن منه تعالى أن يخصهم بأنواع التعظيم . الثاني : إنه تعالى لو أعطاهم تلك المنافع بعد أن كانوا مستحقين لها ، كان ذلك ألذ وأكمل مما إذا حصلت تلك المنافع بمحض التفضل . الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن إمكان حصول هذه السعادات الأخروية للبشر ، مشروط بإتيانهم بهذه العبادات، على ما هو مذهب الحكماء . وذلك لأنهم قالوا : الإنسان إذا واظب على تحصيل هذه الشهوات البدنية واللذات الجسمانية فإنه تقوى رغبته فيها ، ويعظم ميله إلى تحصيلها ، فعند الموت يبقى ذلك الميل والرغبة مع العجز عن الوجدان ، فتعظم الآلام الروحانية . أما إذا امتنع عن تحصيل هذه اللذات، وأقبل على ذكر الله ، وذكر الملائكة وذكر الدار الآخرة فإنه تفتر رغبته في الجسمانيات ، ويقوى ميله إلى الروحانيات ، فعند الموت يصير كأنه يتخلص من المكروه إلى المحبوب. ومن الحبس إلى البستان ، وهذا هو السبب في التكاليف بالطاعات والعبادات .
Then it is said: Why may it not be said that those obligations are good only because of a benefit accruing to the grateful and obedient? Its explanation is from aspects: First: To honor the one who does not deserve honor is rationally evil. He (exalted) only obligated them so that by performing those acts of obedience they become deserving of honor, and then it is good of Him (exalted) to bestow upon them various kinds of honor. Second: If He (exalted) gave them those benefits after they were deserving of them, that would be more delightful and complete than if those benefits were obtained by mere favor. Third: Why may it not be said that the possibility of obtaining these otherworldly felicities for humans is conditioned by their performing these acts of worship, according to the doctrine of the philosophers? For they said: If a person persists in pursuing these bodily desires and physical pleasures, his desire for them increases and his inclination to acquire them becomes great. At death, that inclination and desire remain with inability to attain them, so the spiritual pains become severe. But if he abstains from pursuing these pleasures and turns to the remembrance of God, the remembrance of angels, and the remembrance of the Hereafter, his desire for physical things diminishes and his inclination towards spiritual things becomes strong. At death, it is as if he escapes from the disliked to the beloved, and from prison to the garden. This is the reason for obligations of acts of obedience and worship.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (the author's opponent's viewpoint) to the previous conclusion that rational judgment of good and evil does not apply to God's actions. The objection proposes that divine obligations are good because they benefit the worshiper, not God. The objection is supported by three arguments. The first argument: Honoring someone who does not deserve it is rationally evil. God's obligations make humans deserving of His honor through their obedience, so it becomes good for God to honor them. The second argument: Giving benefits after the recipient deserves them is more delightful and complete than unconditional giving. The third argument: The philosophers' view that otherworldly felicities are conditional on performing worship, because worship purifies the soul and reduces attachment to physical pleasures, making the transition to the afterlife pleasant. This entire passage is the objection, which the author will subsequently answer.

Key Terms
تحسين وتقبيح
تعظيم
تكليف
حكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is the author's own position.
Correction: This is a counterargument presented by an opponent; the author will refute it later.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument of the objection, why is it good for God to honor humans?
A) Because God always benefits from honor
B) Because humans become deserving of honor through obedience
C) Because honor is intrinsically good
D) Because God does not need honor

Key Takeaway
This objection challenges the author's thesis by grounding divine obligations in human benefit, using rational, theological, and psychological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever catch yourself thinking that you are earning God's favor or making Him owe you something? How does this objection challenge that attitude, and what would it mean for your worship if you truly believed no human can ever place God in debt?
?
The third argument suggests that worship is primarily a psychological tool to ease our transition to death—a clever coping mechanism. Is that the only reason you worship? If you stripped away all fear of death and all hope of reward, would you still worship Allah? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your heart?
?
Consider the first argument: it assumes that honoring an undeserving person is evil, so God must make us deserving before honoring us. Do you ever feel entitled to Allah's mercy or reward because of your deeds? How would your relationship with Allah change if you internalized that nothing you do can make you deserving, and that all honor from Him is pure favor?

Three Rebuttals Against Mu'tazilite Hardship Argument
Three rebuttals against the idea that worship makes humans deserving of honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rebuttals Sanusi uses to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that hardship is necessary for obligations to cause deserving of honor.

Core Idea
Hardship is not required for divine honor; God can grant honor directly.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles the cornerstone argument of the Mu'tazila that obligations must involve hardship to make humans deserving of honor. He declares it weak and offers three distinct rebuttals drawing from prophetic rewards, the nature of delight, and the risk of punishment. These rebuttals reinforce the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not bound by rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice that the passage begins by stating that the Mu'tazila rely on this argument.
2
Identify the three rebuttals
Look for the markers 'الوجه الأول', 'الوجه الثاني', and 'الوجه الثالث'. Each introduces a separate line of argument against the Mu'tazilite position.
These three aspects form the core of Sanusi's response.
3
Examine the evidence in each rebuttal
For each rebuttal, identify the key evidence: prophetic rewards, direct creation of delight, and the risk of punishment.
The first uses analogy with prophets; the second negates the need for intermediary; the third assesses cost-benefit.
4
Consider how each rebuttal undermines the Mu'tazilite claim
Reflect on how each point challenges the necessity of hardship. For example, if honor can be given without hardship, the whole basis of the argument collapses.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Understand that the conclusion (though cut off) supports the Ash'ari view that rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God's actions.
This lesson connects to broader discussions of divine transcendence.

Source Text

فالجواب : أما جواب الوجه الأول : وهو الذي عليه تعويل المعتزلة . وهو ضعيف . فبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إنه ليس من شرط كون الفعل موجباً لاستحقاق التعظيم كونه شاقاً بدليل : إن ثواب الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أعظم من ثواب آحاد الأمة . وإن كانوا قد تحملوا من العناء فوق ما تحمله الرسول المعصوم . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن كان المقصود هو ما ذكرتم من استحقاق التعظيم فكان من حقه تعالى أن يكلفهم بالتكاليف السهلة الهيئة. وأن يزيد في تقوية أبدانهم ، حتى أنهم إذا أتوا بها سهل ذلك عليهم ، وحصلت لهم المنفعة التي ذكرتموها وهي استحقاق التعظيم . الوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن المقصود من التعظيم حصول السرور في القلب ولا نوع من أنواع السرور إلا ويحسن من الله أن يخلقه في قلب العبد بدون واسطة هذه التكاليف . الوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا الكلام : إن عند حصول التكليف يحصل احتمالان فإنه إن أطاع حصل له استحقاق التعظيم ، وإن لم يطع وقع في العذاب العظيم والعناء الشديد، فهذا التكليف دائر بين هذين الاحتمالين . ومن المعلوم أن حصول التعظيم ليس من الأمور الضرورية فإن الرجل إذا حصلت له المنافع الحالية عن التعظيم ، بقي سليماً عن الآفات ، منتفعـاً بالخيرات . فحصول التعظيم من المطالب الزائدة . أما إذا لم يأت به وقع في العذاب العظيم. ومعلوم أن السعي في دفع العذاب العظيم أولى من السعي في تحصيل منفعة زائدة ، ولا حاجة إلى حصولهما في شيء من الأوقات . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح في أفعال الله وأحكامه
The answer: As for the answer to the first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal): It is that upon which the Mu'tazila rely, and it is weak. Its explanation is from aspects: First: It is not a condition for an act to cause deserving of honor that it be hardship, evidenced by the fact that the reward of the prophets (peace be upon them) is greater than the reward of ordinary individuals of the community, even though they have endured suffering beyond that which the protected messenger endured. If this is established, we say: If the intention was what you mentioned of deserving honor, then it would have been incumbent upon Him (exalted) to obligate them with easy, gentle obligations, and to increase the strength of their bodies so that when they performed them, it would be easy for them, and they would obtain the benefit you mentioned, which is deserving of honor. Second aspect in the answer: The purpose of honor is the occurrence of delight in the heart, and there is no kind of delight except that it is good for God to create it in the heart of the servant without the intermediary of these obligations. Third aspect in refuting this statement: When obligation occurs, two possibilities arise: if he obeys, he obtains deserving of honor; if he disobeys, he falls into great punishment and severe hardship. So this obligation is subject to these two possibilities. And it is known that obtaining honor is not among necessary matters. If a person obtains benefits while being free from honor, he remains safe from afflictions and benefits from good things. So obtaining honor is an additional objective. But if he does not perform it, he falls into great punishment. And it is known that striving to repel great punishment is more important than striving to obtain an additional benefit, and there is no need to obtain both at any time. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that if rational judgment of good and evil were considered in God's actions and judgments...
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the first of the three arguments presented in the objection. The author declares that this argument, relied upon by the Mu'tazila, is weak. He then provides three rebuttals. The first rebuttal argues that hardship is not a necessary condition for deserving honor, citing the example of prophets who suffer more but receive greater reward, so one could have been given easier obligations to achieve the same end. The second rebuttal states that the purpose of honor is delight in the heart, and God can create that delight directly without the mediation of difficult obligations. The third rebuttal points out that obligations entail risk of punishment; avoiding punishment is more important than gaining extra honor, so the supposed benefit does not justify the risk. The author concludes, though the text cuts off, that this disproves the claim that rational judgments of good and evil apply to God's actions.

Key Terms
تعظيم
تكليف
استحقاق
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the response rejects the notion of honor entirely, or that it denies any value to human effort.
Correction: The response only rejects the necessity of hardship for deserving honor. It does not deny that honor can be given through obligations, but argues that God can also grant it directly without hardship.

Checkpoint
What does the third aspect of the response argue about the nature of obligation?
A) That obligation always results in honor
B) That obligation involves risk of punishment outweighing potential honor
C) That honor is a necessary benefit
D) That God cannot create delight directly

Key Takeaway
Sanusi provides three independent rebuttals—hardship is not necessary, honor can be given directly, and the risk of punishment outweighs the potential benefit—to refute the Mu'tazilite argument that obligations must involve hardship to be meaningful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel that your worship is only valuable when it is difficult. After reading Sanusi's first rebuttal, what does this reveal about your assumption about God's generosity? Will you today thank Him for an easy act of worship, and how?
?
The third rebuttal shows that seeking honor from God can be a risky gamble if it leads to disobedience. Are there areas in your life where you prioritize gaining honor from people over avoiding what displeases Allah? What is one step you will take immediately to realign your priorities?
?
Sanusi argues that God can create delight directly in the heart without any act of worship. Does your relationship with worship rely on feeling a sense of achievement? How would your worship change if you believed God could give you delight even when you feel your efforts are minimal? What would you ask Him for tonight?

First Argument: Burden Not Necessary for Veneration
First argument refuting Mu'tazilite view on veneration through hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first refutation: that burden is not a necessary condition for veneration, as shown by the greater reward of prophets despite greater hardship.

Core Idea
The prophets' greater reward proves that veneration does not require hardship.

The Mu'tazilites argued that divine commands are designed to make humans deserve veneration through hardship. This lesson presents the first counterargument: the example of the prophets, who endured more yet received greater reward, shows that hardship is not essential for veneration. In fact, if hardship were the goal, God could have imposed easy commands and strengthened bodies instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author begins by declaring the Mu'tazilite position weak, then presents the first argument.
The argument starts with 'الوجه الأول'.
2
Understand the evidence from the prophets
The author cites the prophets' reward as proof that burden is not required for veneration. Why does this disprove the Mu'tazilite claim?
Prophets endured greater hardship, so if hardship caused merit, they would have even more reward—but the point is that hardship is not the cause.
3
Reflect on the hypothetical scenario
If the goal were veneration, God could have made obligations easy. This shows that veneration is not dependent on difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب (۱) :
أما جواب الوجه الأول : وهو الذي عليه تعويل المعتزلة . وهو ضعيف . فبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : إنه ليس من شرط كون الفعل موجباً لاستحقاق التعظيم ( كـونه شاقاً ) (۲) بدليل : إن ثواب الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أعظم من ثواب آحاد الأمة . وإن كانوا قد تحملوا من العناء فوق ما تحمله الرسول المعصوم . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن كان المقصود هو ما ذكرتم من استحقاق التعظيم فكان من حقه تعالى أن يكلفهم بالتكاليف السهلة الهيئة. وأن يزيد في تقوية أبدانهم ، حتى أنهم إذا أتوا بها سهل ذلك عليهم ، وحصلت لهم المنفعة التي ذكرتموها وهي

الوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا الكلام : إن عند حصول التكليف يحصل احتمالان فإنه إن أطاع حصل له استحقاق التعظيم ، وإن لم يطع وقع في العذاب العظيم والعناء الشديد، فهذا التكليف دائر بين هذين الاحتمالين . ومن المعلوم أن حصول التعظيم ليس من الأمور الضرورية فإن الرجل إذا حصلت له المنافع الحالية عن التعظيم ، بقي سليماً عن الآفات ، منتفعـاً بالخيرات . فحصول التعظيم من المطالب الزائدة . أما إذا لم يأت به وقع في العذاب العظيم. ومعلوم أن السعي في دفع العذاب العظيم أولى من السعي في تحصيل منفعة زائدة ، ولا حاجة إلى حصولهما في شيء من الأوقات . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح في أفعال (۳) الله وأحكامه
معتبراً ، لكان الواجب اللازم ترك التكليف
•
الوجه الرابع في إبطال هذا الكلام : إن ما ذكرتموه إنما يستقيم إذا كان
العبد مستقلاً بالفعل والترك أما لما لم يثبت أن عند حصول القدرة والداعي يجب الفعل ، فإنه لا يبقى لهذا الكلام فائدة ..
The answer (1): As for the answer to the first aspect: it is that upon which the Mu'tazila rely, and it is weak. Its explanation is from [several] aspects: First: It is not a condition of an act's being a cause for deserving veneration that it be burdensome, as evidenced by the fact that the reward of the prophets, peace be upon them, is greater than the reward of the common people, even though they endured hardship beyond what the infallible messenger bore. If this is established, we say: If the intended [goal] is what you mentioned of deserving veneration, then it would be proper for Him, exalted, to impose upon them easy, gentle obligations, and to increase the strength of their bodies so that when they performed them, it would be easy for them, and they would obtain the benefit you mentioned, which is deserving veneration. The second aspect in the answer: The purpose of veneration is the occurrence of joy in the heart, and there is no type of joy except that it is fitting for God to create it in the heart of the servant without the mediation of these obligations. The third aspect in refuting this speech: When obligation occurs, two possibilities arise: if he obeys, he obtains deserving veneration; if he disobeys, he falls into great punishment and severe hardship. So this obligation oscillates between these two possibilities. It is known that obtaining veneration is not among necessary matters, for if a man obtains the immediate benefits of veneration, he remains safe from afflictions and benefits from good things. So obtaining veneration is among the supererogatory demands. But if he does not perform it, he falls into great punishment. It is known that striving to repel great punishment is more important than striving to obtain a supererogatory benefit, and there is no need to obtain both at any time. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: if the judgment of reason regarding the good and evil of God's actions and rulings were considered, the necessary duty would be to abandon obligation. The fourth aspect in refuting this speech: What you have mentioned only works if the servant is independent in action and omission. But since it is not established that when power and motive are present the act must occur, this speech retains no benefit.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first objection raised by the Mu'tazilites regarding divine commands. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazilite reliance on this argument is weak and then presents four arguments (وجوه) to refute it. First argument: It is not a condition of an act's being worthy of veneration that it be burdensome; proof is that prophets have greater reward despite undergoing more hardship. If the goal were veneration, God could have imposed easy commands and strengthened their bodies so that performing them would be easy, and they would still obtain the benefit of veneration. Second argument: The purpose of veneration is achieving joy in the heart, and God can create that joy directly without the mediation of commands. Third argument: Commanding entails two possibilities: obedience leading to merit, or disobedience leading to severe punishment. Since merit is not necessary and punishment is great, it is more rational to avoid command altogether, because the risk of punishment outweighs the potential extra benefit. Fourth argument: The Mu'tazilite reasoning only holds if the servant is independent in action and omission. But if it is not established that when power and motive are present the act must occur (i.e., human action is not necessitated), then their argument collapses.

Key Terms
التعظيم
التكليف
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what evidence does the author use to show that burden is not necessary for veneration?
A) Prophets have greater reward despite more hardship.
B) God can create joy directly.
C) The risk of punishment outweighs benefit.
D) The servant is not independent.

Key Takeaway
Hardship is not a necessary condition for veneration; the prophets' greater reward despite greater hardship disproves the Mu'tazilite claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship that is easy for you, do you ever feel it is less valuable or acceptable to Allah? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what would change in your heart if you truly believed that ease can also bring veneration?
?
Think of a time you endured hardship in worship. Did you secretly feel proud or superior to others because of your struggle? How does the prophets' example of greater hardship without it being the cause of veneration humble you?
?
If God can make worship easy and still grant veneration, what does that say about your own excuses for not worshipping 'enough'? Are you willing to abandon the idea that worship must be hard to be real, and instead seek sincerity in ease?

Second Argument: Veneration's Purpose Is Joy, Achievable Directly
Second argument: veneration's purpose is joy directly creatable by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation: that the purpose of veneration is joy in the heart, and God can create that joy directly without commands.

Core Idea
God can produce joy directly, so no need for commands to generate veneration.

The second argument simplifies the Mu'tazilite claim by focusing on the ultimate aim of veneration: joy in the heart. Since God has the power to create that joy directly without any intermediary, the entire rationale for imposing commands becomes unnecessary. This argument challenges the Mu'tazilite assumption that commands are the means to veneration.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second argument. Notice its brevity.
2
Identify the core claim
The author states that the purpose of veneration is joy in the heart. Understand this premise.
3
Follow the implication
God can create any type of joy directly in the heart. Thus, commands are not needed to produce veneration. How does this refute the Mu'tazilite view?
This undermines the idea that commands are necessary for acquiring merit.

Third Argument: The Risk of Punishment Outweighs the Benefit of Veneration
Third argument: obligation's risk of punishment outweighs merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third refutation: that imposing commands involves a risk of severe punishment that outweighs the potential benefit of veneration, making it irrational to command.

Core Idea
The risk of punishment from commands outweighs the benefit of veneration, making it irrational.

The third argument takes a more practical approach: obligation creates two possibilities—obedience with veneration or disobedience with severe punishment. Since veneration is a supererogatory benefit while punishment is a great harm, it is more rational to avoid imposing commands. This argument challenges the Mu'tazilite reliance on rational good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two possibilities
The author presents two outcomes from obligation: obedience brings veneration, disobedience brings punishment.
2
Compare the value of veneration vs. punishment
Veneration is not a necessary benefit; it is supererogatory. Punishment is severe and harmful. Which is more important?
The author argues that avoiding punishment is more important than seeking an extra benefit.
3
Understand the conclusion
If rational judgment applied to God's actions, the necessary duty would be to abandon obligation entirely.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that commands are rationally justified.

الوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا الكلام : إن عند حصول التكليف يحصل احتمالان فإنه إن أطاع حصل له استحقاق التعظيم ، وإن لم يطع وقع في العذاب العظيم والعناء الشديد، فهذا التكليف دائر بين هذين الاحتمالين . ومن المعلوم أن حصول التعظيم ليس من الأمور الضرورية فإن الرجل إذا حصلت له المنافع الحالية عن التعظيم ، بقي سليماً عن الآفات ، منتفعـاً بالخيرات . فحصول التعظيم من المطالب الزائدة . أما إذا لم يأت به وقع في العذاب العظيم. ومعلوم أن السعي في دفع العذاب العظيم أولى من السعي في تحصيل منفعة زائدة ، ولا حاجة إلى حصولهما في شيء من الأوقات . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح في أفعال (۳) الله وأحكامه
معتبراً ، لكان الواجب اللازم ترك التكليف
•
الوجه الرابع في إبطال هذا الكلام : إن ما ذكرتموه إنما يستقيم إذا كان
العبد مستقلاً بالفعل والترك أما لما لم يثبت أن عند حصول القدرة والداعي يجب الفعل ، فإنه لا يبقى لهذا الكلام فائدة ..
The answer (1): As for the answer to the first aspect: it is that upon which the Mu'tazila rely, and it is weak. Its explanation is from [several] aspects: First: It is not a condition of an act's being a cause for deserving veneration that it be burdensome, as evidenced by the fact that the reward of the prophets, peace be upon them, is greater than the reward of the common people, even though they endured hardship beyond what the infallible messenger bore. If this is established, we say: If the intended [goal] is what you mentioned of deserving veneration, then it would be proper for Him, exalted, to impose upon them easy, gentle obligations, and to increase the strength of their bodies so that when they performed them, it would be easy for them, and they would obtain the benefit you mentioned, which is deserving veneration. The second aspect in the answer: The purpose of veneration is the occurrence of joy in the heart, and there is no type of joy except that it is fitting for God to create it in the heart of the servant without the mediation of these obligations. The third aspect in refuting this speech: When obligation occurs, two possibilities arise: if he obeys, he obtains deserving veneration; if he disobeys, he falls into great punishment and severe hardship. So this obligation oscillates between these two possibilities. It is known that obtaining veneration is not among necessary matters, for if a man obtains the immediate benefits of veneration, he remains safe from afflictions and benefits from good things. So obtaining veneration is among the supererogatory demands. But if he does not perform it, he falls into great punishment. It is known that striving to repel great punishment is more important than striving to obtain a supererogatory benefit, and there is no need to obtain both at any time. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: if the judgment of reason regarding the good and evil of God's actions and rulings were considered, the necessary duty would be to abandon obligation. The fourth aspect in refuting this speech: What you have mentioned only works if the servant is independent in action and omission. But since it is not established that when power and motive are present the act must occur, this speech retains no benefit.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first objection raised by the Mu'tazilites regarding divine commands. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazilite reliance on this argument is weak and then presents four arguments (وجوه) to refute it. First argument: It is not a condition of an act's being worthy of veneration that it be burdensome; proof is that prophets have greater reward despite undergoing more hardship. If the goal were veneration, God could have imposed easy commands and strengthened their bodies so that performing them would be easy, and they would still obtain the benefit of veneration. Second argument: The purpose of veneration is achieving joy in the heart, and God can create that joy directly without the mediation of commands. Third argument: Commanding entails two possibilities: obedience leading to merit, or disobedience leading to severe punishment. Since merit is not necessary and punishment is great, it is more rational to avoid command altogether, because the risk of punishment outweighs the potential extra benefit. Fourth argument: The Mu'tazilite reasoning only holds if the servant is independent in action and omission. But if it is not established that when power and motive are present the act must occur (i.e., human action is not necessitated), then their argument collapses.

Key Terms
التكليف
التعظيم
العذاب
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about imposing commands based on rational judgment?
A) Commands are always beneficial.
B) It would be rational to abandon commands.
C) Commands are necessary for veneration.
D) Punishment is not severe.

Key Takeaway
Obligation entails a risk of severe punishment that outweighs the benefit of veneration, making it irrational to impose commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious command that is difficult, do you weigh the risk of punishment as more urgent than the reward? In your daily life, where have you prioritized convenience over obedience, treating a command as optional?
?
If you truly believed that avoiding Allah's punishment is more important than gaining extra merit, would you change any of your current practices? What specific supererogatory acts have you been delaying while neglecting obligatory ones?
?
This argument shows that reason alone would lead to abandoning obligation. Are you relying on your own reasoning to decide which commands to follow, or do you submit to revelation even when it seems risky? Where have you let your own judgment override divine command?

Fourth Argument: The Mu'tazilite Argument Requires Human Independence
Fourth argument: Mu'tazilite reasoning depends on human independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth refutation: that the Mu'tazilite reasoning only holds if humans are independent agents, a premise that is not established.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite argument collapses if human action is not independent.

The fourth argument targets a hidden assumption in the Mu'tazilite position: that the human servant is independent in performing or omitting actions. If this independence is not proven—and in the Ash'ari view, actions are created by God—then the entire Mu'tazilite argument falls apart. This refutation underscores the theological difference regarding human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
The author states that the Mu'tazilite argument only works if the servant is independent in action and omission.
2
Understand the condition
Independence means the servant can choose to act or not act without external determination. The author questions whether this is established.
3
Consider the consequence
If it is not proven that when power and motive are present the act must occur (i.e., if actions are not necessitated), then the Mu'tazilite argument loses its foundation.
This aligns with the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

أما جواب الوجه الأول : وهو الذي عليه تعويل المعتزلة . وهو ضعيف . فبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : إنه ليس من شرط كون الفعل موجباً لاستحقاق التعظيم ( كـونه شاقاً ) (۲) بدليل : إن ثواب الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أعظم من ثواب آحاد الأمة . وإن كانوا قد تحملوا من العناء فوق ما تحمله الرسول المعصوم . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن كان المقصود هو ما ذكرتم من استحقاق التعظيم فكان من حقه تعالى أن يكلفهم بالتكاليف السهلة الهيئة. وأن يزيد في تقوية أبدانهم ، حتى أنهم إذا أتوا بها سهل ذلك عليهم ، وحصلت لهم المنفعة التي ذكرتموها وهي

Answer to the Second Objection: Three Reasons Why Undeserved Grace Is Superior
Three arguments against the claim that deserved benefit is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational arguments that demonstrate why an undeserved benefit from God is superior to a deserved one, specifically in the context of divine grace and creation.

Core Idea
Undeserved divine grace is better than earned benefit, as shown by reason and analogy.

Sanusi addresses the objection that a deserved benefit is better than an undeserved one. He presents three compelling arguments, each from a different angle: the psychological value of dependence on the master, the necessity of grace in creation, and the rational calculus of obligation. This lesson unpacks each argument to reveal why undeserved grace is not only good but superior.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its context
The objection claims that a deserved benefit is better. Sanusi answers with three aspects. Read the passage to see how he structures his response.
2
Analyze the first argument: The servant and the sultan's robe
Focus on the analogy: a servant earns money but prefers an equal-value robe from the sultan. This shows that a gift from a superior is valued more than earned wealth.
The key term 'المنفعة المستحقة' (deserved benefit) is contrasted with the gift.
3
Examine the second argument: Creation as an act of grace
Creation must begin with grace (tafdil). If conveying grace causes harm, creation would be evil—which is false. Hence, grace is inherently good.
Note the term 'التفضيل' (grace/favor) is central.
4
Study the third argument: Rationality of abandoning obligation
If obedience yields a non-essential benefit and disobedience leads to great punishment, reason says to avoid the risk. Thus, undeserved grace without obligation is superior.
This argument relies on a cost-benefit analysis.
5
Synthesize the three arguments
Each argument independently shows why undeserved grace is superior. Reflect on how they reinforce each other against the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني وهو قوله : المنفعة المستحقة أفضل .
فجوابه من وجوه : الأول : إن كون العبد مستكفياً من إنعام مولاه، مفيد جداً . ولذلك فإن العبد إذا اكتسب بسعي نفسه مائة دينار، وخلع عليه السلطان الأعظم خلعة تساوي مائة دينار، فإن كل أحد يرجح هذه الخلعة على ذلك المال .
·
الوجه الثاني : إن ابتداء الخلق لا بد وأن يقع على سبيل التفضيل . فإن كان إيصال التفضيل إليه يوجب نوعاً من أنواع الضرر، لزم أن يقبح ابتداء الخلق والتكوين .
الوجه الثالث : إذا كلفه فإن أطاع استفاد هذه الفائدة التي لا يلزم من فواتها قوات أمر مهم، وإن لم يطع وقع في العذاب العظيم . وقد بينا أن مقتضى العقل في هذه الصورة : الترك .
As for the second aspect, which is his saying: 'The deserved benefit is better.' Its answer is from [several] aspects: First: The servant's being independent of the grace of his Lord is very beneficial. For if a servant earns a hundred dinars by his own effort, and the great sultan bestows upon him a robe worth a hundred dinars, then everyone would prefer this robe over that money. Second: The beginning of creation must necessarily occur on the basis of grace. For if conveying grace to him causes some type of harm, then it would follow that the beginning of creation and bringing into existence would be evil. Third: If He obliges him and he obeys, he obtains this benefit, the loss of which does not necessitate the loss of an important matter; but if he disobeys, he falls into great punishment. We have already shown that reason dictates in this situation: abandonment.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which claims that a benefit that is deserved (by action) is better than an unearned one. The author provides three arguments. First, using the analogy of a servant who earns money by his own effort and then receives a robe of equal value from the sultan; everyone prefers the robe because it comes from a generous patron. This shows that a gift – even if equal in value – is considered superior because it highlights the servant's dependence and the master's grace. Second, the very act of creation must be an act of grace (tafdil). If giving grace to a creature causes harm, then creation itself would be evil, which is false. Third, if God imposes obligations and the servant obeys, he gets a benefit whose absence is not critical; but if he disobeys, he falls into great punishment. Reason suggests that it is better to avoid the risk of punishment, so abandoning obligation would be more rational.

Key Terms
المنفعة المستحقة
التفضيل
الخلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a deserved benefit is always better than an undeserved one in all contexts.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates through three arguments that undeserved divine grace is superior, especially in the context of God's relationship with creation.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to argue that a gift from a master is preferred over earned wealth?
A) A servant who earns money and then receives a robe from the sultan.
B) A prophet who undergoes hardship.
C) A person who avoids punishment.
D) A child who is given a toy.

Key Takeaway
Undeserved divine grace is superior to deserved benefit, as shown by logical arguments from dependence, creation, and rational risk avoidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, do you truly value the blessings you have earned by your effort more than those you received as unearned grace? Why does your heart lean one way or the other?
?
The author argues that reason dictates abandoning obligations that carry the risk of punishment rather than imposing them. When you think of God's commands in your life, do you ever secretly wish He had not obligated you? How does this lesson challenge that feeling?
?
If creation itself is an act of grace, does that change how you view your very existence? Do you live as if your existence is a gift you must earn, or as if it is a pure favor from your Lord? What one change would reflect this truth starting today?

Answer to the Third Objection: Divine Power and Afterlife Pleasure
Answer to objection that afterlife pleasure depends on obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's power to create pleasure in the afterlife is not dependent on religious obligations as intermediaries.

Core Idea
God’s omnipotence makes obligations unnecessary for afterlife pleasure.

The third objection claims that the pleasure of the afterlife is conditional upon performing religious duties. This lesson presents the author's response: since pleasure is possible and God is all-powerful, He can create it directly without needing obligations as a means. The argument emphasizes divine independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage that presents the third objection and its answer. Notice how the author first states the objection then refutes it.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the key points: the pleasure is possible in itself, God is capable of all possibles, so He can create it without mediation.
This is a simple syllogism.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this answer matters for the debate on the necessity of obligations. It shows God's direct creative power.
This guards against limiting God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثالث : وهو إيصال اللذة والسرور ( إليه في الدار الآخرة، مشروط بهذا التكليف فجوابه: إن وجود اللذة والسرور ( ) ممكن في ذاته . والله تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات، فكان قادراً على إيجادهما من غير واسطة هذه التكاليف
وأما مذهب الحكماء : فغير وارد على هذا الكلام لأن عندهم الثواب والعقاب لا يحصلان بقصد الفاعل المختار وبتكوينه، بل هما نوعان من لوازم الأفعال البشرية . وذلك قول صحيح معلوم لكن تفريعاً على القول بأن إله العالم موجب بالذات : أما على التفريع بأن إله العالم فاعل مختار وأنه باختياره يخص
As for the third aspect: it is the conveying of pleasure and joy to him in the afterlife, conditioned by this obligation. Its answer: The existence of pleasure and joy is possible in itself. And God, exalted, is capable of all possible things, so He is capable of bringing them into existence without the mediation of these obligations. As for the doctrine of the philosophers: it does not apply to this speech, because for them reward and punishment are not obtained by the intention and creation of the free agent, but rather they are two types of necessary concomitants of human actions. That is a correct and known statement, but as a branch upon the statement that the Lord of the world is a necessary agent. As for the branch that the Lord of the world is a free agent and that He chooses specifically...
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: the answer to the third objection and a critique of the philosophers' view. The third objection claims that the pleasure and joy of the afterlife are conditional upon the performance of religious obligations. The author answers by pointing out that pleasure and joy are possible in themselves, and since God is omnipotent over all possibles, He can create them directly without any need for obligations as intermediaries. Next, the author addresses the philosophers (الحكماء) who hold that reward and punishment are necessary consequences of human actions, not acts of a free divine agent. The author notes that this view is correct in itself, but only as a derivative of a particular premise: that God is a necessary agent (موجب بالذات). However, if one holds that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار) who acts by choice, then the philosophers' doctrine does not apply. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is that the philosophers' framework is incompatible with divine freedom.

التكاليف

Correction: God can create pleasure directly without obligations as intermediaries because He is omnipotent over all possible things.

Refutation of Philosophers: Divine Free Will vs. Necessity
Critique of philosophers' view that reward/punishment are necessary concomitants of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers' view of reward and punishment as necessary concomitants is only valid if one assumes God is a necessary agent, not if He is a free agent.

Core Idea
The philosophers' doctrine assumes God is necessary, but He is free.

The author now critiques the philosophers who claim that reward and punishment are necessary consequences of human actions, not bestowed by a free divine agent. He observes that this view is correct only under the premise that God is a necessary agent (موجب بالذات). But if one affirms that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار), the philosophers' framework collapses. This lesson clarifies the conditional validity of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the philosophers' view: reward and punishment are not from a free agent but are necessary concomitants of human actions.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author says their view is correct but only as a branch of the statement that God is a necessary agent. Note the conditional nature.
This is a key nuance.
3
Understand the alternative
Contrast with the branch that God is a free agent. The author implies that under free agency, reward and punishment are not necessary concomitants but given by divine choice.
The text cuts off, but the implication is clear.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding divine justice and free will.

وأما الوجه الثالث : وهو إيصال اللذة والسرور ( إليه في الدار الآخرة، مشروط بهذا التكليف فجوابه: إن وجود اللذة والسرور ( ) ممكن في ذاته . والله تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات، فكان قادراً على إيجادهما من غير واسطة هذه التكاليف

As for the third aspect: it is the conveying of pleasure and joy to him in the afterlife, conditioned by this obligation. Its answer: The existence of pleasure and joy is possible in itself. And God, exalted, is capable of all possible things, so He is capable of bringing them into existence without the mediation of these obligations. As for the doctrine of the philosophers: it does not apply to this speech, because for them reward and punishment are not obtained by the intention and creation of the free agent, but rather they are two types of necessary concomitants of human actions. That is a correct and known statement, but as a branch upon the statement that the Lord of the world is a necessary agent. As for the branch that the Lord of the world is a free agent and that He chooses specifically...
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: the answer to the third objection and a critique of the philosophers' view. The third objection claims that the pleasure and joy of the afterlife are conditional upon the performance of religious obligations. The author answers by pointing out that pleasure and joy are possible in themselves, and since God is omnipotent over all possibles, He can create them directly without any need for obligations as intermediaries. Next, the author addresses the philosophers (الحكماء) who hold that reward and punishment are necessary consequences of human actions, not acts of a free divine agent. The author notes that this view is correct in itself, but only as a derivative of a particular premise: that God is a necessary agent (موجب بالذات). However, if one holds that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار) who acts by choice, then the philosophers' doctrine does not apply. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is that the philosophers' framework is incompatible with divine freedom.

Key Terms

الموجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' view about reward and punishment is universally valid regardless of God's nature.
Correction: It is only valid if we assume God is a necessary agent; it fails if we affirm He is a free agent.

Checkpoint
Under what premise does the author consider the philosophers' view of reward and punishment as necessary concomitants to be correct?
A) If God is a free agent.
B) If God is a necessary agent.
C) If God is not involved.
D) If actions are not free.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' view of reward and punishment as necessary is valid only under the assumption of a necessary God, not a free Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Have you ever reduced Allah to a necessary cause, as if He is bound by laws of nature? How would your worship change if you fully embraced that He is a free agent who chooses to respond to you?

Analogies of Impossibility Refute Divine Knowledge as Preventer
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the seventh argument: analogies showing the absurdity of commanding the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-`Izz uses analogies of clear impossibilities to refute the claim that divine knowledge prevents action.

Core Idea
Impossible commands are rationally absurd — analogies make the point undeniable.

In this passage, Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents its seventh argument, using striking analogies to demonstrate why commanding the impossible is rationally impermissible. The argument assumes a hypothetical scenario where divine knowledge would make belief impossible; by showing the absurdity of analogous commands, the argument refutes that assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic once to get the flow without dissection.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument begins with a conditional premise: if commanding the impossible were permissible in this case, then three absurd commands would be permissible.
The conclusion is that since those commands are not permissible, commanding the impossible is not permissible.
3
Examine each analogy
The three analogies are: (1) commanding a blind man to dot the Quran, (2) commanding a paralyzed person to fly, (3) asking a bound man thrown from a cliff why he didn't fly.
Each is clearly impossible to reason.
4
Connect back to the original issue
The original issue is whether God's knowledge that a person will not believe makes commanding belief impossible. If that were true, then analogously all these impossible commands would be possible, but they are not.
5
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, knowledge does not prevent action, and commanding belief is not commanding the impossible.
The argument refutes the premise that divine foreknowledge makes commands impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لو جاز ورود الأمر بالمحال في هذه الصورة لجاز أمر الأعمى بنقط المصاحف، وأمر الزمن أن يطير في الهواء، وأن يقال لمن قيد يداه ورجلاه، وألقى من شاهق جبل : لم لم نطر إلى السموات ولما لم يجز شيء من ذلك في العقول. علمنا أنه لا يجوز الأمر بالمحال، وذلك يدل على أن العلم والخبر غير مانع من الفعل.
The seventh proof: If it were permissible for the command of the impossible to occur in this form (i.e., commanding belief while knowledge prevents it), then it would be permissible to command a blind man to dot the copies of the Quran, and to command a paralyzed person to fly in the air, and to say to one whose hands and feet are tied and who is thrown from a mountain peak: 'Why did you not fly to the heavens?' And since none of that is permissible in intellects, we know that commanding the impossible is not permissible. And that indicates that knowledge and report are not preventers of action.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which uses clear analogies to show the absurdity of commanding the impossible. The argument assumes (for the sake of refutation) that divine knowledge prevents action, meaning that commanding an act like belief would be commanding something impossible (since God knows it won't happen). If that were permissible, then by analogy, all sorts of obviously impossible commands would also be permissible. Examples include: commanding a blind man to dot the Quran (i.e., write diacritical marks), commanding a paralyzed person to fly, and asking a person bound and thrown from a cliff why he didn't fly to the heavens. These are universally recognized as absurd by rational minds. Since such commands are not permissible, the original premise that divine knowledge prevents action (making commands impossible) must be false. Therefore, knowledge and report do not prevent action.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
الأعمى
نقط المصاحف
الزمن
الطيران
العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that physical impossibility and logical impossibility are the same; the argument uses physical impossibilities as clear examples to argue for logical impermissibility.
Correction: The analogies illustrate logical absurdity, not just physical incapacity. The point is that commanding the impossible is rationally impermissible, not merely practically difficult.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the seventh argument use to show the absurdity of commanding the impossible?
A) Commanding a thirsty man to drink water
B) Commanding a blind man to dot the Quran
C) Commanding a rich man to give charity
D) Commanding a sinner to repent

Key Takeaway
The analogies of commanding a blind man to dot Qurans and a paralyzed person to fly show that commanding the impossible is rationally absurd, disproving the claim that divine knowledge prevents action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim you 'cannot' obey Allah in a particular command, are you really implying that Allah has commanded the impossible? How does this lesson expose the weakness of that excuse?
?
The analogy of the tied man thrown from a cliff asks why he didn't fly. In what area of your life have you used impossibility as a shield against obedience? What first step will you take to act, knowing that your inability is not a legitimate excuse?
?
If divine knowledge does not prevent action, then your own knowledge of your past failures does not prevent future obedience. What one command have you been avoiding because you assume you will fail? Will you now attempt it, trusting that action is possible despite your feelings?

Rational Judgments

The First Proof: Rational Judgment Cannot Apply to God's Actions
First proof with logical argument and one complete analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof that reason cannot judge good and evil in God's actions, and how the analogy of the fort illustrates the flaw in applying human ethical reasoning to God.

Core Idea
If rational judgment applied to God, commanding a known disbeliever would be evil—but it's not.

This passage presents the first of several proofs against the idea that human reason can independently determine good and evil in God's actions. The author formulates a conditional argument: if rational judgment governed God's actions, then a particular command (burdening a known disbeliever with obligation) would be evil. Since that conclusion is false, the premise must be false. He then clarifies the entailment and illustrates it with a powerful analogy from human experience.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional argument
Read the opening lines where the author states the conditional: 'If rational judgment applied to God, then commanding a known disbeliever would be evil. This is false, so the premise is false.' Identify the logical structure.
The argument is a modus tollens: If P, then Q; Not Q; therefore Not P.
2
Examine the entailment (mulazama)
The author clarifies that we do not claim faith is impossible from the disbeliever; rather, we claim that in human context such a command would be considered evil. This is key to the analogy.
This step prevents a common misunderstanding that the argument denies human free will.
3
Analyze the first analogy (the fort builder)
Read the analogy of a person who builds a fort knowing it will become a stronghold for thieves. He claims good intentions, but human judgment condemns him. Notice how this mirrors the case of God commanding a known disbeliever.
The analogy shows that when an action leads to predictable harm despite claimed good intentions, human reason judges it evil.
4
Consider the application to God
Reflect on how the analogy transfers to God's command. If human reason would judge the fort builder's action evil, it would also judge God's command evil—but we know God's command is not evil, so human reason is not a valid standard.
The author concludes 'So likewise here' to complete the parallel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات التاسع
في
ذكر أنواع أخر من الدلائل على فساد القول بتحسين العمل وتقبيحه في أفعال الله وفي أم كامه
فالحجة الأولى(١) : أن نقول : لو جرى حكم تحسين العقل وتقبيحه في أفعال الله ، وفي أحكامه لقبح منه تكليف من علم أنه يكفر . وهذا باطل فذاك
باطل
بين الملازمة : إنا لا ندعي ههنا أن صدور الإيمان منه ممتنع ، إلا أنـا
نقول : إن هذا التكليف قبيح في الشاهد . وبيان ذلك بأمثلة :
المثال الأول : أن من بني رباطاً في مفازة مهلكة ، وعلم علماً يقيناً لا ريب فيه أنه متى بنى ذلك الرباط ، فإن اللصوص وقطاع الطريق يتخذون قلعة حصينة لأنفسهم ويعظم ضررهم على الناس ، ويتعذر بسبب ذلك الرباط قهرهم ومنعهم . ثم إنه مع هذا اليقين يبني ذلك الرباط ويقول : ما أردت منه إلا أن يجتمع أولئك اللصوص فيه ، ويتركون اللصوصية ويؤمنون الطرقات ، ويعينون القوافل على ما لهم من المهمات والخيرات ، فكل من اعتقد فيه أنه مع ذلك اليقين بلى ذلك الرباط قضى عليه بكونه ساعياً في فتح باب الآفات ، والمخافات ويكذبونه في قوله : إلي ما قصدت إلا الخير والنفع فكذا مهنا
•
المثال الثاني : كل من جمع بين عبيده وإماته ، وزين البعض في عين
The ninth section (or: The ninth proof?): Mentioning other types of evidences for the corruption of the statement of the rationalistic judgment of good and evil in God's actions and in His commands. So the first proof: that we say: If the ruling of rational judgment of good and evil were applied to God's actions and His commands, then it would be evil for Him to burden (with obligation) one whom He knows will disbelieve. And this is false, so that (the premise) is false. Clarifying the entailment: We do not claim here that the occurrence of faith from him is impossible, but we say that this burdening (obligation) is evil in the witnessed (human) context. And its clarification is by examples: The first example: that one who builds a fort in a perilous desert, and knows with certain knowledge without doubt that whenever he builds that fort, the thieves and highway robbers will take it as a strong fortress for themselves, and their harm to people becomes great, and due to that fort it becomes difficult to overpower them and prevent them. Then, with this certainty, he builds that fort and says: I intend nothing from it except that those thieves gather in it, and they abandon thievery and make the roads safe, and they assist the caravans with their important matters and good deeds. So anyone who judges him - that with that certainty he built that fort - judges him to be one who strives to open the door of calamities and fears, and they reject his statement: 'I intended nothing but good and benefit.' So likewise here. • The second example: Everyone who gathers his male slaves and his female slaves, and adorns some in the eye...
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the doctrine that reason can judge good and evil in God's actions and commands (al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-aqli). The author begins by stating the argument: if rational judgment applied to God, then it would be evil for Him to command someone whom He knows will disbelieve. But this conclusion is false (since we see in revelation that God did command such people, and it is not evil), therefore the premise (that rational judgment applies to God) is false. The author clarifies the entailment (al-mulazama): we do not claim that faith is impossible from that person; rather, we say that in human context (al-shahid) such an action would be considered evil. Then the author illustrates with two analogies from human experience. The first analogy: a person builds a fort in a dangerous desert knowing with certainty that it will become a stronghold for thieves and increase harm, yet he claims his intention is for good. In human judgment, such a person is condemned as opening the door to calamities, and his claimed good intention is rejected. The author implies that similarly, if God commands a known disbeliever, it would be evil according to human rational judgment, but since it is not evil in God's case, the rational standard does not apply to God. The second analogy is cut off but likely continues the same theme.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
الحجة الأولى
المثال الأول
المثال الثاني
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God's command is actually evil or that the person cannot possibly believe.
Correction: The argument does not assert either; it only says that if human rational standards were applied, the command would be deemed evil, but since it is not evil in reality, the standard is invalid for God.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof's main claim about rational judgment in God's actions?
A) Rational judgment applies to God if it applies to humans.
B) If rational judgment applied to God, then commanding a known disbeliever would be evil, but it is not, so rational judgment does not apply.
C) God never commands someone who will disbelieve, so the example is invalid.
D) The analogy of the fort proves that human judgment is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that applying human rational ethics to God leads to absurd consequences, so reason cannot be the source of moral obligation in God's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of God that seems harsh or unfair by your own reasoning, do you instinctively judge it as ‘bad’? How does this proof expose the arrogance of applying your own standards to the Creator? Will you commit to surrendering your judgment to His wisdom, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The fort builder’s analogy shows that good intentions do not justify actions that cause foreseeable harm. Have you ever excused a wrong action of yours by claiming a noble intention, while ignoring the negative consequences you could have foreseen? How will you now hold yourself more accountable for the full impact of your choices?
?
If reason cannot judge God’s actions, then your sense of justice is limited. How does this realization challenge your tendency to question God’s decrees in your life—like the loss of a loved one or an unanswered prayer? What concrete step will you take to trust His plan more than your own understanding?

Rational Judgments

The First Proof Against Rational Good and Evil in God's Actions
Al-Ghazali presents the first proof with five examples against rational moral judgment in God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's first proof that rational moral judgments do not apply to God's actions, and how he uses analogies from human experience to illustrate his argument.

Core Idea
If rational good and evil applied to God, His obligation of the disbeliever would be evil; but it is not, so the doctrine is false.

Al-Ghazali challenges the doctrine of rational good and evil by arguing that if it applied to God, then actions like obligating someone He knows will disbelieve would be deemed evil. Since Muslims do not deem such actions evil, the doctrine fails. He supports this with five vivid examples from everyday life showing that when someone knowingly facilitates harm, their claimed good intention is dismissed by observers. This lesson unpacks the logic and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, a logical implication, and five examples.
The passage is dense; take your time with each example.
2
Identify the logical core
Identify the main argument: if rational judgments applied to God, then obligating someone known to disbelieve would be evil. The conclusion that this is false (since opponents do not consider it evil) leads to the rejection of the premise.
Look for the statement 'فهذا باطل' (this is false) and the connection.
3
Examine the analogies
Go through each of the five examples. For each, note the scenario, the agent's claimed intention, the certain knowledge of harm, and the observers' judgment. Reflect on how each parallels the case of God obligating the disbeliever.
The examples build in intensity; the last one involves a father and son.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
After studying the examples, articulate how they collectively show that if rational judgment applied to God, we would have to condemn Him, but we do not. Therefore, the doctrine must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في ذكر أنواع أخر من الدلائل على فساد القول بتحسين العمل وتقبيحه في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه فالحجة الأولى أن نقول : لو جرى حكم تحسين العقل وتقبيحه في أفعال الله ، وفي أحكامه لقبح منه تكليف من علم أنه يكفر . وهذا باطل فذاك باطل بين الملازمة : إنا لا ندعي ههنا أن صدور الإيمان منه ممتنع ، إلا أنا نقول : إن هذا التكليف قبيح في الشاهد . وبيان ذلك بأمثلة : المثال الأول : أن من بني رباطاً في في البعض على أقصى الوجوه ، ثم وضع عندهم كل ما يحتاجون إليه من الأطعمة والأشربة وحصل في قلوبهم شهوة أن يفجر بعضهم بالبعض ، وقوى تلك الشهوة ، وأزال الموانع . ثم يقول : إني إنما فعلت ذلك حتى يجتهد كل واحد منهم في منع نفسه عن تلك الشهوات ، حتى يستوجب مزيد الثواب والكرامة . ثم إنه يعلم علماً يقيناً أنه لا تحصل هذه العصمة والطهارة البتة ، وأن الحاصل ليس إلا الفجور والفسق ، فإن كل أحد يكذبه في دعواه ، ويقول له : إنك ما أردت إلا فتح باب الفجور والشرور . المثال الثالث : من دفع سكيناً إلى عبده ، وعلم يقيناً أنه متى دفع ذلك السكين إليه فإنه يقتل به ولده ، ثم إنه مع هذا اليقين ، يقول : إني إنما دفعت هذا السكين إليه ليقتل به عدواً لي . فإن كل أحد يكذبه ، ويقول : إنك لما علمت علماً يقيناً أنه لا يقتل بذلك السكين إلا ولدك ، ثم إنك دفعته إليه ، وأزلت عنه جميع الموانع من قتل ولدك . دل هذا على أنك كنت ساعياً في قتل ولدك . المثال الرابع : من وقع في بئر ، فألقى إنسان إليه حبلاً . ويعلم هذا الملقى يقيناً أنه متى ألقى الحبل إليه ، فإنه يخنق بذلك الحبل نفسه . ثم إنه مع هذا اليقين ألقى ذلك الحبل إليه ، حكم كل أحد بأنه إنما ألقاه إليه ليقتل به نفسه . المثال الخامس : قال محمد بن زكريا الرازي في المناظرة الطويلة التي دارت بينه وبين الكعبي : إن رجلاً لو علم ابنه السباحة حتى تمهر فيها ، ثم كلفه عبور نهر ، ليصل إلى موضع فيه دواء يشفيه من مرض به ، إلا أنه عالم بأن ابنه هذا سيمسك عن السباحة باختياره حتى يصير ذلك سبباً لغرقه ، ثم إنه مع هذا العلم يأمر ولده بالسباحة ، ولم يمنعه منه أشد المنع ، فإنه يعد ذلك من الأب عاقاً غير ناظر لذلك الولد ولا مريداً للأمر الأصلح له.
The ninth chapter: On mentioning other types of evidences for the corruption of the doctrine of rational good and evil in the actions of God and in His rulings. The first proof: that we say: If the judgment of rational good and evil applied to the actions of God and to His rulings, then it would be evil for Him to obligate someone He knows will disbelieve. And this is false, so that is false. The proof of the connection: We do not claim here that the occurrence of faith from him is impossible, but rather we say that this obligation is evil in the witnessed realm. And its explanation is by examples: The first example: That someone builds a fort in a deadly desert, and knows with certain knowledge without doubt that whenever he builds that fort, then thieves and highwaymen will take it as a strong fortress for themselves and their harm to people will become great, and due to that fort, overpowering them and preventing them becomes difficult. Then, with this certainty, he builds that fort and says, 'I only intended by it that those thieves gather there, abandon thievery, make roads safe, and help caravans with what they need of important matters and goods.' So everyone who considers him with that certainty building that fort judges that he is striving to open the door of calamities and fears, and they deem him a liar in his saying, 'I only intended good and benefit.' So likewise here. The second example: Whoever gathers his male and female slaves, adorns some in the eyes of others, and makes some desire others to the utmost degree, then places with them all they need of food and drink, and creates in their hearts a desire to commit fornication with one another, strengthens that desire, and removes obstacles. Then he says, 'I only did that so that each one of them might strive to restrain himself from those desires, to merit increased reward and honor.' Then he knows with certain knowledge that this infallibility and purity will never occur, and that the outcome is nothing but fornication and transgression. Then everyone deems him a liar in his claim and says to him, 'You only intended to open the door of fornication and evils.' The third example: Someone hands a knife to his slave, and knows with certainty that whenever he hands that knife to him, he will kill his son with it. Then, with this certainty, he says, 'I only handed this knife to him for him to kill an enemy of mine with it.' Then everyone deems him a liar and says, 'Since you knew with certain knowledge that he will kill none but your son with that knife, then you handed it to him and removed from him all obstacles to killing your son, this indicates that you were striving to kill your son.' The fourth example: Someone falls into a well, and a person throws a rope to him. And this thrower knows with certainty that whenever he throws the rope to him, he will strangle himself with that rope. Then, with this certainty, he throws that rope to him: everyone judges that he only threw it to him for him to kill himself with it. The fifth example: Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi said, in the long debate that took place between him and al-Kaʿbi, that if a man taught his son swimming until he became proficient in it, then charged him with crossing a river to reach a place containing medicine that would cure him from an illness he has, except that he knows that this son will refrain from swimming by his own choice until that becomes a cause for his drowning, then with this knowledge he commands his son to swim and does not prevent him with the utmost prevention, then that father is considered negligent, not caring for that son, nor desiring what is best for him.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a proof by al-Ghazali against the doctrine of rational good and evil (taḥsīn and takbīḥ) as applied to God's actions and rulings. The argument proceeds by establishing an implication: if rational moral judgments applied to God, then it would be evil (qabīḥ) for God to obligate someone He knows will disbelieve. But this is false (bāṭil) because God does such obligations, and the opponent does not consider them evil. Therefore, the premise that rational judgments apply is false. Al-Ghazali clarifies that he is not claiming that faith is impossible from the person, but that in human experience (shāhid), such an obligation would be considered evil. He then illustrates this with five examples, each an analogy to God's actions. In each example, a person performs an action with certain knowledge that it will lead to harm, yet claims a good intention. Observers judge the person's actions as evil, despite the claimed intention. Al-Ghazali argues that similarly, if rational moral judgment applied, God's actions (like obligating someone He knows will disbelieve) would be condemned, but they are not. Therefore, the doctrine of rational good and evil does not apply to God. The examples progress: building a fort that enables thieves, placing slaves in a situation likely to lead to fornication, giving a knife to a slave who will kill his son, throwing a rope to a drowning man who will strangle himself, and a father sending his son to swim knowing he will drown. Each example drives home the point that certain knowledge of a harmful outcome, combined with the action that enables it, leads observers to deem the agent's intention insincere and the action evil.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه (rational judgment of good and evil)
تكليف (obligation)
قبيح (evil)
شاهد (witnessed realm)
الملازمة (implication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the examples deny God's wisdom or justice; rather, they show that human reasoning cannot judge divine actions.
Correction: Al-Ghazali's examples are analogies to show that if rational moral judgments applied to God, we would have to condemn Him, but we do not. They do not claim that God's actions are actually evil, but that human categories of good and evil do not apply to Him.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument in al-Ghazali's first proof against rational good and evil in God's actions?
A) That rational good and evil do not exist.
B) That if rational moral judgments applied to God, they would make His actions evil, but since they are not, the doctrine is false.
C) That God never obligates someone He knows will disbelieve.
D) That the examples show human actions are always evil.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's first proof demonstrates that applying rational moral categories to God leads to absurd implications, thereby invalidating the doctrine of rational good and evil in divine actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust by your own reasoning, do you trust that God's wisdom is beyond your comprehension, or do you let your own sense of fairness dictate your trust? This lesson demands that you examine your heart: can you submit to God's actions even when they seem absurd to your mind? What would it take for you to genuinely surrender that judgment to Him?
?
Think of a time you doubted God's wisdom in a trial. According to this lesson, your moral judgment is limited. Are you willing to stop measuring God by your standards and instead measure your standards by God? What concrete change in your attitude would that require?
?
Al-Ghazali's examples show that even in human affairs, the agent's claimed good intention is rejected when the outcome is certain. Do you sometimes deceive yourself into thinking your intentions are pure while your actions cause predictable harm? How does this lesson call you to be more honest about your own motives before God?
