The Contradiction of Self-Defeating Threats
Second division of third argument, analogy, and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division of the third argument, including the analogy of the master and slave, and the conclusion about the non-essential nature of merit for veneration.

Core Idea
Punishing someone for failing to benefit themselves is like cutting a slave's limbs for not earning a dinar—sheer contradiction.

The author continues the third argument by presenting the second division, which unfolds as an even more explicit contradiction. The command to the servant is for his own benefit, yet the punishment is inflicted precisely because he did not obtain that benefit. This is illustrated with a powerful analogy: a master commanding his slave to earn a dinar for himself and then cutting his limbs with fiery scissors for failing. The lesson concludes by noting that the merit for veneration that comes from obligations is not a primary need, so its loss does not justify such punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division
Note how the author first expresses the contradiction in abstract terms: the command is for the servant's benefit, yet punishment is for not obtaining that benefit.
The phrase 'this is contradictory' (hādhā mutanāqiḍ) appears twice.
2
Examine the analogy
The analogy of the master and slave makes the contradiction vivid: a master orders his slave to earn a dinar for himself, then punishes him for failing with fiery shears. This is 'extremely corrupt' speech.
The analogy is designed to show the absurdity of the second division.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author adds that the only thing dependent on obligation is the merit for veneration (istiḥqāq al-taʿẓīm), which is not a primary need. Losing it does not justify punishment.
This reinforces that the threat is not only contradictory but also excessive.

Source Text

Do You punish me for a benefit returning to You? Or to me, or to a third? If You punish me for such an act or such an omission for a benefit returning to You, then You are needy and seeker of drawing benefit and repelling harm. And that is impossible. Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against the validity of rational moral judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) in relation to God. The argument is structured as a conditional: If reason's judgment of good and evil were valid for God, then it would be evil for God to threaten His servant with punishment for doing or not doing certain acts. This reinforces the impossibility of applying human rational ethics to divine commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God cannot punish any disobedience.
Correction: The argument only targets punishment that is for the servant's own benefit and fails. It does not deny God's right to punish for other reasons, such as justice or vindication of His authority.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use in the second division to illustrate the contradiction?
A) A king punishing a subject for not paying taxes.
B) A father scolding his son for not studying.
C) A master telling his slave to earn a dinar and then cutting his limbs with fiery scissors for failing.
D) A teacher failing a student for not completing homework.

Key Takeaway
The second division shows that punishing a servant for failing to benefit himself is contradictory, and the loss of non-essential merit does not justify eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a time when you expected someone to do something for their own good, but then punished or resented them when they failed? How did your reaction mirror the contradiction in the passage? This week, when you feel frustration at someone's lack of self-care, stop and ask: Am I being like the master who commands benefit but then harms for the failure?
?
The passage says that the only thing dependent on obligation is 'merit for veneration'—not essential needs. What does that say about how you view your own religious duties? Do you sometimes act as if God's commands are for God's benefit, or as if failing them brings ultimate harm beyond the loss of that merit? Pause and examine your heart: Do you truly believe that God's commands are for your good, or do you secretly think God needs your obedience?
?
Imagine you are the servant in the analogy: commanded to earn a dinar for yourself, then threatened with fiery scissors if you don't. How would you feel about the master? Now, how often do you relate to God with that same sense of threat? Are there areas of your life where you obey God out of fear of punishment rather than desire for the benefit He promises? Be honest: would you still seek God if there were no threat at all? Use this lesson to reshape your intention.

Rational Judgments

Divine Punishment and the Limits of Analogy
Refutes Mu'tazilite punishment divisions and contrasts divine vs human justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite division of punishment is flawed and how the master-servant analogy fails to apply to God.

Core Idea
God's punishment is for the servant's benefit, not His own.

The author examines possible reasons for God punishing a servant and finds each either contradictory or unjust. He then contrasts with human punishment to expose the flaw in the Mu'tazilite analogy, showing that God's actions are fundamentally different from human masters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument structure.
The passage begins mid-argument, so pay attention to the flow.
2
Identify the three divisions
Notice how the author presents and rejects each division: first (implied), third, and fourth.
The first division is mentioned only briefly; focus on the explicit ones.
3
Examine the master-servant analogy
Understand why human punishment is justified (master's benefit) and why that logic does not apply to God.
God cannot benefit from servants' actions.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that God's commands are for the servants' benefit, so punishment is not evil.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that rational judgments of good and evil apply to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتعذيبه عليه يقتضي إلحاق أعظم المضار به ، لأجل أنه قصر في تحصيل منفعة غير ضرورية لنفسه . ومعلوم أنه متناقض
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يعذبه الله تعالى لأجل أن يحصل بواسطته نفع لغيره . فهذا محض الظلم ، لأن الظالم لا يفعل شيئاً إلا أن يلحق الضرر بغيره ، لأجل أن يلحق النفع بنفسه . والله متعالي عن الظلم
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أنه تعالى يلحق العقاب بهذا العبد ، لا لشيء من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة . فهذا أيضاً محض الظلم ولا يبقى معه اعتبار البتة
بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه .
4
واعلم أن السيد إذا أمر عبده في الشاهد بفعل . فما أطاعه ، فإنه يحسن من السيد إيذاء ذلك العبد وإيلامه . وذلك لأن السيد يقول : إنما أمرتك بالفعل الفلاني لأجل أن أستفيد من ذلك الفعل ، نفعاً بنفسي فلما لم تأت بذلك الفعل ، فقد بقيت محروماً من ذلك النفع ، فأعاقبك لأجل أنك سعيت في تفويت النفع علي . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال ، لأنه يمتنع أن ينتفع بأفعال العباد . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أمرهم ، وإنما نهاهم لمنافع عائدة إليهم. وقد بينا أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك فإنه ( لا ) (۱) يقبح من الله تعالى أن يعاقبهم على ترك الواجب ، أو على فعل الحرام ، فظهر الفرق بين البابين
His punishment of him necessitates causing the greatest harm to him, because he fell short in obtaining a non-necessary benefit for himself. And it is known that he is contradictory.
As for the third division: that God punishes him in order that through him benefit may accrue to another. This is sheer injustice, because the unjust person does not do anything except that he causes harm to another in order to bring benefit to himself. And God is exalted above injustice.
As for the fourth division: that He, exalted, inflicts punishment upon this servant, for none of these three divisions. This also is sheer injustice, and no consideration remains with it at all.
by [the principle of] rational judgment of good and evil.
4
Know that if a master commands his servant in the witnessed world to do an act, and he does not obey him, then it is good for the master to harm that servant and cause him pain. That is because the master says: 'I only commanded you to do such an act in order that I may derive benefit from that act for myself. Since you did not do that act, I have remained deprived of that benefit, so I punish you because you strove to cause me to lose the benefit.' This is impossible in the case of God, exalted, because it is impossible that He benefits from the acts of servants. So nothing remains except to say that He, exalted, only commanded them and only forbade them for benefits returning to them. And we have explained that whenever the matter is thus, then it is not evil for God, exalted, to punish them for abandoning the obligatory or for doing the forbidden. So the difference between the two cases has become clear.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on rational judgments of good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqli) in relation to divine punishment. The author examines the possible reasons for God's punishment of a servant and rejects all of them, then contrasts with human punishment in the empirical world (shāhid).

The passage begins by stating that if God punishes a servant for the first reason (that the servant fell short in obtaining a non-necessary benefit for himself), then it leads to a contradiction. Then it discusses the third division: that God punishes to benefit another. This is described as sheer injustice (zulm) because an unjust person harms another to benefit himself, but God is exalted above that. The fourth division is punishment for no reason at all, which is also sheer injustice.

Then the author introduces a comparison with the empirical world: a master who commands his servant and the servant disobeys. It is considered good (hasan) for the master to punish the servant because the master sought his own benefit from the servant's action. Since the servant failed to provide that benefit, the master is justified in punishing him. However, this reasoning does not apply to God because God cannot benefit from the actions of His servants. Therefore, God's commands and prohibitions are only for the benefit of the servants themselves. Since that is the case, it is not evil for God to punish them for abandoning obligations or committing prohibitions. Thus, the difference between the divine case and the human case becomes clear.

The author is arguing against the Mu'tazilite claim that actions are inherently good or evil and that God must act according to those rational judgments. Here, he shows that the analogy between human masters and God is flawed because God's motives are entirely different.

Key Terms
تعذيب (punishment)
ظلم (injustice)
نفع (benefit)
ضرر (harm)
السيد (master)
العبد (servant)
الشاهد (the witnessed world)
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه (rational judgment of good and evil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's punishment is arbitrary because it does not benefit Him or the punished.
Correction: God punishes for the servant's ultimate benefit through divine wisdom, as commands are for their own good.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the master-servant analogy in the empirical world not applicable to God?
A) Because the master can never punish the servant genuinely.
B) Because God cannot benefit from the actions of His servants.
C) Because the servant never disobeys God.
D) Because punishment is always evil.

Key Takeaway
Divine punishment differs from human punishment: human masters punish for personal benefit, while God punishes for the servant's own benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you truly believe that His punishment is for your own benefit, or do you secretly think of Him as a harsh master? What would change in your heart if you deeply internalized that His commands are for your good? Will you start your next act of obedience with that intention?
?
This lesson shows that human masters punish for their own gain, but Allah punishes for yours. Think of a sin you struggle with. How does framing Allah's prohibition as a protection for you, not a restriction, change your desire to avoid it? What concrete step will you take today to act on that belief?

The Fourth Argument: Refuting Rational Evil in God's Punishment
The author presents a conditional argument against rational judgment of God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a conditional argument to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can judge God's actions as good or evil.

Core Idea
Reason cannot deem God's punishment evil because benefit analysis fails.

The Mu'tazilites held that human reason can determine what is good and evil for God. But if that were true, would not God's punishment of disbelievers be evil? The author presents a powerful logical refutation: he examines the possible benefits of punishment and shows none can apply to God. He then contrasts with human masters to clarify the difference. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If rational judgment were valid, then...' Let the author's logical flow sink in.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional and its parts
The argument is a conditional: if A then B. A = rational judgment is valid, B = God's punishment would be deemed evil. The author then proves B is false, so A is false. Find where he states the consequent is false.
The phrase 'وهذا باطل فذاك باطل' marks the falsity of consequent and antecedent.
3
Analyze the three divisions of benefit
The author examines possible beneficiaries of punishment: God, the punished person, or others. He refutes each. List the three and understand why each fails.
This is the core of the proof that punishment would be rationally evil.
4
Contrast with the master-servant analogy
The author then shows that in human affairs, punishment can be good because it relieves the master's grief or reforms the servant. But God is free from such needs. Notice how this contrast highlights the difference.
This rebuts the analogy the Mu'tazilites might use.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself: does this argument reject all use of reason in theology? Or just a specific claim? The commentary clarifies that it only rejects rational judgment independent of revelation.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان حكم العقل معتبراً لقبح من الله تعالى تعذيب الكفار والعصاة وهذا باطل فذاك باطل . بيان الشرطية : إن المنفعة الحاصلة من ذلك التعذيب إما أن ترجع إلى الله أو إلى ذلك العبد المعذب . أو إلى غيره ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . أما الأول : فلان إله العالم متعالي عن النفع والضرر ، والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأن العذاب الخالص الدائم لا يكون نفعاً ، والثالث أيضاً : باطل لأن تعذيب الإنسان لأجل أن يصل بسببه نفع إلى الغير ظلم ، ولأنه ليس تعذيب هذا ، لأجل اتصال النفع إلى الآخرين ، أولى من الضد . فيثبت أن هذا التعذيب قبيح في العقول، بخلاف الشاهد ، فإن السيد إنما يحسن منه تأديب عبده وتعذيبه في بعض الوجوه ، لأن السيد يتأذى بإساءة العبد إليه ، ويبقى حب الانتقام في قلبه ، فإذا انتقم من ذلك العبد ، فإنه يتخلص بسبب ذلك الانتقام من الغم الذي كان حاصلا في قلبه . وأيضاً : فإذا أدب العبد وهذبه ، عاد العبد إلى المواظبة على الأفعال النافعة لذلك السيد . أما الإله تعالى فإنه منزه عن الضرر الحاصل بسبب حب الانتقام ، ومتعالي عن أن ينتفع بحسن آداب العبد ، أو يتضرر بسوء أفعاله . فظهر الفرق . والله أعلم .
The fourth argument: If rational judgment were valid, it would be evil for God to punish the disbelievers and sinners. But this is false, so that is false. Explanation of the conditional: The benefit resulting from that punishment either returns to God, or to that punished servant, or to another. And the three divisions are false. As for the first: because the God of the world is exalted above benefit and harm. The second is also false, because pure perpetual punishment is not a benefit. The third is also false, because punishing a person in order that benefit may reach another through him is injustice, and because this punishment for the sake of connection of benefit to others is not more appropriate than the opposite. So it is established that this punishment is evil in the intellects, unlike the witnessed world, for the master is only deemed good in disciplining his servant and punishing him in some respects, because the master is harmed by the servant's misdeed toward him, and the desire for revenge remains in his heart. When he takes revenge on that servant, he thereby gets rid of the grief that was in his heart. Also, if he disciplines and reforms the servant, the servant returns to consistently performing beneficial acts for that master. As for God, exalted, He is free from the harm caused by the desire for revenge, and exalted above benefiting from the good manners of the servant or being harmed by his evil deeds. So the difference has become clear. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument (ḥujja) that refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that rational judgment (ḥukm al-‘aql) can deem God's actions good or evil. The author presents a conditional: if rational judgment were valid, then it would deem God's punishment of disbelievers and sinners as evil (qabīḥ). But the consequent is false (since punishment is not evil), therefore the antecedent is false (rational judgment is not valid in these matters).

To prove the conditional, the author examines the possible benefits (manfa‘a) of punishment: it must benefit either God, the punished person, or someone else. The first is impossible because God is transcendent (mutā‘ālī) above benefit and harm. The second is impossible because pure perpetual punishment cannot be a benefit (it is harmful). The third is impossible because harming one person to benefit another is injustice (ẓulm), and there is no reason to prefer this person's punishment over another's. Therefore, the punishment cannot have any rational benefit, so by rational standards it would be evil. However, we know God's punishment is not evil, so the premise that rational judgment is valid must be false.

Then the author contrasts with the empirical world (shāhid): a master's punishment of his servant is considered good because the master is harmed by the servant's disobedience and seeks revenge to relieve his own grief, or to reform the servant for his own benefit. But God is free from such emotions and needs, so the analogy fails. Thus, the difference between divine and human justice is established.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة (the fourth argument)
حكم العقل (rational judgment)
قبح (evil/evilness)
تعذيب (punishment)
الكفار والعصاة (disbelievers and sinners)
منفعة (benefit)
ظلم (injustice)
تأديب (discipline)
انتقام (revenge)
غم (grief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth argument rejects all use of reason in theology.
Correction: The argument only rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that reason independently judges God's actions. Reason is still used within revelation's framework.

Checkpoint
What is the structure of the fourth argument?
A) God's punishment benefits the punished person.
B) If rational judgment were valid, God's punishment would be evil; since it is not evil, rational judgment is not valid.
C) God's punishment is always beneficial for others.
D) The master-servant analogy proves God punishes for revenge.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that rational judgment cannot be applied to God's actions, because any benefit analysis fails, and the analogy with human punishment breaks down.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or calamity, do you find yourself questioning God's justice based on your own reasoning? How does this argument challenge that tendency, and what can you do to submit more fully?
?
Have you ever tried to rationalize a hardship by thinking 'this will benefit me in some way'? How does the argument's claim that benefit analysis fails for God's actions change the way you should view such situations?
?
The author contrasts human masters who punish for revenge or reform, but God is free from those. In your own life, do you sometimes project human-like emotions onto God when you feel punished? How should this lesson transform your response to divine trials?

The Story of Al-Jubbai and the Three Sons
A story refuting Mu'tazilite divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a famous story refutes the Mu'tazilite concept of divine justice by exposing a logical inconsistency.

Core Idea
God's justice is not bound by human rational standards.

This lesson presents a well-known argument from Sunni theology against the Mu'tazilite position that God must act according to human notions of justice. The story of al-Jubbai and the three sons dramatizes the contradiction that arises when divine foreknowledge is combined with Mu'tazilite rationalism. Pay close attention to how the questioner's final objection leaves al-Jubbai speechless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: setup, initial verdict, objections, and final silence.
The story is meant to be a logical refutation, not just a narrative.
2
Identify the three sons and their fates
List the three sons and the judgment al-Jubbai gives for each. Note the basis for the child's 'safety'.
Al-Jubbai's initial answers seem plausible within Mu'tazilite thought.
3
Follow the objections step by step
Trace the child's objection, al-Jubbai's response, and then the disbeliever's objection. Understand why the second objection creates a contradiction.
The key is that God's foreknowledge is used inconsistently.
4
Understand why al-Jubbai was silenced
Reflect on why al-Jubbai could not answer the disbeliever's question. What does this show about the Mu'tazilite framework?
The story demonstrates the failure of human rationalism to account for divine decree.
5
Connect to the broader theological lesson
Relate this story to the Ash'arite position that God's actions are not subject to human criteria of justice.
This is a classic argument for the futility of measuring God by human reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قال أهل العلم : نفرض امرأة كان لها ثلاثة أولاد ، أحدهم مات في كبره وكان مؤمناً زاهداً ، والثاني مات في كيره ، وكان كافراً فاسقاً . والثالث : مات في صغره. تسألوا أبا علي الجبائي رئيس المعتزلة عن حالهم . فقال : أما الزاهد ففي أعالي الجنة . وأما الكافر ففي دركات النار وأما الصغير فمن أهل السلامة . فقال السائل : إن أراد ذلك الصبي أن يذهب إلى درجات الجنة ، في الموضع الذي هو موضع أخيه المؤمن الزاهد . هل يمكنه منه ؟ فقال الجبائي : لا . لأنه يقال : إن أخاك إنما وصل إلى تلك الدرجات بسبب زهده وعلمه ، ولم يحصل لك ذلك ، فكيف تصل إليه ؟ فقال السائل : فلو قال ذلك الصبي : إلهي ليس الذنب مني ، لأنك أمتني قبل البلوغ ، بـل كان من الواجب عليك أن تمهلني حتى إذا بلغت أتيت بالطاعات والعبادات الكثيرة مثل ما أتى به الأخ الزاهد . فقال الجبائي : إن الله تعالى يقول : كنت أعلم أنك لو بلغت لكفرت ، ولصرت مستحقاً للنار ، فراعيت مصلحتك وأمتك قبل البلوغ حتى لا تكون من أهل النار . فقال السائل : فلو أن الأخ الكافر يقول : يا إلهي كما علمت من حال ذلك الأخ الصغير أنه لو بلغ الكفر. ولاستحق العقاب فكذلك علمت من حالي أن الأمر كذلك ، فلم راعيت مصلحته ؟ وما راعيت مصلحتي ؟ فلما ذكر السائل هذا السؤال انقطع
The fifth argument: The people of knowledge said: Let us suppose a woman who had three children: one died in his adulthood and was a believing ascetic; the second died in his adulthood and was a disbelieving transgressor; and the third died in his childhood. They asked Abu Ali al-Jubbai, the head of the Mu'tazila, about their states. He said: As for the ascetic, he is in the highest parts of Paradise; as for the disbeliever, he is in the depths of the Fire; and as for the child, he is among the people of safety. The questioner said: If that child wants to go to the ranks of Paradise, to the place where his believing ascetic brother is, can he do so? Al-Jubbai said: No, because it will be said: Your brother only attained those ranks through his asceticism and knowledge, and you did not obtain that, so how can you reach him? The questioner said: So if that child says: 'My God, the fault is not mine, because You caused me to die before puberty. Rather, it was incumbent upon You to give me respite so that when I reached puberty I would perform many acts of obedience and worship like what the ascetic brother did.' Al-Jubbai said: God says: 'I knew that if you reached puberty you would disbelieve and become deserving of the Fire, so I took care of your welfare and caused you to die before puberty so that you would not be among the people of the Fire.' The questioner said: So if the disbelieving brother says: 'O my God, just as You knew from the condition of that child that if he reached puberty he would disbelieve and deserve punishment, likewise You knew from my condition that the case is such, so why did you take care of his welfare and not take care of my welfare?' When the questioner mentioned this question, he [al-Jubbai] was silenced.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-ḥujja al-khāmisah) against the Mu'tazilite position on rational good and evil and divine justice. It is a well-known story (qissa) involving Abu Ali al-Jubbai, the head of the Mu'tazila, and an anonymous questioner.

The scenario: a woman has three sons: a believing ascetic who dies as an adult, a disbelieving transgressor who dies as an adult, and a child who dies before puberty. Al-Jubbai gives the Mu'tazilite verdict: the ascetic is in Paradise, the disbeliever in Hell, and the child is in a state of safety (i.e., neither punished nor rewarded until he would have reached the age of accountability).

The questioner then challenges al-Jubbai with a series of questions. First, if the child asks why he cannot attain the high ranks of his ascetic brother, al-Jubbai replies that he did not earn them. Then the child protests that he died before he could earn them, so God should have given him a longer life. Al-Jubbai responds by invoking God's foreknowledge: God knew that if the child had lived, he would have disbelieved and deserved Hell, so God mercifully caused him to die early to save him from punishment. But then the disbelieving brother objects: God also knew that he would disbelieve, yet He let him live and be punished. Why the difference? Al-Jubbai is unable to answer this contradiction within the Mu'tazilite framework, and the story concludes that he was silenced (inqaṭa‘a).

The argument shows that the Mu'tazilite reliance on human notions of justice and rational judgment leads to contradictions when divine foreknowledge and different fates are considered. The Ash'arite position, which does not require God to act according to human concepts of justice, avoids such problems.

Key Terms
الحجة الخامسة (the fifth argument)
الجبائي (al-Jubbai)
الزاهد (the ascetic)
الكافر (the disbeliever)
الصغير (the child)
السلامة (safety)
البلوغ (puberty)
مصلحة (welfare/benefit)
انقطع (he was silenced)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the story shows that God acts arbitrarily or unjustly, because He saves one person and punishes another based on foreknowledge.
Correction: The story actually refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must act according to human rational standards. It demonstrates that God's justice is not subject to human criteria; He does as He wills, and His actions are not constrained by human notions of fairness.

Checkpoint
Why was al-Jubbai silenced at the end of the story?
A) He could not explain why the child was saved from Hell while the disbeliever was not, despite God's foreknowledge of both.
B) He admitted that the child should go to Paradise.
C) He could not answer the question about the ascetic's reward.
D) The questioner left before he could answer.

Key Takeaway
The story refutes Mu'tazilite divine justice by showing that human rational standards lead to contradictions when applied to God's foreknowledge and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the disbeliever's question—'Why did You take care of his welfare but not mine?'—do you ever feel that God's treatment of you compared to others is unfair? What would you say to God if you were in that position? Commit to trusting His wisdom even when you don't understand.
?
Do you secretly believe that your good deeds earn you a place in Paradise, similar to the ascetic brother? How does this story challenge that assumption? Pause and ask God to purify your intention so that you seek Him alone, not a reward you think you deserve.
?
Imagine you are the child who died before accountability. Are you content that God, knowing your future choices, decided to end your life early? Does that thought disturb you? If so, what does your discomfort reveal about your view of divine justice? Resolve to submit to God's decree as better than your own planning.

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Answers
Razi refutes both Mu'tazilite answers as extremely weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers both Mu'tazilite answers extremely weak and how he uses their failure to argue that human reason does not govern divine actions.

Core Idea
Rational judgments fail to constrain God; His actions transcend our categories.

Razi has just presented two Mu'tazilite attempts to explain why Iblis was allowed to tempt humans. Now he dismantles both with sharp rational arguments. But instead of affirming rational ethics, he uses the very weakness of these answers to show that reason itself is not the measure of God's deeds. This lesson traces his double refutation and its surprising conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation together to get the flow of Razi's argument.
Notice that the passage moves from refuting the first answer to refuting the second, then pivots to a broader principle.
2
Identify the two refutations
Razi attacks each Mu'tazilite answer separately. First, he targets Abu Ali's claim that Iblis's words have no harmful effect; second, he targets Abu Hashim's claim that the benefit of reward outweighs the risk of punishment.
Razi uses empirical observation (the singer example) and a cost–benefit analysis against them.
3
Understand the final twist
After showing that reason would judge it better to remove devils, Razi declares that this rational judgment does not apply to God. The very failure of reason to decide the matter proves that reason is 'isolated' (ma'zul) regarding divine actions.
This is a key Ash'ari move: reason can reason, but God is not bound by its conclusions.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If human reason cannot evaluate God's actions, where do we get our knowledge of good and evil? The Ash'ari answer is revelation, not independent reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذين الجوابين في غاية الضعف . أما الأول : فلانا نعلم بالضرورة أن من جلس مغنياً وواظب على وصف بعض الأشياء بالحسن واللذة والراحة فلقول تأثير شديد في حصول الرغبة في ذلك الشيء . وأما الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً . لأن الفائدة في حصول هؤلاء الشياطين : صعوبة الأمر . وتأثير تلك الصعوبة في مزيد الثواب . على قدر الطاعة ، وفي حصول العقاب الشديد ، على قدر المعصية. وتلك الزيادات من الثواب والعقاب ليست في محل الحاجة والاحتراز عن العقاب الشديد في محل الحاجة العظيمة ، وإذا كان كذلك كان تركه أولى . هذا هو الحكم الذي يحكم به صريح العقل ، مع أنه غير معتبر في حق الله تعالى . وذلك يدل على أن العقل معزول في حق أفعال الله ، وفي حق أحكامه
Know that these two answers are extremely weak. As for the first: we know by necessity that one who sits as a singer and persists in describing some things as good, pleasurable, and comfortable—speech has a strong effect in producing desire for that thing. As for the second: it is also weak, because the benefit in the existence of these devils is the difficulty of the matter, and the effect of that difficulty in increased reward commensurate with obedience, and in the occurrence of severe punishment commensurate with disobedience. And those increases in reward and punishment are not in a position of need, while avoidance of severe punishment is in a position of great need. Since this is the case, it would be more appropriate to avoid it. This is the judgment that pure reason would judge, although it is not considered with respect to God, exalted. And that indicates that reason is set aside with respect to God's actions and with respect to His judgments.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage refutes both Mu'tazilite answers. Against Abu Ali's answer, Razi argues that words have a strong influence on desire, as seen when a singer describes something as good—so Iblis's whispering does cause extra harm. Against Abu Hashim's answer, Razi points out that while increased reward is good, the accompanying severe punishment for sin is a great harm, and avoiding that harm is more important. Pure reason would thus judge that it is better not to empower devils. However, Razi concludes that such rational judgments are not applicable to God, showing that reason is isolated from judgments about God's actions. This reinforces the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not subject to human rational evaluation.

Key Terms
ضعف
مغنى
رغبة
ثواب
عقاب
عقل معزول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is denying that reason can know good and evil in any sense.
Correction: Razi is not denying that reason can know good and evil in general; he is denying that God is bound by such judgments.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute Abu Ali al-Jubba'i's answer?
A) By citing scripture
B) By arguing that speech influences desire
C) By saying Iblis is more powerful
D) By claiming the answer is illogical

Key Takeaway
Razi uses rational argument to undermine the Mu'tazilite defenses, then concludes that reason itself does not govern God's actions—a core Ash'ari tenet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty that seems pointless or unjust, do you catch yourself trying to calculate whether it's 'worth it' in terms of reward? How does Razi's point that God's judgments transcend our rational calculations challenge your instinct to demand fairness?
?
The singer analogy shows that words powerfully shape desire. Think of a time you repeated something positive or negative to yourself or others. Did you recognize the weight of your own words in steering your desires toward or away from obedience?
?
Razi says that reason would judge it better to avoid devils, yet God allowed them. Is there a situation in your life where you have insisted that your rational judgment about what is best must align with God's decree? What would it mean to truly accept that God's actions are not bound by your sense of appropriateness?

The Seventh Proof: Sinful Causes Outweigh Righteous Ones
Razi enumerates nineteen powers inclining to sin as proof against rational theodicy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's seventh proof that the overwhelming inclination toward sin in human nature challenges the validity of rational moral judgments about God's actions.

Core Idea
The nineteen forces within us make sin inevitable, yet God created them—so rational judgment does not apply to God.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the seventh of his proofs that rational judgments about good and evil cannot be applied to God. He argues that human beings are created with a preponderance of inclinations toward sin over obedience. If we were to judge this by human reason, we would call it unjust. Yet God does it, showing that such rational criteria are invalid for divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Razi begins by stating that the states leading to sin are more than those leading to obedience, and that this would be considered evil by rational standards.
The claim is not that sin is inevitable, but that the factors inciting sin outnumber those inciting obedience.
2
Follow the enumeration of the nineteen powers
Razi lists five external senses, five internal senses, lust and anger, and seven natural powers—totaling nineteen. Trace each category and see how they all call the soul toward bodily pleasures.
The verse 'Over it are nineteen' (Quran 74:30) is cited as a subtle reference to these forces.
3
Reflect on the implication
If a human judge were to create beings with such a strong pull toward sin, we would call that judge unjust. But God does exactly this, and since we know God is just, it follows that rational judgments about good and evil do not apply to God's actions.
This is a key move in Razi's critique of rationalist ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : أن نقول : الأحوال الحاملة على المعصية أكثر من الأحوال الحاملة على الطاعة ، وذلك قبيح في العقول . بيان الأول : أن الحواس الخمسة الظاهرة كل واحد منها يدعو إلى طلب اللذات الجسمانية والحواس الخمسة الباطنة أيضاً . كذلك لأن الإنسان كلما تخيل صورة حسنة مال طبعه إليها ، وكلما تنكر في حالة طيبة لذيذة من الأحوال الشهوانية مال طبعه إليها ، وكلما تذكر حالة سيئة صار أيضاً كذلك ، فيثبت أن هذه الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة تدعو الإنسان إلى طلب اللذات الجسمانية . وأيضاً : الشهوة والغضب يحملان الإنسان على ذلك. وأيضاً : القوى الطبيعية السبعة تحمل الإنسان على ذلك ، فهذه تسعة عشر نوعاً من أنواع القوى الجسمانية مركوزة في هذا البدن ، وكل واحد منها يدعو النفس إلى طلب اللذات الجسمانية ، كما قال تعالى : ( عليها تسعة عشر )
The seventh proof: We say: the states that lead to sin are more than the states that lead to obedience, and that is evil in the intellects. Explanation of the first: the five external senses, each one of them calls to seeking bodily pleasures. And the five internal senses also are like that, because whenever a person imagines a beautiful image, his nature inclines to it, and whenever he [؟] in a good, delicious state of the lustful states, his nature inclines to it, and whenever he remembers a bad state, he also becomes like that. Thus it is established that these external and internal senses call a person to seeking bodily pleasures. Also: lust and anger drive a person to that. Also: the seven natural powers drive a person to that. So these are nineteen types of physical powers embedded in this body, and each one of them calls the soul to seeking bodily pleasures, as He, exalted, said: "Over it are nineteen".
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof. It argues that the factors inciting sin outnumber those inciting obedience, and this would be considered evil by rational standards. The proof enumerates the sources of inclination toward bodily pleasures: five external senses, five internal senses (imagination, etc.), the two faculties of lust and anger, and seven natural powers (like growth, digestion, etc.), totaling nineteen. All these pull the soul toward sin. The verse 'Over it are nineteen' (Quran 74:30) is cited as a subtle reference to the idea that these forces are numerous. The implication is that since God created humans with such strong inclinations to sin, a rational evaluation would deem this unjust or evil. But God does so, demonstrating that rational judgments do not apply to His actions.

Key Terms
أحوال حاملة
معصية
طاعة
قبيح
حواس
قوى طبيعية
تسعة عشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is a deterministic argument that humans are forced to sin and therefore not responsible.
Correction: Razi is not arguing for determinism; rather, he is showing that the natural predisposition toward sin is so strong that if we applied human rational standards, we would call God unjust. The point is to demonstrate the inadequacy of rational ethics for divine actions.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that the natural human inclination toward sin is so overwhelming that rational judgments about good and evil cannot be applied to God's actions.

?

The Argument from Bodily Desires vs. Reason
Argument from the prevalence of bodily desires over reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prevalence of bodily desires over reason leads to the conclusion that rational judgment cannot be applied to God's actions and commands.

Core Idea
Reason cannot judge God's actions because desire outweighs reason in human nature.

This passage presents a psychological and social argument against using human reason to evaluate God's commands. By examining the development of the human soul and the widespread inclination toward worldly pleasures, the author shows that the causes for sin far outweigh the causes for obedience. If reason were the measure, God's command to seek the hereafter would seem unfair; therefore, reason must be set aside in matters of divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two powers
Notice the contrast between the bodily powers (like sight) that act from childhood and the rational power that arises only after puberty. Consider how frequent actions create rooted dispositions.
The bodily powers are described as having infinite types of whispering.
3
Consider the social factor
The author points out that most people are inclined toward corporeal things, which reinforces the natural inclination. This shows the imbalance is not just individual but universal.
4
Understand the conclusion
God's command to seek the hereafter despite this imbalance would be considered 'ugly' by rational standards, but since God commanded it, reason's judgment is invalid. See how this argument parallels the previous one about jinn devils.
The argument is similar to the previous one but focuses on internal character traits.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كل واحد منها ليس يعمل عملاً واحداً بل يأتي بأنواع لا نهاية لها من الوسوسة والدعاء ، فإن القوة الباصرة ، كلما أبصرت شيئاً من الجسمانيات دعت النفس إلى طلبه ، وكذا القول في سائر القوى . وأيضاً : النفس في أول الخلقة إلى كمال البلوغ إنما تفعل وتتحرك بمقتضى هذه القوة الجسمانية، ولا شك أن كثرة الأفعال توجب الملكات الراسخة . وأما القوة العاقلة الناهية عن طلب الجسمانيات الأمرة بطلب الباقيات الصالحات ، فهي إنما تحدث بعد سن البلوغ. فهي في الحقيقة إنما تقوى بعد استيلاء أولئك التسعة عشر على مملكة البدن واىضاً : فأكثر أهل العالم راغبون في طلب الجسمانيات ، معرضون عن طلب الروحانيات ، والطبع يقوى ميله بسبب كثرة الأعوان ، ويضعف ميله بسبب قلة الأعوان ، فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا : أن الأسباب الداعية إلى المعصية واللذة والفسق والفجور كثيرة قوية ، والأسباب الداعية إلى الطاعات ، والإعراض عن الدنيا والإقبال على الروحانيات قليلة، ثم من هذه الحالة نهى تعالى عن الميل إلى الدنيا ، وأمر بطلب الآخرة ، والأسباب الموضوعية أسباب توجب أضداد هذه الأحوال ، فلو كان حكم العقل في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه معتبراً ، لكان هذا التكليف قبيحاً ، وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أن حكم (۱) العقل في أفعال الله ، وفي أحكامه معزول . والله أعلم • وهذا الدليل قريب من الدليل الذي قبله ، إلا أن ذلك في شياطين الجن ، وهذا في شياطين الأخلاق المذمومة .
Then each one of them does not perform a single action, but rather comes with infinite types of whispering and calling. For the visual power, whenever it sees something of corporeal things, it calls the soul to seek it; and similarly for the rest of the powers. Also: the soul, from the beginning of creation until the completion of puberty, acts and moves only in accordance with this corporeal power, and there is no doubt that frequency of actions produces firmly rooted dispositions. As for the rational power which forbids seeking corporeal things and commands seeking the good things that endure, it only arises after the age of puberty. In reality, it only strengthens after the domination of those nineteen over the kingdom of the body. Also: most of the people of the world are desirous of seeking corporeal things, turning away from seeking spiritual things; and the natural inclination increases in strength due to the abundance of supporters, and weakens due to the scarcity of supporters. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned: that the causes calling to disobedience, pleasure, immorality, and licentiousness are many and strong, while the causes calling to acts of obedience, turning away from the world, and turning toward spiritual things are few. Then, in this state, He, exalted, forbade inclining toward the world and commanded seeking the hereafter, while the objective causes are causes that necessitate the opposites of these states. So if the judgment of reason regarding God's actions and His commands were considered, this obligation would be ugly. And since the matter is not so, we know that the judgment of reason regarding God's actions and His commands is invalid. And God knows best. • This argument is close to the argument before it, except that that one was about the devils of the jinn, while this one is about the devils of blameworthy character traits.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the validity of rational judgment regarding God's actions. It begins by describing the multitude of internal and external factors that lead humans toward sin and worldly desires. The author explains that the bodily powers (like sight) constantly incite the soul to seek corporeal things, and from early childhood until puberty, these powers dominate. The rational power that commands good and forbids evil only develops later and is weak because by then the bodily powers have already taken control. Additionally, most people are inclined toward worldly pleasures, and this social constant reinforces the natural inclination. Thus, the causes leading to disobedience are far more numerous and stronger than those leading to obedience. God then commands us to seek the hereafter despite this imbalance. If reason were the judge of God's actions, it would deem this command unfair or ugly. Since God has given this command, we know that reason's judgment is not applicable to God's actions. The author notes this argument is similar to a previous one about jinn devils, but this one concerns internal bad character traits.

The Eighth Proof: God's Actions Cannot Be for a Purpose
Disjunctive proof that God's actions cannot be for a purpose, with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's actions cannot be for a purpose because that would imply need or equality, and why the objection based on benefit to others does not refute it.

Core Idea
God's actions cannot be driven by a purpose outside Himself without implying imperfection.

This passage presents the eighth proof from a classical kalam work, demonstrating through a rigorous disjunctive syllogism that attributing purposes to God's actions leads to contradictions. It then addresses a potential objection that God may act for the benefit of others. Understanding this proof clarifies the Islamic doctrine of divine self-sufficiency and the nature of God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument structure come through.
Notice the disjunctive structure: 'Either... or...'.
2
Identify the two branches of the disjunctive
The proof presents two possibilities regarding whether attaining a purpose is more preponderant for God or not. Examine each branch and its consequence.
Each branch leads to an impossibility: need for completion or contradiction.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
The objector says God acts for benefit of servants. See how the author refutes this by applying the same disjunctive reasoning to God's perspective.
The reply shows the objection either fails due to equality or returns to the original problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : أن نقول الفعل الذي يفعله الله تعالى لغرض من الأغراض. إما أن يكون حصول ذلك الغرض أولى لـه من لا حصوله . أو لا تحصل هذه الأولوية ، فإن كان الأول لزم أن يكون إله العالم باقتضاء لذاته ، مستكملاً بغيره ، وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني . فالاستواء يناقض الرجحان . فالقول بأن الفعل والترك بالنسبة إليه على السواء يمنع من كونه فاعلاً له ، فإن قالوا : إن ذلك الفعل ، وإن كان وجوده وعدمه يتساويان بالنسبة إليه ، إلا أنه أنفع للعباد ، فلأجل أنه أنفع بالنسبة إلى العباد ، يدخله الله في الـوجـود فنقول : كونه أنفع للعباد مع كونه ليس أنفع لهم ، إما أن يكون بالنسبة إلى الله على التساوي ، أو يكون أحد الجانبين أرجح. فإن كان على التساوي امتنع الفعل ، وإن كان أحد الطرفين أرجح ، عاد حديث كونه ناقصـاً لـذاتـه ، مستكملا بغيره .
The eighth proof: We say that an action that God, exalted, does for a purpose. Either the attainment of that purpose is more preponderant for Him than its non-attainment, or this preponderance does not obtain. If the first, then it would necessitate that the Lord of the world is needful in His essence and perfecting Himself through another, which is impossible. If the second, then equality contradicts preponderance. So the statement that action and non-action are equal with respect to Him prevents Him from being an agent of it. If they say: That action, even though its existence and non-existence are equal with respect to Him, yet it is more beneficial for the servants; so because it is more beneficial with respect to the servants, God brings it into existence. We say: Its being more beneficial for the servants while it is not more beneficial for them [i.e., from God's perspective] — either it is equal with respect to God, or one side is more preponderant. If it is equal, then the action is impossible. If one side is more preponderant, then the discussion of Him being deficient in His essence and perfecting Himself through another returns.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof against the idea that God acts for a purpose. The proof is a disjunctive syllogism. If God acts for a purpose, then either attaining that purpose is better for Him or not. If it is better, that implies He is in need of something external, which contradicts His self-sufficiency. If it is not better, then there is no reason for action, and the action would be arbitrary. The objector may argue that the purpose is benefit for others, not for God. The author replies that if the benefit for others is equal with respect to God (i.e., it doesn't affect Him), then there is no preponderance, and the action still cannot be explained. If it is more preponderant (i.e., from God's perspective He prefers it for others), then again He is moved by something external, implying deficiency. So both alternatives lead to contradiction.

Key Terms
الغرض
الاستواء
الرجحان
النقص
الاستكمال بالغير
الحسن والقبح العقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions being for a purpose means they have wisdom and are beneficial.
Correction: The proof distinguishes between purpose as need (which implies deficiency) and wisdom as an attribute. God's actions have wisdom, but they are not caused by a need for an external purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what is the consequence if God acts for a purpose and attaining that purpose is more preponderant for Him?
A) God would be perfecting Himself through another, implying need.
B) The action would be arbitrary.
C) The action would benefit others.
D) God would be all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not motivated by external purposes because that would undermine His absolute self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does this humble you to accept that Allah's wisdom is beyond your comprehension? Are you willing to submit to His will without demanding a reason?

The Ninth Proof: Purpose's Eternity or Temporality Leads to Impossibility
Proves that if God's action had a purpose, that purpose would either be eternal leading to eternal act, or temporal leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's actions cannot be motivated by a temporal purpose without leading to an infinite regress, and why the purpose cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
A purpose for God's action leads to impossibility—either it's eternal or causes regress.

This proof is a concise logical argument that examines two possibilities for the purpose behind God's action. It shows that both lead to contradictions, thereby demonstrating that God's actions are not subject to purposes in the way human actions are.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the proof is structured as a conditional (if... then) with two branches.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The passage presents two possibilities for the purpose: eternal or temporal. For each, it deduces a consequence. Identify these two branches and their conclusions.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Both branches lead to impossibility. Understand why each consequence is impossible: an eternal action contradicts temporality, and regress is logically impossible.
This shows that the initial assumption (that God acts for a purpose) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنه تعالى لو فعل فعلاً لغرض ، لكان ذلك الغرض ، إن كان قديماً لزم من قدمه قدم الفعل ، وإن كان حادثاً كان إحداثه لغرض آخر ، ولزم التسلسل وأنه محال .
The ninth proof: If He, exalted, did an action for a purpose, then that purpose: if it is eternal, then from its eternity would follow the eternity of the action; and if it is temporal, then its bringing into existence would require another purpose, and infinite regress would follow, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This is a concise proof against God acting for a purpose. The proof hinges on the nature of the purpose. If the purpose is eternal, then the action must also be eternal, which would mean the action is not temporally originated, but many actions are temporal. If the purpose is temporal (originated in time), then it itself requires a purpose, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, God's actions are not motivated by a purpose in the ordinary sense.

Key Terms
الغرض
القديم
الحادث
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that this proof means God acts without wisdom or purpose altogether.
Correction: The argument does not say God acts without wisdom; it says the purpose cannot be a temporal cause like in humans. God's actions are wise but not motivated by a purpose that is external or temporal.

Checkpoint
Why does the ninth proof reject the idea that God's action has a temporal purpose?
A) Because temporal purposes are always beneficial.
B) Because a temporal purpose would itself need another purpose, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because temporal purposes are evil.
D) Because God does not care about purposes.

Key Takeaway
God's action cannot have a temporal or eternal purpose without leading to contradiction, so His actions are not motivated by purposes as we understand them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own actions, do you often find yourself needing a purpose to justify them? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that everything, including God's actions, must have a purpose? Will you let go of trying to fully comprehend God's wisdom and submit to His absolute freedom?
?
This proof shows that a purpose for God leads to contradiction. Do you ever feel uncomfortable with the idea that God's actions are not explainable by human reasoning? How does that discomfort reveal a need to trust in His perfection? What will you do to cultivate that trust?
?
Reflect on a time you insisted on finding a reason for a divine decree. How might the lesson's argument help you accept that God's actions are not bound by purposes we understand? Are you willing to stop measuring God's actions by human standards?

The Tenth Proof: Human Actions Are Compulsory
Proves that human actions are compulsory or accidental, invalidating rational ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument reduces all human actions to compulsory or accidental, thereby undermining the foundation of rational ethics.

Core Idea
If every action is compulsory, moral responsibility based on free choice collapses.

The tenth proof confronts the idea that humans can independently choose between good and evil. Through a rigorous analysis of causal necessity, it argues that all actions are either determined by a preponderating factor or happen accidentally, leaving no room for libertarian free will. This passage focuses on the first branch: compulsory actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument uses a conditional structure.
2
Follow the logical case analysis
Identify the two main cases: power determined to one action vs. power capable of both. Within the latter, trace the sub-argument about preponderating factors.
The author concludes that in both sub-cases, the action is compulsory.
3
Reflect on the implications for moral judgments
Consider how the argument undermines the idea of rational good and evil. If actions are compulsory, can they be morally evaluated?
This connects to the broader debate on free will and moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية ، وإما اتفاقية . وعلى التقديرين فالقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين باطل . بيان المقام الأول : أن القدرة الصالحة للحركة إن كانت صالحة للسكون فعند حصول القدرة على الحركة ، كانت الحركة اضطرارية ، وإن كانت صالحة للضدين ، فرجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر إما أن يتوقف على مرجح أو لا يتوقف على مرجح ( فإن توقف على مرجع )(١) فعند حصول ذلك المرجح يجب حصول ذلك الطرف الأول ، لأن عند حصول ما يرجح ذلك الطرف لم يكن مرجح الطرف الثاني موجوداً ، وإذا لم يوجد مرجحه امتنع حصول الطرف الثاني . وإلا يحصل من غير مرجح . ونحن في هذا القسم نعتقد أن حصول الرجحان بدون المرجح محال . وإذا امتنع حصول الطرف الثاني ، وجب حصول الطرف الأول ، ضرورة أن الخروج عن طرفي النقيض محال ، فيثبت أن على هذا التقدير يكون الفعل أيضاً اضطرارياً .
The tenth proof: Human actions are either compulsory or accidental. On both assumptions, the claim of rational good and evil is false. Explanation of the first position: If the power that is capable of motion is capable of rest, then when the power for motion occurs, the motion is compulsory. If it is capable of both opposites, then the preponderance of one side over the other either depends on a preponderating factor or does not depend on a preponderating factor. If it depends on a preponderating factor, then when that preponderating factor occurs, that first side must occur, because when what preponderates that side is present, the preponderating factor of the second side is not present; and if its preponderating factor is not present, the occurrence of the second side is impossible. Otherwise, it would occur without a preponderating factor. And we in this section believe that the occurrence of preponderance without a preponderating factor is impossible. And if the occurrence of the second side is impossible, then the occurrence of the first side is necessitated, of necessity because exiting from the two contradictory sides is impossible. So it is established that on this assumption, the action is also compulsory.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage begins the tenth proof against rational good and evil by arguing that all human actions reduce to being compulsory or accidental. It focuses on the first case (compulsory). The argument examines two possibilities concerning the nature of human power: either the power is determined to one action (e.g., it can only move, not rest) - then the action is compulsory; or it is capable of both opposites. If it is capable of both, then for one side to occur a preponderating factor is needed. If that factor is present, the action must occur, and the opposite cannot occur, making the action compulsory. If no preponderating factor is needed, that would mean an effect without a cause, which is impossible. Thus in all cases, the action is compulsory, and therefore moral evaluation based on free choice is undermined.

Key Terms
الاضطرارية
الاتفاقية
القدرة الصالحة للحركة والسكون
المرجح
طرفي النقيض
الحسن والقبح العقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies human agency entirely, making humans mere puppets.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans are agents; it denies that they have absolute freedom independent of causes. Agency remains but is causally determined.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what happens if the power is capable of both motion and rest and a preponderating factor for motion occurs?
A) The agent freely chooses motion.
B) Motion occurs necessarily.
C) Rest occurs instead.
D) The action becomes accidental.

Key Takeaway
All human actions are reduced to either compulsory or accidental, leaving no basis for rational moral judgments of good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a significant moral choice you made recently. According to the logic of this proof, was that choice truly free or was it determined by factors beyond your control? How does that challenge your sense of personal accountability before Allah? Will you now rely more on revelation to judge right and wrong, knowing that reason alone may be insufficient?
?
The author argues that if an action depends on a preponderating factor, then it is compulsory. Consider your own daily actions—prayer, charity, avoiding sin. Can you identify any preponderating factors (e.g., environment, desires, knowledge) that push you toward or away from obedience? How does this affect your view of your own efforts to change? Are you content with the causes Allah has placed in your life, or do you feel a need to resist them?
?
This proof ultimately denies the basis of rational ethics. If moral judgments cannot be derived from reason, what does that mean for the way you decide what is right and wrong? Does it make you more dependent on divine guidance, or does it leave you feeling lost? How will you ensure your actions align with Allah's pleasure if your own reasoning is not a reliable guide?

Proof Twelve: Moral Obligation Cannot Apply to God
Argument that obligation implies blame, impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational concept of obligation cannot be attributed to God, based on the analysis of blame.

Core Idea
Obligation implies blame; blame implies imperfection; God is perfect.

This lesson presents a key argument from rational theology: the concept of moral obligation (wujub) is tied to blame (dhamm), and blame involves psychological harm. Since God is perfect and self-sufficient, He cannot be subject to such blame, and thus obligation cannot apply to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the condensed style of the proof.
2
Understand the definition of obligation
The passage defines obligation as an act whose abandoner deserves blame. This is a rational definition, not a legal one.
3
Analyze the definition of blame
Blame is described as causing pain, aversion, and disruption of interests. These are psychological states.
4
Apply the reasoning to God
Since blame involves imperfection, it cannot apply to God. Therefore, obligation (which entails blame) also cannot apply to God.
This argument protects God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : المعقول من الوجوب ، كون الفعل بحال يستحق تاركه الذم على بعض الوجوه ، وحصول هذه اللازمة في حقى الله تعالى محال لأن المعقول من الذم ( أنه عن ( قول ، أو فعل ، أو ترك قول ، أو ترك فعل ، يلزم منه تألم قلب الإنسان ، ونفرة طبعه ، واختلال مصالحه . وكل ذلك في حق الله : محال فكان كونه مستحقاً للذم : محالاً. فيلزم ( أن يكون تحقق معنى الوجود في حقه : محالا ) .
The twelfth proof: The rationally understood meaning of obligation (wujub) is that the act is such that its abandoner deserves blame (dhamm) in some respects. And the occurrence of this necessary consequence in the case of God, exalted, is impossible, because the rationally understood meaning of blame is that it is from a word, or an act, or omission of a word, or omission of an act, which entails pain of the human heart, aversion of his nature, and disruption of his interests. All of that is impossible in the case of God. Thus, His being deserving of blame is impossible. Consequently, the realization of the meaning of obligation in His case is impossible.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof (al-hujja al-thaniya 'ashara). The author defines obligation (wujub) in rational terms: it means that if one fails to perform the obligatory act, one deserves blame (dhamm). This blame is a consequence that cannot apply to God. The author analyzes blame as involving psychological harm (pain of heart, aversion, disruption of interests), which are all impossible for God, who is perfect and self-sufficient. Therefore, God cannot be deserving of blame. And since obligation implies potential blame for non-compliance, obligation cannot be predicated of God. Thus, the very meaning of obligation (wujub) cannot apply to God.

Key Terms
وجوب
ذم
المعقول
تألم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that God commands obligations upon humans.
Correction: The proof only denies that God Himself is subject to moral obligation; it does not deny that He imposes obligations on His creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the twelfth proof, why is obligation impossible for God?
A) Because God cannot perform actions.
B) Because blame entails psychological harm, impossible for God.
C) Because God is above all laws.
D) Because obligation only applies to angels.

Key Takeaway
God's perfection precludes the possibility of moral obligation, because obligation entails blame and blame entails imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When life disappoints you, do you ever find a subtle blame toward God rising in your heart? How does this lesson's definition of blame as psychological harm—and its impossibility for God—expose the error in that feeling? What concrete step will you take to redirect that feeling to trust in His perfection?
?
The lesson says obligation entails blame upon omission. You are obligated to pray, fast, and give charity. Do you fulfill these duties primarily to avoid blame (from others, from your own conscience, or from God) or out of love and recognition of God's perfection? Be honest: which motive dominates? How can you shift your intention?
?
Blame is defined as causing pain, aversion, and disruption. Think of a person you have blamed recently—perhaps for a small mistake. Did your blame inflict psychological harm on them? In light of this lesson, how will you change your response when someone fails your expectations?

The True Meanings of Blame and Praise: Why They Cannot Apply to God
Al-Razi gives the first of two answers to the objection that God could be blamed in another sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that blame and praise are intrinsically linked to human emotions (grief and joy), and therefore cannot be attributed to God in any sense.

Core Idea
Blame and praise are defined by feelings, not abstract concepts; God cannot feel, so they cannot apply.

Al-Razi confronts a subtle objection: perhaps 'blame' can be redefined in a way that applies to God? He responds by first clarifying what blame and praise really mean—rooted in human emotional experience. This passage lays out his first answer, showing why any alternative meaning is impossible to conceive, and therefore cannot be used to describe God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Al-Razi's setup
Begin by reading the objector's challenge: 'Why not say God deserves blame in another sense?' Notice how Al-Razi frames his answer as two aspects, and that we are only seeing the first.
Al-Razi's 'first aspect' focuses on the very definition of blame and praise.
2
Identify the core definitions
Al-Razi states that blame is understood as that which causes grief, and praise as that which causes joy. Mark these definitions in the Arabic text (الغم والسرور).
3
Trace the argument about inconceivability
Follow Al-Razi's logic: if blame and praise have no known meaning beyond these feelings, then any other meaning is inconceivable (غير متصور). Without conception (تصور), no judgment (تصديق) can be made. So the burden is on the opponent to show another meaning exists.
This is a key move in Islamic epistemology: affirmation depends on conception.
4
Observe the psychological grounding
Al-Razi reinforces that our desire for praise and aversion to blame are solely due to their emotional effects. If those effects are removed, no effect remains. This ties the concepts entirely to feelings.
He argues this is confirmed by thorough reflection.
5
Conclude the impossibility for God
Since God cannot experience grief or joy, these definitions cannot apply to Him. Therefore, the very nature (ماهية) of good and evil (حسن وقبح) is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى يستحق الذم بمعنى آخر ؟ فنقول : الجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إن الشروع في بيان أن الله تعالى هل يعقل أن يستحق الذم أم لا ؟ فرع على المفهوم من الذم والمدح والمعقول عندنا من الذم : أمر يوجب حصول الغم في القلب ، ومن المدح : أمر يوجب حصول السرور في القلب . فأما معنى ما وراء ذلك فهو غير متصور عندنا . وإذا كان التصور مفقوداً ، كان التصديق به ممتنعاً ، فعلى القوم أن يثبتوا للمدح والذم أثراً وراء ما ذكرناه ، حتى ننظر : أنه هل يمكن إثباته في حق الله تعالى ؟ واعلم أن إثبات أمر آخر وراء ما ذكرناه كالميؤس منه ، فإنا لا نرغب في المدح إلا لأنه يورث الفرح والسرور واللذة . والذم لا ينفر طباعنا عنه إلا لأنه يورث الغم والحزن والألم في القلب . ولو رفعنا هذه الآثار من المخاطر لم نجد للمدح والذم أثراً أصلا . والفكر والتأمل التام يكشف عن حقيقة ما ذكرناه كشفا لا يرتاب العاقل فيه ، وإذا كان حاصل المدح والذم يرجع إلى هذه المعاني ، وثبت أن هذه المعاني يمتنع حصولها في حق الله تعالى ، ظهر حينئذ : أن ماهية الحسن والقبح يمتنع تحققها في حق الله تعالى .
If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, deserves blame in another sense? We say: The answer to it is from two aspects: The first: that embarking on the explanation of whether God, exalted, can be conceived as deserving blame or not is a branch of the conception of blame and praise. What is understood by us of blame is a thing that necessitates the occurrence of grief in the heart, and of praise is a thing that necessitates the occurrence of joy in the heart. As for a meaning beyond that, it is inconceivable for us. And if conception is missing, then regarding it as true is impossible. So it is upon the people to prove for praise and blame an effect beyond what we mentioned, so that we may consider: whether it can be established regarding God, exalted? And know that establishing another thing beyond what we mentioned is nearly despaired of; for we do not desire praise except because it produces joy, happiness, and pleasure. And blame does not repel our natures except because it produces grief, sorrow, and pain in the heart. And if we remove these effects from consideration, we find no effect for praise and blame at all. And thorough reflection and meditation reveals the truth of what we mentioned with a revelation that no rational person doubts. And if the sum of praise and blame reduces to these meanings, and it is established that these meanings are impossible to occur regarding God, exalted, then it becomes clear that the essence (mahiyya) of good and evil (husn wa qubh) is impossible to be realized regarding God, exalted.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors ask: Why can we not say that God deserves blame in a different sense of the word 'blame'? Al-Razi gives the first of two answers. He begins by noting that any discussion about whether God can be blamed depends on what we mean by 'blame' and 'praise'. As we normally understand them, blame is something that causes grief in the heart, and praise is something that causes joy in the heart. Any other meaning is inconceivable for us. If the conception is impossible, then affirming the judgment is impossible. So the opponent must first establish a further meaning for praise and blame beyond these psychological effects, and then show that such a meaning can apply to God. Al-Razi then argues that it is nearly impossible to establish another meaning, because our desire for praise is only due to the joy it brings, and our aversion to blame is only due to the pain it causes. If you remove these effects, we find no other effect for praise and blame at all. This is confirmed by thorough reflection. Since the reality of praise and blame reduces to these feelings, and these feelings are impossible for God (as He cannot feel grief or joy), it follows that the essence of good and evil cannot apply to God.

Key Terms
الذم
المدح
الغم
السرور
ماهية الحسن والقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'blame' can have a purely conceptual meaning detached from emotional effects, allowing it to be applied to God without anthropomorphism.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that blame is essentially tied to causing grief; any other meaning is inconceivable and unprovable. Therefore, no sense of blame can apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, why is it impossible to conceive of blame in a way that is not tied to psychological effects?
A) Because blame is an abstract concept that transcends feelings.
B) Because our only understanding of blame is as something that causes grief, and any other meaning is inconceivable.
C) Because God is beyond all human language.
D) Because the Quran forbids attributing blame to God.

Key Takeaway
The very meanings of praise and blame are rooted in emotional experiences that are impossible for God, so it is rationally impossible for God to be subject to moral evaluation in any sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek praise from others for your good deeds, how much of that desire is really about the joy it brings you? Does this lesson expose a subtle form of self-worship that you need to repent from? Commit to one action this week that you will do purely for God, without telling anyone.
?
Think of a time when criticism or blame caused you deep grief. Did that grief reveal that your heart was attached to people's opinions rather than God's pleasure? How can you train yourself to care only about God's assessment, even when it costs you human approval?
?
Al-Razi argues that praise and blame lose all meaning apart from their emotional effects. If you stripped away every ounce of emotional reward or punishment from your religious practice, would your faith remain? What does your answer reveal about the sincerity of your worship?
