Negative Conditions Cannot Ground Blame
Al-Razi argues against negative conditions as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative conditions cannot serve as part of the cause for a positive judgment like deserving blame.

Core Idea
A negative condition cannot explain a positive effect.

This lesson unpacks one of al-Razi's most concise arguments against the rationalist claim that acts are inherently good or evil. He focuses on the concept of 'injustice' and shows that its definition relies on two negative conditions—things that are absent. If these absences were part of the cause for blame, a non-existence would produce an existence, which is impossible in causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp al-Razi's terse style. Note the two negative conditions he mentions.
The phrases 'غير مسبوق بحياته' and 'غير ملحوق بعوض جائر' might need clarification: they mean 'not preceded by his life' and 'not followed by just compensation'.
2
Identify the two negative conditions
Find the two conditions that define injustice: (1) the act is not preceded by the agent's life (i.e., not a perpetual action), and (2) it is not followed by just compensation. Both are absences.
3
Understand the logical implication
Al-Razi argues that if these negative conditions were part of the cause for deserving blame (a positive judgment), then a non-existent thing would be the cause of something existent. This is absurd, so the rationalist claim fails.
The proof assumes that blameworthiness must have an efficient cause, and a negative cannot be an efficient cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر : في إبطال القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه : أن نقول : استحقاق الذم حكم ثابت ، وكون الفعل ظلماً يعتبر فيه قيدان عدميان ، وهما : كونه غير مسبوق بحياته ، وكونه غير ملحوق بعوض جائر ، فلو كان كونه ظلماً يوجب استحقاق الذم ، لكان القيد العدمي جزءاً من علة الحكم الوجودي وهو برهان
The thirteenth proof: In refuting the claim of the rational determination of good and evil (tahsin al-'aql wa taqbiheh): We say: Deserving blame is an established judgment, and the act being injustice (zulm) is conditioned by two negative conditions, namely: its not being preceded by his life, and its not being followed by a just compensation. So if its being injustice necessitated deserving blame, then the negative condition would be part of the cause of the positive judgment. And this is a proof.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof against rational good and evil. Al-Razi states that deserving blame is a positive judgment. The act being considered 'injustice' involves two negative conditions: the act is not preceded by the agent's life (meaning the agent did not have a previous life in which he consented? Or perhaps 'his' refers to the victim? The text is a bit obscure) and it is not followed by a just compensation. Al-Razi argues that if being 'injustice' were to cause deserving blame, then a negative condition would be part of the cause of a positive judgment. But this is problematic (برهان = a proof showing impossibility). The implicit argument is that a negative condition (a non-existence) cannot be a part of a cause for a positive effect. Hence, the rationalist claim that certain acts are inherently blameworthy due to their intrinsic qualities fails because those qualities involve negative conditions. This proof is extremely concise and assumes the reader knows the background.

Key Terms
استحقاق الذم
ظلم
قيد عدمي
حكم وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative conditions can be part of a cause for a positive effect.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that a non-existence cannot be a real cause; only positive realities can cause effects.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the thirteenth proof?
A) Blame is impossible for God.
B) Injustice is defined using positive conditions.
C) A negative condition cannot be part of the cause of a positive judgment.
D) Compensation removes blame.

Key Takeaway
A positive judgment like blame cannot have a negative condition as part of its cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself quick to blame someone for a wrong, do you ever pause to consider whether the blame is justified solely by the act, or does it require a deeper cause? How can you apply Al-Razi's reasoning to suspend judgment until you examine if the blame is truly warranted? Will you commit to reflecting before blaming today?
?
Think of a mistake you made that you thought was clearly wrong and deserving of guilt. Can you see that the guilt is not inherent in the act itself? How does this free you to seek forgiveness and grow rather than dwell on self-blame?
?
Al-Razi points out that negative conditions like 'absence of life' cannot cause a positive judgment. How often do you base your moral outrage on what is missing rather than what is present? Can you identify one situation where your criticism of another was based on a lack (of effort, of kindness) rather than a positive evil? Will you change your response next time?

Why Composite Speech Cannot Be Good or Evil
Al-Razi argues from the temporal nature of speech against moral realism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that composite statements have no momentary existence and therefore cannot be the bearers of moral properties like good and evil.

Core Idea
Composite speech never exists at any moment, so it cannot cause goodness or evil.

Al-Razi presents a subtle argument rooted in the physical nature of speech. He defines truth and falsehood as properties of assertions, which are types of speech. But speech, being composed of letters uttered one after another, never exists as a whole at any instant. Only individual letters exist momentarily, and they are not assertions. Thus, the composite cannot be a carrier of moral value.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the definitions of truth (sidq) and falsehood (kidhb) as properties of assertions (khabar). Also note that assertion is a type of speech (kalam).
These definitions set up the argument.
2
Analyze the nature of speech
Al-Razi explains that speech is composed of letters that occur consecutively. At any given moment, only one letter exists. The whole word never exists at once.
This is the key physical observation.
3
Trace the conclusion
Since a single letter is not an assertion, it cannot be true or false. The composite (e.g., the whole word) never exists, so it cannot be the cause of goodness or evil. Therefore, speech cannot be intrinsically good or evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر : الصدق عبارة عن الخبر المطابق للمخبر عنه . والكذب هو الخبر الذي لا يطابق المخبر عنه ، ومن المعلوم أن الصدق والكذب نوعان داخلان تحت جنس الخبر . فإن الخبر نوع داخل تحت جنس الكلام ، والكلام عبارة عن اللفظة المؤلفة من الحروف ، المتعاقبة المتوالية . والموجود منها أبداً ، ليس إلا الحرف الواحد ، وعند انقضائه يحصل الحرف الثاني . وعلى هذا الترتيب . حتى يحصل الحرف الأخير من الكلمة ، وحينئذ تتم الكلمة ، فعلى هذا : الكلمة لا وجود لها البتة في شيء من الأحوال والأزمنة وإنما الموجود منها هو الحرف الواحد ، والحرف الواحد ليس كلاماً واحداً . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الحرف الواحد ليس بخبر ولا بصدق ولا بكذب ، فيمتنع أن يوجب كونه حسناً أو قبيحاً ، وأما بمجموع الكلمة فلا وجود له البتة ، وما لا وجود له البتة يمتنع أن يكون علة لكونه حسناً ، أو لكونه قبيحاً . فيثبت بهذا البرهان : أن الكلام يمتنع أن يكون حسناً أو قبيحاً لكونه
The fourteenth proof: Truth (sidq) is an expression for an assertion (khabar) that corresponds to what it is about. And falsehood (kidhb) is the assertion that does not correspond to what it is about. And it is known that truth and falsehood are two types falling under the genus of assertion. For assertion is a type falling under the genus of speech (kalam), and speech is an expression for the utterance composed of letters, consecutive and successive. And what exists of it at any moment is nothing but a single letter, and upon its completion the second letter occurs. And in this order until the last letter of the word occurs, and then the word is complete. On this basis, the word has no existence at all in any state or time; rather, what exists of it is the single letter, and the single letter is not a speech. When this is established, we say: The single letter is neither an assertion nor truthful nor false, so it is impossible for it to cause its being good or evil. And as for the totality of the word, it has no existence at all, and what has no existence at all cannot be a cause for its being good or for its being evil. Thus it is established by this proof that speech is impossible to be good or evil because of its being...
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is the fourteenth proof against rational good and evil. Al-Razi argues from the nature of speech and assertion. He defines truth and falsehood as properties of assertions (khabar). Assertions are a type of speech. Speech is composed of letters that occur one after another. At any given moment, only one letter exists; the whole word never exists at once as a single entity. Therefore, the word as a whole has no existence. Since a single letter is not a full assertion, it cannot be true or false, and thus cannot be good or evil. And the whole word never exists, so it cannot be the cause of goodness or evil. Therefore, speech cannot be intrinsically good or evil. The proof relies on the fact that composite entities have no momentary existence; only their parts exist in succession. Hence, moral qualities cannot attach to such composites.

Key Terms
الصدق
الكذب
الخبر
الكلام
الحرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A statement as a whole exists in the mind at once.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that composite statements have no simultaneous existence in external reality; only parts exist successively.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi conclude that speech cannot be good or evil?
A) Because truth and falsehood are not moral categories.
B) Because speech as a composite does not exist at any moment.
C) Because letters are not speech.
D) Because God's speech is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Moral values cannot be inherent in speech because speech as a composite lacks simultaneous existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If speech as a composite never exists simultaneously, then your own words are never fully present as a whole. When you claim to have spoken the truth, what exactly are you claiming? How can you be sure your statement is true when only a part of it exists at any moment? Will you now examine the basis of your own assertions more carefully?
?
Al-Razi shows that a single letter cannot be good or evil. Think about the last time you judged someone's speech as 'bad' or 'evil'—did you consider the whole statement or just a part? How often do you rush to moral judgment before the entire speech is even complete? What change can you make to avoid hasty moral verdicts?
?
This proof strips speech of inherent moral value. If your words are not good or evil in themselves, then what gives them moral weight? How does this affect the way you speak—will you be more careful, knowing that the moral quality of your speech comes from something beyond the words themselves?

God Is Not Subject to Blame or Obligation
Concludes that nothing is evil from God or obligatory upon Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that blameworthiness is not inherent in acts themselves, and therefore God cannot be subject to blame, evil, or obligation.

Core Idea
God's acts are beyond blame and obligation.

This concluding passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah focuses on the implications of God's nature for moral categories. It argues that blame is not attached to specific acts, so God cannot be blamed or obligated. The reasoning is concise but decisive, establishing a key theological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if that is the case... then it is impossible.'
2
Understand the pivotal premise
Identify the premise: blame is not based on the specific act performed or omitted. This is the foundation of the argument.
The passage says: 'not because he did such an act or left such an act.'
3
Follow the logical inference
If blame does not depend on the act itself, then any act by the same agent does not trigger blame. Apply this to God.
The passage generalizes to 'whether he did any act or left any thing, it is impossible that he deserves blame.'
4
Grasp the conclusions
From the impossibility of blame, two conclusions follow: no act of God is evil (qabīḥ) and no act is obligatory (wājib) upon God.
These are direct implications of the logical chain.
5
Reflect on the decisiveness
The author asserts this as a decisive statement with no ambiguity. Consider why this is important for theodicy and divine attributes.
The phrase 'لا غبار عليه البتة' emphasizes certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مستحق للذم ليس لأجل أنه فعل الفعل الفلاني، أو ترك الفعل الفلاني، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فسواء فعل أي فعل قدراً، أو ترك أي شيء قدراً، فإنه يمتنع كونه مستحقاً للذم. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع أن يقبح من الله تعالى شيء. وامتنع أن يجب على الله تعالى شيء. وهذا كلام قاطع في هذا الباب. لا غبار عليه البتة والله أعلم.
He is not deserving of blame, not because he did such an act or left such an act. If that is the case, then whether he did any act at all or left any thing at all, it is impossible that he deserves blame. If that is the case, it is impossible that anything be considered evil from God Almighty, and impossible that anything be obligatory upon God Almighty. This is a decisive statement in this chapter; there is no dust upon it at all. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument. It explains that blameworthiness does not attach to an agent based on the specific act performed or omitted. Since the condition for blame is not the act itself but something else (presumably a rational cause), and God is not subject to that condition, it follows that no act of God can be evil (qabīḥ) and no act can be obligatory (wājib) upon God. The author asserts this as a decisive, clear conclusion with no ambiguity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can be blamed for evil acts or that He can be obligated to do certain things.
Correction: The passage argues that blame is not based on the act itself, so God, being beyond such conditions, cannot be blamed or obligated.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is God not deserving of blame for any action?
A) Because God only does good acts.
B) Because blame is not based on the specific act but on something else, and God lacks that condition.
C) Because God is powerful and cannot be questioned.
D) Because all acts of God are neutral.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are beyond moral evaluation; He is not subject to blame or obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you live as though God is obligated to reward your good deeds? Examine your expectations: do you secretly believe He 'owes' you Paradise? How does this lesson uproot that entitlement, and what concrete step can you take today to serve Him purely without expecting repayment?
?
If no act of God can be evil, then every event in your life—even your deepest pain—is from Him and is good by virtue of being His. Can you sincerely say 'All praise is for God' for every moment of your past year? What prevents you, and what will you do to train your heart to see His goodness in all things starting now?

The Thirteenth Argument: Blame and Negative Conditions
Presents a logical proof against rational determination of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the thirteenth argument refutes rational good and evil by showing that blameworthiness (istiḥqāq al-dhamm) cannot depend on negative conditions.

Core Idea
A negative condition cannot be part of the cause of a positive judgment like blame.

This lesson presents a concise logical argument from the Ash'ari tradition against the Mu'tazilite claim that acts are intrinsically good or evil. The argument shows that blameworthiness (istiḥqāq al-dhamm) is a positive judgment, but the act's being 'unjust' depends on negative conditions. The author then reveals a logical flaw: if blame were caused by injustice, a negative condition would be part of the cause of a positive judgment, which is absurd. This argument strikes at the heart of rationalist ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall claim.
Pay attention to the terms 'positive judgment' and 'negative condition'.
2
Identify the two negative conditions
Find the two conditions mentioned for injustice: 'not preceded by its life' and 'not followed by just compensation'. Why are these called 'negative'?
The exact meaning of 'life' (حياته) is ambiguous; it may refer to permission or existence.
3
Understand the conditional logic
The argument uses a conditional: 'If the act being unjust necessitated blame, then a negative condition would be part of the cause of an existential judgment.' Trace this reasoning step by step.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the conclusion is absurd, so the premise is false.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this invalidate rational good and evil? Think about how blame cannot be intrinsic to an act if it depends on external negative conditions.
5
Summarize in your own words
Try to restate the entire argument briefly to ensure you understand the logical chain.
This will help solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر: في إبطال القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه: أن نقول: استحقاق الذم حكم ثابت، وكون الفعل ظلماً يعتبر فيه قيدان عدميان، وهما: كونه غير مسبوق بحياته، وكونه غير ملحوق بعوض جائر، فلو كان كونه ظلماً يوجب استحقاق الذم، لكان القيد العدمي جزءاً من علة الحكم الوجودي وهو برهان.
The thirteenth argument: In refuting the claim of rational judgment of good and evil: We say: Deserving blame is a fixed judgment, and the act being injustice involves two negative conditions: that it is not preceded by its [life?] and that it is not followed by a just compensation. If its being injustice necessitated deserving blame, then the negative condition would be part of the cause of the existential judgment. That is a proof.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against rational determination of good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ al-ʿaql). It argues that blameworthiness (istiḥqāq al-dhamm) is a positive judgment, but the act being 'unjust' (ẓulm) depends on two negative conditions (qaydān ʿadamiyān): the act is not preceded by something (perhaps permission) and is not followed by a just compensation. If the act's being unjust caused blame, then a negative condition would be part of the cause of a positive judgment, which is absurd. Thus, blame cannot be rationally established.

Key Terms
استحقاق الذم
ظلم
قيد عدمي
حكم وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unjust acts are intrinsically blameworthy regardless of context.
Correction: This argument shows that blameworthiness depends on negative conditions (absence of permission and compensation), so it is not purely intrinsic.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the thirteenth argument?
A) Blame is based on the act itself.
B) Negative conditions cannot be part of the cause of a positive judgment like blame.
C) God is always just.
D) Rational good and evil are valid.

Key Takeaway
The thirteenth argument refutes rational good and evil by showing that blame is a positive judgment that cannot be caused by negative conditions inherent in an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If blame for your sins is not solely based on the act you did but on conditions you may not control, how does that change your view of your own accountability? Read the argument again: does it make you more aware of your need for God's mercy rather than relying on your own sense of justice? Let it drive you to sincere repentance today.
?
You often judge others for their actions as unjust. But this lesson teaches that even 'injustice' depends on negative conditions outside the act itself. When did you last condemn someone without knowing their full context? In light of this argument, will you change how you think about the people you consider wrongdoers? Let this lesson humble you and turn your judgment into prayer for them.
?
The argument refutes the idea that your own reason can fully determine good and evil. Is there an area of your life where you trust your own reasoning more than the Qur'an and Sunnah? What if your 'obvious' moral judgments are incomplete without divine guidance? Commit today to submit your moral reasoning to revelation, even when it challenges your feelings.

The Atomistic Argument: Why Words Cannot Be Good or Evil
Defines truth/falsehood and argues that speech's atomistic nature prevents them from causing moral value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that truth and falsehood cannot be causes of moral goodness or evil because the word as a whole never exists at any moment.

Core Idea
Words never truly exist—only their letters do, so truth cannot be a moral cause.

The author begins by defining truth and falsehood as types of statements, and statements as part of speech. He then analyzes the nature of speech itself: it is composed of letters that appear one after another, and the complete word never actually exists at any single moment. Based on this, he argues that since the word as a whole has no existence, it cannot be a cause for moral attributes. This is a key argument against rational moral evaluation of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of truth and falsehood
Notice how the author defines truth as correspondence and falsehood as lack of correspondence. Understand that they are types of statements.
2
Follow the analysis of speech
The author breaks speech down into letters existing sequentially. See how he concludes that a word never exists as a whole.
Focus on the step-by-step reasoning from letters to the non-existence of the word.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author uses this to argue that since a single letter is not a statement, and the whole word never exists, truth/falsehood cannot cause goodness or evil.
This is the main takeaway from this section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر: الصدق عبارة عن الخبر المطابق للمخبر عنه. والكذب هو الخبر الذي لا يطابق المخبر عنه، ومن المعلوم أن الصدق والكذب نوعان داخلان تحت جنس الخبر. فإن الخبر نوع داخل تحت جنس الكلام، والكلام عبارة عن اللفظة المؤلفة من الحروف، المتعاقبة المتوالية. والموجود منها أبداً، ليس إلا الحرف الواحد، وعند انقضائه يحصل الحرف الثاني. وعلى هذا الترتيب. حتى يحصل الحرف الأخير من الكلمة، وحينئذ تتم الكلمة، فعلى هذا: الكلمة لا وجود لها البتة في شيء من الأحوال والأزمنة وإنما الموجود منها هو الحرف الواحد، والحرف الواحد ليس كلاماً واحداً. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الحرف الواحد ليس بخبر ولا بصدق ولا بكذب، فيمتنع أن يوجب كونه حسناً أو قبيحاً، وأما بمجموع الكلمة فلا وجود له البتة، وما لا وجود له البتة يمتنع أن يكون علة لكونه حسناً، أو لكونه قبيحاً. فيثبت بهذا البرهان: أن الكلام يمتنع أن يكون حسناً أو قبيحاً لكونه صدقاً أو لكونه كذباً. فإن قالوا: فهذا وارد عليكم أيضاً لأنكم سلمتم كونه حسناً وقبيحاً، بمعنى كونه سبباً لحصول المصلحة أو حصول المفسدة. فما ذكرتموه وارد عليكم في هذا المقام. فنقول: الفرق أنكم تقولون: إن كونه صدقاً يوجب صفة الحسن، وكونه كذباً يوجب صفة القبح. فنقول: الصدق هو المجموع وهذا المجموع لا وجود له البتة، وما لا وجود له البتة امتنع كونه موجباً لصفة ثابتة حقيقية، بخلاف قولنا: فإنا نقول: إنا لما سمعنا تلك الحروف المتوالية التي اصطلحوا على جعلها معرفات لبعض المعاني، جرم بهذا الطريق فهمنا منها بعض المعاني. فحينئذ يتبني على ذلك الاعتقاد أمراً من الأمور. فإن كان ذلك الكلام كذباً فحينئذ ينكشف لنا: أن سعينا كان باطلاً، وعلمنا كان مانعاً. ولا معنى لكونه قبيحاً إلا هذا فظهر الفرق.
The fourteenth argument: Truth (ṣidq) is a statement (khabar) that corresponds to what is reported. Falsehood (kidhb) is a statement that does not correspond. It is known that truth and falsehood are two types under the genus of statement. The statement is a type under the genus of speech (kalām). Speech is an utterance composed of successive letters. What exists from it at any moment is only a single letter, and upon its passing the second letter occurs. And so on until the last letter of the word occurs, then the word is completed. Thus, the word does not exist at all in any state or time; only the single letter exists, and a single letter is not one speech. If this is established, we say: A single letter is not a statement, nor truth, nor falsehood, so it is impossible that it necessitates its being good or evil. As for the totality of the word, it has no existence at all, and what has no existence cannot be a cause for its being good or evil. So it is proven by this proof that speech cannot be good or evil because it is true or false. If they say: This also applies to you because you admit that it is good or evil in the sense of being a cause for benefit or harm. So what you mentioned applies to you in this context. We say: The difference is that you say that its being true necessitates the attribute of good, and its being false necessitates the attribute of evil. We say: Truth is the totality, and this totality has no existence at all, and what has no existence cannot necessitate a real attribute. In contrast, our view: When we hear those successive letters which people have agreed to make indicators for certain meanings, by this means we understand some meanings. Then upon that belief something is built. If that speech is false, then we discover that our effort was futile and our knowledge was preventing. And there is no meaning to its being evil except this. So the difference becomes clear.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the rational moral evaluation of speech. It begins by defining truth and falsehood as types of statements, and statements as a type of speech. Speech is composed of individual letters that exist only one at a time; the complete word never actually exists at any moment. Therefore, a single letter is not a statement, and the word as a whole has no existence. Consequently, neither truth nor falsehood can be a cause for moral goodness or evil. The objection is raised that the author's own view (that speech is good or evil based on consequences) also faces the same problem. The answer distinguishes that the opponent claims truth and falsehood are inherent attributes causing moral qualities, while the author only claims that the understanding derived from the sequence of letters leads to benefit or harm, which is a real effect. Thus, the proof does not undermine the author's position.

Key Terms
صدق
كذب
خبر
كلام
لفظة
حرف
حسن
قبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because a word is composed of letters, it can have moral value as a whole.
Correction: The argument shows that the word as a whole never exists, so it cannot be a cause for moral attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't truth or falsehood cause a word to be good or evil?
A) Because truth and falsehood are not real.
B) Because a word as a whole never exists at any moment.
C) Because God alone determines moral value.
D) Because speech is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The atomistic analysis of speech shows that no word exists as a whole at any moment, so truth and falsehood cannot be inherent causes of moral value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If no word you utter ever fully exists as a whole, what does that mean for the weight you place on your own statements? How does this affect your accountability before Allah for every letter you speak?
?
When you say 'I believe' or 'I repent,' are you relying on the existence of that phrase as a real entity? How does this argument challenge the sincerity of your verbal declarations?
?
This argument reduces all human speech to a series of vanishing atoms. Does it make you more conscious of the power of each individual word, or does it make you feel that speech is meaningless? What is your response to this theological insight in your daily conversation?

Responding to the Objection: Consequence vs. Inherent Attribute
Responds to objection by distinguishing inherent attributes from consequential effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his own consequentialist view against the claim that it faces the same atomistic problem.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the author's good/evil is based on real consequences, not on non-existent inherent attributes.

After establishing that truth and falsehood cannot be moral causes due to the atomistic nature of speech, the author anticipates an objection: his own view that goodness and evil are based on benefit and harm also involves speech, so it faces the same problem. The author responds by clarifying a crucial distinction: his opponents claim that truth and falsehood are inherent attributes causing goodness/evil, while he only claims that the understanding derived from the sequence of letters leads to real consequences like benefit or harm. Thus, the atomistic argument does not apply to his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
See how the opponent tries to turn the argument against the author by claiming consistency: the atomistic problem applies to the author's view too.
The objection claims that the author's own admission that speech is good/evil based on benefit/harm is subject to the same critique.
2
Understand the author's distinction
The author distinguishes between inherent attributes (which require a real existent subject) and consequential effects (which do not depend on the existence of the whole word).
The opponent claims truth/falsehood necessitate inherent attributes; the author only claims that understanding the speech leads to real benefits/harms.
3
See the conclusion
The author concludes that his view remains unscathed because the goodness/evil is based on actual outcomes, not on non-existent properties of the word itself.
The difference is that the author's good/evil is not an attribute of the speech but a result of the cognitive and practical consequences.

?

?
If moral value lies in consequences rather than inherent qualities, then every word you speak carries responsibility for the effect it has on others. Are you mindful of the beliefs your speech creates and the actions they lead to? What change will you make today in how you speak?

The Dilemma of a Threatening Promise
A dilemma argument against intrinsic truth/falsehood morality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifteenth argument uses a dilemma to challenge the view that truth and falsehood are intrinsically good or evil.

Core Idea
Truth cannot be intrinsically good if a true threat of injustice would be good.

This argument presents a powerful dilemma against those who claim truth is good and falsehood evil by their very nature. Using a concrete scenario of a person threatening unjust harm, the argument shows that either carrying out the threat or refraining from it leads to an absurd conclusion. This challenges the idea that truth and falsehood alone determine moral value.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Observe the scenario: a person threatens unjust harm. Note the two possibilities considered.
The passage is a single continuous argument, so read it as a unit.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument sets up two horns: either it is good to refrain from the threatened acts or good to carry them out. Follow each horn to its conclusion.
Both horns lead to contradictions that undermine the opponent's position.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument shows that truth and falsehood cannot be the sole determinants of moral value. The conclusions are absurd, so the premise must be false.
This connects to the learning objective about challenging intrinsic moral values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر: لو قال إنسان لغيره: سأقتلك غداً، وأنهب أموالك وأسبي أولادك، على سبيل الظلم والعدوان. فنقول: إما أن يكون الحسن ترك هذه الأشياء أو فعلها. فإن كان الحسن تركها فنقول: ترك هذه الأشياء يوجب ضرورة كون ذلك الخبر كذباً، وإذا كان ذلك القول حسناً وثبت أن ذلك الترك يستلزم هذا الكذب، وثبت أن ما يستلزمه الحسن يجب أن يكون حسناً، فحينئذ وجب أن يكون ذلك الكذب حسناً، وذلك يقدح في قلوبهم: إن الكذب إنما يقبح لكونه كذباً. وأما إن كان الحسن فعل ما أخبر عنه، ووعد به، لزم أن يكون القتل والنهب والسبي، على سبيل العدوان حسناً. وذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
The fifteenth argument: If a person says to another: 'I will kill you tomorrow, plunder your wealth, and enslave your children,' unjustly and aggressively. Then we say: Either the good (ḥasan) is to refrain from these things or to do them. If the good is to refrain from them, we say: Refraining from these things necessarily makes that statement false. If that statement is good and it is established that refraining entails this falsehood, and it is established that what entails good must itself be good, then it would be necessary that that falsehood is good. That undermines their belief that falsehood is evil because it is false. But if the good is to do what he informed and promised, then it would be necessary that killing, plundering, and enslaving unjustly are good. No rational person would say that.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma argument against the view that truth is good and falsehood is evil intrinsically. The scenario: a person threatens unjust harm. If it is good to refrain from the threatened acts, then the statement becomes false (since the threat is not carried out). The statement being good, and refraining (which entails falsehood) being good, then falsehood would be good by entailment—contradicting the claim that falsehood is evil. If instead it is good to carry out the threat, then the unjust acts become good, which is absurd. Thus, truth and falsehood cannot determine moral value.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
صدق
كذب
ظلم
عدوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a statement is true, it must be good regardless of consequences.
Correction: This argument shows that a true threat of injustice cannot be good, so truth alone does not determine moral value.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the fifteenth argument illustrate?
A) If refraining from harm is good, then the threat becomes false, and falsehood would be good.
B) If carrying out the threat is good, then injustice is good.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither A nor B.

Key Takeaway
The fifteenth argument shows that if truth were intrinsically good, then a truthful threat of injustice would be good, which is absurd; therefore, truth cannot be the sole determinant of moral value.

Refuting the Objection on Causation of Good and Evil
Author refutes an objection by distinguishing causation from non-existent abstraction vs. actual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that his view on good and evil is similar to the opponent's, by showing that his causation is based on perceived speech rather than non-existent abstractions.

Core Idea
Good and evil are caused by real perception, not by abstract non-existent entities.

In this passage, the author faces an objection: if good and evil are defined by benefit and harm, doesn't that make his view similar to the opponent's? The author clarifies by distinguishing the causation: the opponent claims truth itself necessitates goodness, but truth as an abstract composite doesn't exist. The author's view is grounded in actual heard words that lead to beliefs, and when false, that causes harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's claim that the author's view is also vulnerable because he admits good and evil as causes of benefit/harm.
2
Identify the author's critique of the opponent's view
The author argues that the opponent's nexus ('truth necessitates goodness') is invalid because truth as a composite has no real existence, so it cannot cause a real attribute.
Focus on the phrase 'truth is the composite and has no existence'.
3
Examine the author's alternative causation
The author presents his own view: causation flows from actual perception of conventional words, which gives rise to beliefs. If the words are false, the belief leads to wasted effort and prevented knowledge—that is what 'evil' means.
Note the contrast: causation from a real process vs. from an abstraction.
4
Reflect on the difference revealed
The difference is clear: the opponent's view relies on a non-existent entity, while the author's view relies on actual perception. This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فهذا وارد عليكم أيضاً لأنكم سلمتم كونه حسناً وقبيحاً ، بمعنى كونه سبباً لحصول المصلحة أو الحصول المفسدة . فما ذكرتموه وارد عليكم في هذا المقام . فنقول : الفرق أنكم تقولون : إن كونه صدقاً يوجب صفة الحسن ، وكونه كذباً يوجب صفة القبح. فنقول : الصدق هو المجموع ( وهذا المجموع) لا وجود له البتة ، ( وما لا وجود له البتة ) امتنع كونه موجباً لصفة ثابتة حقيقية ، بخلاف قولنا : فإنا نقول : إنا لما سمعنا تلك الحروف المتوالية التي اصطلحوا على جعلها معرفات لبعض المعاني ، جرم بهذا الطريق فهمنا منها بعض المعاني . فحينئذ يتبني على ذلك الاعتقاد أمراً من الأمور . فإن كان ذلك الكلام كذباً فحينئذ ينكشف لنا : أن سعينا كان باطلاً ، وعلمنا كان مانعاً . ولا معنى لكونه قبيحاً إلا هذا فظهر الفرق .
If they say: 'This also applies to you, because you have conceded that it is good and evil, meaning that it is a cause for obtaining benefit or harm. So what you have mentioned applies to you in this context.' We say: The difference is that you say: 'Its being truth necessitates the attribute of goodness, and its being falsehood necessitates the attribute of evil.' We say: Truth is the composite (and this composite) has no existence at all, and what has no existence at all is impossible to be the necessitator of a real, established attribute, unlike our statement: For we say: When we hear those sequential letters that they have conventionally made indicators of certain meanings, we thereby derive from them some meanings. Then upon that belief a matter is built. If that speech is false, then it becomes clear to us that our effort was vain and our knowledge was a prevention. And there is no meaning to its being evil except this. So the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The opponent argues that the author's view (that good and evil are determined by benefit and harm) is also vulnerable to the same critique they made earlier. The author clarifies the distinction: The opponent claims that truth (ṣidq) itself necessitates goodness and falsehood (kidhb) itself necessitates evil. The author responds that truth as a composite entity has no actual existence, so a non-existent thing cannot cause a real attribute. In contrast, the author's view is based on the actual perception of spoken words: hearing conventionally designated letters leads to understanding meanings, from which a belief arises. If that speech turns out to be false, then the effort is wasted and knowledge is hindered; that is what evil means. Thus, the causation is from the actual perception, not from a non-existent abstraction.

Misconception: One might think both the opponent's and author's views depend on the same causation of good and evil.
Correction: The opponent's causation relies on an abstract non-existent entity (truth as composite), whereas the author's causation relies on actual perception of speech.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?
The opponent claims truth itself necessitates goodness, but the author says only real perception of existent speech can cause good or evil. The Fifteenth Argument: Threat of Injustice Refutes Intrinsic Evil of Lies
Proof that goodness cannot be determined by truth/falsehood alone via a threat scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a threat scenario is used to argue that truth/falsehood alone do not determine good and evil, and why this refutes the opponent's position.

Core Idea
A threat that is good can make a lie good, or make evil acts good.

In this argument, the author presents a clever dilemma: imagine someone threatens unjust acts. If the good is in refraining, then the threat is a lie, and since the threat is good, the lie must also be good. If the good is in acting, then evil becomes good. Both options contradict the opponent's view. This passage shows how logical entailment can expose contradictions in moral frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Understand the threat scenario
Read the passage and translation. The scenario is a person threatening another with unjust acts (killing, looting, enslaving) by way of injustice.
The threat is deliberately unjust.
2
Identify the dilemma
The good (ḥusn) of the threat must be either in refraining from the threatened acts or in actually committing them. These are the two branches.
3
Follow the first branch (refraining)
If the good is in refraining, then the threat is a lie (since he doesn't follow through). Since the good entails goodness of its entailments, the lie becomes good. This contradicts the opponent's claim that lying is evil because it is a lie.
This branch shows the opponent's view leads to a good lie.
4
Follow the second branch (acting)
If the good is in acting, then the evil deeds (killing, looting, etc.) become good. This is absurd and no rational person would accept it.
This branch shows the opponent's view leads to good evil acts.
5
Conclude the refutation
Both branches lead to contradictions. Therefore, the opponent's position that truth/falsehood alone determine good and evil is false.
The argument is a successful reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر : لو قال إنسان لغيره : سأقتلك غداً ، وأنهب أموالك وأسبي أولادك . على سبيل الظلم والعدوان . فنقول : إما أن يكون الحسن ترك هذه الأشياء ) أو فعلها . فإن كان الحسن تركها فنقول : ترك هذه الأشياء ( ) يوجب ضرورة كون ذلك الخبر كذباً ، وإذا كان ذلك القول حسناً وثبت أن ذلك الترك يستلزم هذا الكذب ، وثبت أن ما يستلزمه الحسن يجب أن يكون حسناً ، فحينئذ وجب أن يكون ذلك الكذب حسناً ، وذلك يقدح في قلوبهم : إن الكذب إنما يقبح لكونه كذباً . وأما إن كان الحسن فعل ما أخبر عنه ، ووعد به ، لزم أن يكون القتل والنهب والسبي ، على سبيل العدوان حسناً . وذلك لا يقوله عاقل
The fifteenth argument: If a person says to another: 'I will kill you tomorrow, and I will plunder your wealth and take your children captive,' by way of injustice and aggression. We say: Either the good is refraining from these things or doing them. If the good is refraining from them, we say: refraining from these things necessitates that that statement is necessarily a lie. And if that statement is good, and it is established that that refraining entails this lie, and it is established that what the good entails must be good, then that lie becomes good. And that demolishes their claim that lying is evil only because it is a lie. But if the good is doing what he informed and promised, then it follows that killing, plundering, and taking children captive by way of injustice are good. No rational person would say that.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the fifteenth argument) against the Ash'ari view that truth and falsehood intrinsically cause good and evil. The author presents a dilemma: a person threatens unjust acts (killing, looting, enslaving). The good (ḥusn) of this threat must be either in refraining from those acts or in actually committing them. If the good is in refraining, then the threat must be a lie (since he does not follow through). Since the threat (the statement) is considered good, and it entails a lie, then the lie must also be good (because what the good entails is good). This contradicts the opponent's claim that lying is evil because it is a lie. If the good is in committing the acts, then the evil deeds themselves become good, which is absurd. Thus, the opponent's position is refuted.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
كذب
استلزام
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the threat itself is neutral and doesn't affect moral judgments.
Correction: The argument shows that within the opponent's framework, the threat leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
In the fifteenth argument, if the good is refraining from the threatened actions, what follows?
A) The threat is a lie and must be evil.
B) The threat is a lie and must be good.
C) The threat is true and must be good.
D) The threat is true and must be evil.

Key Takeaway
The fifteenth argument shows that the opponent's view leads to either a good lie or good evil acts, thus refuting that truth/falsehood alone determine moral value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a promise or a statement, how often do you consider whether you will actually fulfill it? Does this argument reveal that your words can have moral weight beyond their truth value? Will you now be more careful to speak truthfully, or to ensure your intentions align with your statements?
?
Think of a time you told a lie to avoid harming someone. Did you consider that lie 'good'? How does this argument force you to re-evaluate the moral basis of that action? Are you willing to accept that lying might sometimes be good, or does that disturb your sense of right and wrong?
?
This argument shows that holding an absolute rule (e.g., 'lying is always evil') can lead to absurdity. What absolute rules do you hold in your own life that might crumble under logical scrutiny? Are you ready to examine them and potentially change your convictions?

The Obligation to Lie to Save a Prophet
Proof that being a lie does not necessitate evil by a case where lying is obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixteenth argument from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which demonstrates that an act being a lie does not inherently make it evil, using the case of hiding a prophet.

Core Idea
A lie becomes obligatory when it saves a prophet's life, proving that lies are not intrinsically evil.

This lesson presents a concrete scenario from a great scholar's work: an oppressor seeks to kill a prophet, and a man must lie to protect him. The argument shows that if lying were inherently evil, it could never be obligatory; yet in this case, lying is obligatory, so its evil cannot be inherent. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the scenario: an oppressor, a prophet, a hiding place, and a man asked about the prophet's location.
Notice that the man knows the truth but chooses to lie because telling the truth would lead to murder.
2
Identify the key inference
The author concludes that lying does not necessitate evil (qubh). Find the line that states this.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters: it challenges the view that certain acts are always bad. If an act can be obligatory, it cannot be intrinsically evil.
This is a classic argument in Islamic theology about moral valuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : إذا قصد ظالم قتل نبي من الأنبياء ، فهرب ذلك النبي واختفى في دار رجل ، فجاء ذلك الظالم وسأل ذلك الرجل مكان ذلك النبي ، وعلم ذلك الرجل أنه لو صدق أقدم ذلك الظالم على قتل ذلك النبي فههنا يجب عليه الكذب وترك الصدق . وذلك يدل على أن كونه كذباً لا يوجب القبح
The sixteenth argument: If an oppressor intends to kill a prophet, and that prophet flees and hides in a man's house, then the oppressor comes and asks that man about the location of that prophet, and that man knows that if he tells the truth, the oppressor would proceed to kill that prophet—then here lying is obligatory upon him, and truthfulness is abandoned. And that indicates that its being a lie does not necessitate evil.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the sixteenth argument) that being a lie does not inherently necessitate evil (qubh). The scenario: an oppressor seeks to kill a prophet. The prophet hides in someone's house. The oppressor asks the homeowner about the prophet's location. The homeowner knows that if he tells the truth, the prophet will be killed. In this situation, the homeowner is obligated to lie and avoid truthfulness. Since lying is obligatory, it cannot be evil, proving that the intrinsic property of being a lie does not cause evil. Otherwise, an obligatory action would be evil, which is a contradiction.

Key Terms
كذب
صدق
قبح
وجوب
نبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lying is always evil and forbidden.
Correction: The author shows a scenario where lying is obligatory, proving that being a lie does not necessitate evil.

Checkpoint

A) Because the lie saves the prophet's life.
B) Because lying is always better than truth.
C) Because in this case lying is obligatory, so it cannot be evil.
D) Because the oppressor is unjust.

Key Takeaway
Lying can be obligatory in certain circumstances, proving that its evilness is not intrinsic but depends on context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument proves that actions are not inherently good or evil—their morality depends on context. Does this challenge your comfort zone? Are you ready to submit to Allah's wisdom even when it contradicts your ingrained moral instincts? What step will you take today to deepen your understanding of Islamic moral theory so you can act with certainty?

Circumlocution vs. Lying to Save a Prophet
First objection (circumlocution) and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circumlocution (ta'rīḍ) is not always a valid alternative to lying when protecting a prophet from a knowing oppressor.

Core Idea
Circumlocution fails when the oppressor knows all evasions; only lying or spilling blood remain.

The first objection to the sixteenth argument claims that one should use circumlocution instead of lying. The response shows that in the specific scenario where the oppressor is fully aware of all evasions, circumlocution is useless. This forces a stark choice: commit a lie or allow the prophet's death.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection and its answer. Notice the structure: objection (فإن قيل) then answer (والجواب).
2
Understand the objection
The objector says circumlocution is always permissible and lying is not. Grasp the principle that circumlocution avoids lying.
Circumlocution means using ambiguous true statements.
3
Follow the answer's reasoning
The answer restricts the scenario: the oppressor knows all circumlocutions, so none work. Only two options remain: kill the prophet or lie. Since killing is wrong, lying becomes obligatory.
4
Note the conclusion
The answer establishes that in this case, lying is the lesser evil and thus obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : على هذا الكلام سؤالان : الأول : إنه لا يجوز له أن يكذب : بل الواجب عليه أن يقدم على التعريضات ، كما قيل : « إن في المعاريض لمندوحة عن الكذب ) . الثاني : هب أنه يجب عليه الكلب ( في هذه الصورة ) إلا أنا نقول : إن العلة قد يتخلف عنها معلولها لقيام المانع . وههنا حصل هذا المانع ، فلا تخلف الأثر عن العلة ، وذلك لا يقدح في قولنا : إن كونه كذباً يوجب القبح . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا نفرض الكلام فيما إذا كان ذلك الظالم بجميع وجوه المعاريض ، وكان المسئول عنه عالماً بأنه لو أقدم على شيء من التعريضات ، لعلم ذلك الظالم كون النبي عنده ( وحينئذ يبالغ في الطلب ، ويقدم على القتل ، فههنا لا فائدة في التعريض البتة ) فلم يبق إلا أحد أمرين : إما السعي في إهدار دم ذلك النبي المعصوم ، أو التزام الكذب ، ولما كان الأول باطلاً ، تعين الثاني . والجواب عن الثاني : أن نقول : لو جوزنا تخلف القبح ( عن الكذب ) لبعض الموانع فحينئذ لا كذب إلا ويحتمل أنه حصل فيه مانع من كونه قبيحاً . وعلى هذا التقدير وجب أن لا يبقى الوثوق بشيء من كلام الله ، ولا من كلام رسوله ، فإنه تصير كل هذه النصوص مظنونة لا معلومة
If it is said: Concerning this statement, there are two questions: First: It is not permissible for him to lie; rather, he must resort to circumlocutions (ta'rīḍāt), as it is said: 'In circumlocutions there is ample scope to avoid lying.' Second: Suppose that lying is obligatory in this case; but we say: The cause may be separated from its effect due to the presence of a preventing factor. Here this preventing factor has occurred, so the effect is not absent from the cause, and that does not refute our statement that its being a lie necessitates evil. The answer to the first question: We assume the discussion is when that oppressor knows all types of circumlocutions, and the questioned person knows that if he uses any circumlocution, the oppressor would know that the prophet is with him, and then he would intensify the search and proceed to kill. So there is no benefit in circumlocution at all. Then only two matters remain: either striving to spill the blood of that protected prophet, or committing a lie. Since the first is invalid, the second is determined. The answer to the second: If we allow that evil (qubh) can be absent from lying due to some preventing factor, then there is no lie except that it is possible that a preventing factor from its being evil has occurred in it. On this assumption, no trust would remain in any speech of God or any speech of His Messenger, because all these texts would become probable rather than certain.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage contains an objection (فإن قيل) with two questions and their answers. The objection is against the sixteenth argument about lying to save a prophet. First objection: The man should use circumlocution (ta'rīḍ), not outright lying. Second objection: Even if lying becomes obligatory, the cause of evil (being a lie) may be overridden by a preventing factor (māni'), so the absence of evil does not disprove causation. The answer to the first: The scenario assumes the oppressor knows all circumlocutions, so they are useless; lying becomes the only option. The answer to the second: If we allow the effect (evil) to be absent from its cause because of a preventing factor, then every lie could be suspected of having such a factor, leading to uncertainty in all divine and prophetic speech, which is unacceptable. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
تعريض
كذب
معاريض
مندوحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circumlocution is always a valid alternative to lying, no matter the situation.
Correction: The answer shows that when the oppressor is aware of all types of circumlocutions, they become useless, and lying may become necessary to save a life.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, why is circumlocution not possible in this case?
A) The prophet forbade it.
B) The oppressor knows all circumlocutions.
C) Circumlocution is always sinful.
D) The prophet was already killed.

Key Takeaway
When circumlocution is ineffective, lying may become obligatory to save a prophet's life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a situation in your life where you used ambiguous language to avoid the truth? Were you truly protecting someone innocent, or were you protecting your own image? What would you do if that ambiguity were seen through and you faced a choice between a dangerous lie and a devastating loss?
?
If you were in a position where only a lie could save an innocent life, would your fear of Allah's displeasure with lying outweigh your fear of the consequences of not lying? How do you prepare your heart and mind now for such a trial?
?
When you examine your daily speech, how often do you rely on subtle evasions to avoid discomfort? Are you cultivating the sincerity before Allah that would empower you to speak the truth even when it costs you, or are you building a habit of concealment that may fail when tested?

Preventing Factors and Certainty in Revelation
Second objection (preventing factor) and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing a preventing factor to remove the evil of lying would undermine certainty in all divine and prophetic speech.

Core Idea
If a preventing factor could nullify the evil of lying, then no lie can be known to be evil, destroying trust in revelation.

The second objection argues that the cause (lying being evil) may be overridden by a preventing factor, so the absence of evil does not disprove causation. The answer points out the catastrophic consequence: if we allow such a possibility, then every lie could be excused by an unknown factor, making all revealed texts uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read the Arabic text for the second objection and its answer. Note the logical structure.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objector accepts that lying might be obligatory but says that the connection between lying and being evil can be broken by a preventing factor (māni'). So the absence of evil does not invalidate the general rule.
Cause (ʿilla) and effect (maʿlūl) terminology is used.
3
Follow the answer's reductio ad absurdum
The answer argues that if preventing factors are allowed, then any lie might have an unknown factor making it not evil. This would make it impossible to be certain about any claim that lying is evil, including in divine and prophetic speech.
4
Appreciate the implication for revelation
The key point is that accepting a preventing factor undermines the certainty needed for religious texts.

If it is said: Concerning this statement, there are two questions: First: It is not permissible for him to lie; rather, he must resort to circumlocutions (ta'rīḍāt), as it is said: 'In circumlocutions there is ample scope to avoid lying.' Second: Suppose that lying is obligatory in this case; but we say: The cause may be separated from its effect due to the presence of a preventing factor. Here this preventing factor has occurred, so the effect is not absent from the cause, and that does not refute our statement that its being a lie necessitates evil. The answer to the first question: We assume the discussion is when that oppressor knows all types of circumlocutions, and the questioned person knows that if he uses any circumlocution, the oppressor would know that the prophet is with him, and then he would intensify the search and proceed to kill. So there is no benefit in circumlocution at all. Then only two matters remain: either striving to spill the blood of that protected prophet, or committing a lie. Since the first is invalid, the second is determined. The answer to the second: If we allow that evil (qubh) can be absent from lying due to some preventing factor, then there is no lie except that it is possible that a preventing factor from its being evil has occurred in it. On this assumption, no trust would remain in any speech of God or any speech of His Messenger, because all these texts would become probable rather than certain.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage contains an objection (فإن قيل) with two questions and their answers. The objection is against the sixteenth argument about lying to save a prophet. First objection: The man should use circumlocution (ta'rīḍ), not outright lying. Second objection: Even if lying becomes obligatory, the cause of evil (being a lie) may be overridden by a preventing factor (māni'), so the absence of evil does not disprove causation. The answer to the first: The scenario assumes the oppressor knows all circumlocutions, so they are useless; lying becomes the only option. The answer to the second: If we allow the effect (evil) to be absent from its cause because of a preventing factor, then every lie could be suspected of having such a factor, leading to uncertainty in all divine and prophetic speech, which is unacceptable. Thus, the objection fails.

Razi's Defense of Obligatory Lying
Razi presents and answers two objections regarding obligatory lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi answers two key objections to his argument that lying can be obligatory to save a prophet's life, and why this does not lead to skepticism about divine revelation.

Core Idea
Protecting innocent life can make lying obligatory without undermining the reliability of God's words.

Razi anticipates two objections to his claim that lying can be obligatory to save a prophet. The first objection suggests equivocation as an alternative; the second worries that exceptions break the rule. In this passage, he refutes both objections, showing that his argument is coherent and does not lead to skepticism about revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario presented by Razi
Start by reading the first part of the passage where Razi describes the scenario: a man knows that telling the truth would lead to an oppressor killing a prophet. Notice the conclusion that lying is obligatory here.
This sets up the claim that lying can be obligatory.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues that one should use equivocation (taʿrīḍ) instead of direct lying. Read how the objector presents this alternative.
Equivocation is speaking ambiguously without outright falsehood.
3
Study Razi's answer to the first objection
Razi specifies that the scenario is one where the oppressor is aware of all kinds of equivocation, so they would be useless. Read his reasoning carefully.
When equivocation fails, only two options remain: let the prophet die or lie.
4
Identify the second objection
The second objection concedes that lying might be obligatory here but argues that an impediment (māniʿ) can suspend the inherent evilness of lying without denying that lying is usually evil.
This objection tries to preserve the general rule while allowing exceptions.
5
Understand Razi's answer to the second objection
Razi shows that if any impediment can remove evilness, then no statement (including divine revelation) can be trusted because it might be affected by such an impediment. This leads to radical skepticism, which is unacceptable.
Thus the objection is refuted by its own consequences.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا نفرض الكلام فيما إذا كان ذلك الظالم بجميع وجوه المعاريض ، وكان المسئول عنه عالماً بأنه لو أقدم على شيء من التعريضات ، لعلم ذلك الظالم كون النبي عنده ( وحينئذ يبالغ في الطلب ، ويقدم على القتل ، فههنا لا فائدة في التعريض البتة ) فلم يبق إلا أحد أمرين : إما السعي في إهدار دم ذلك النبي المعصوم ، أو التزام الكذب ، ولما كان الأول باطلاً ، تعين الثاني .

فهذه جملة الدلائل في بيان أن حكم العقل بالتحسين والتقبيح في أحكام الله وفي أفعاله : حكم باطل ، غير ملتفت إليه
And that man knew that if he told the truth, that oppressor would proceed to kill that (prophet). So here lying is obligatory upon him and abandoning truthfulness. And that indicates that its being a lie does not necessitate evilness. If it is said: There are two questions concerning this speech: First: It is not permissible for him to lie; rather he must resort to equivocations, as it is said: 'In equivocations there is a way out of lying.' Second: Grant that lying is obligatory (in this case), but we say: The cause may be separated from its effect due to the existence of an impediment. Here this impediment has occurred, so there is no separation of the effect from the cause, and that does not harm our statement that its being a lie necessitates evilness. And God knows best. The answer to the first question: We suppose the case where that oppressor (is aware of) all kinds of equivocations, and the questioned person knows that if he used any equivocation, the oppressor would realize the prophet's presence and then would intensify the search and proceed to kill him; so here there is no benefit in equivocation at all. Then nothing remains except one of two things: either striving to spill the blood of that infallible prophet, or committing lying. Since the first is invalid, the second is determined. The answer to the second: We say: If we allow that evilness (of lying) can be absent due to some impediment, then there is no lie except that it is possible that an impediment preventing its evilness has occurred. On this assumption, no trust would remain in any speech of God nor in any speech of His messenger, for all these texts would become probable, not certain. So this is a summary of the proofs demonstrating that reason's judgment of good and evil concerning God's rulings and His acts is a false judgment, not to be heeded.

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the claim that lying can be obligatory and their answers. It begins by stating a hypothetical: a man knows that if he tells the truth, an oppressor will kill a prophet; therefore, lying is necessary. This shows that being a lie does not inherently make it evil. Two objections are then raised: (1) One should use equivocation (taʿrīḍ) instead of lying. (2) The cause of evilness may be absent due to an impediment, so the example does not disprove intrinsic evil. The first answer argues that in this scenario, all equivocations are futile because the oppressor would see through them, leaving only two options—kill the prophet or lie; the latter is chosen. The second answer points out that if evilness can be removed by any impediment, then no statement (including divine revelation) can be trusted, because it might be affected by an impediment. This would undermine all certainty in religion. The passage concludes that reason's judgment of good and evil regarding God's acts is false.

Correction: Razi shows that in extreme cases (saving a prophet's life), lying can be obligatory. Moreover, allowing exceptions does not destroy the reliability of divine revelation, because the second objection's consequence of skepticism is avoided by not allowing arbitrary impediments.

The Ash'ari–Mu'tazila Debate on Intrinsic Good and Evil
Razi presents the Ash'ari–Mu'tazila debate and explains the Mu'tazila theory of wujuh with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument against intrinsic goodness and the Mu'tazila alternative based on aspects (wujuh).

Core Idea
Good and evil depend on circumstances, not essences.

This passage presents a classic debate: the Ash'aris argue that if actions had intrinsic moral values, then all instances of killing or pleasure would be equally good or evil, which is false. The Mu'tazila respond by introducing the concept of aspects (wujuh), arguing that values depend on relations and circumstances. Razi then illustrates this with examples from everyday experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari argument
Identify the logical structure: if killing were evil by essence, then all killings (including lawful retaliation) would be evil; if pleasure were good by essence, then all pleasures (including fornication) would be good. Since the consequences are absurd, the premise must be false.
Notice that the Ash'ari employ a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila response
The Mu'tazila deny the premise of intrinsic values. They claim good and evil depend on aspects (wujuh) that relate to the action, not its essence. This shifts the debate to defining those aspects.
The Ash'ari immediately object that 'aspects' are unintelligible.
3
Examine Razi's examples
Razi provides two examples: vinegar's pleasure depends on prior food and stomach condition; desire for medicine depends on bodily humors and absence of alternatives. These illustrate how circumstances (aspects) determine value, not essence alone.
The passage ends mid-sentence; Razi likely continued with further examples.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this debate shapes the kalam approach to ethics: moral judgments are not self-evident but require understanding of context and divine command (for Ash'aris) or rational aspects (for Mu'tazila).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن جماعة عظيمة من أصحاب أبي الحسن الأشعري - رحمه الله - احتجوا على إبطال القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه . بأن قالوا : لو قبح القتل لذاته ، لوجب أن يقبح كل قتل وكان يجب أن يقبح القتل على سبيل القصاص ، ولو حسنت المنفعة لذاتها ، لحسنت كل منفعة ولذة ، فكان يجب حسن الزنا واللواطة ، ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا أن القول بأن الحسن حسن لذاته ، وأن القبيح قبيح لذاته قول باطل .
وقالت المعتزلة : هذا الكلام غير وارد علينا ، لأنا لا نقول : إن الحسن إنما كان حسناً لذاته، وأن القبيح إنما كان قبيحاً لذاته . وإنما نقول : إن الحسن يحسن لوجوه عائلة إليه ، وإن القبيح إنما يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه . وعند هذا قالت الأشعرية : إن التعليل بالوجوه تحليل بأمر غير مفهوم ولا معقول . ونحن نذكر تفسير هذه الوجوه بحيث يصير المراد منه مفهوماً فنقول :
لا شك أن الإنسان قد يلتذ بتناول الخل ، إلا أن المقتضى لحصول تلك اللذة ليس هو تناول الخل فقط ، بل الموجب له مجموع أمور ، مثل أن يكون قد تقدم أكل الطعام الطيب الدسم ، وأن تكون المعدة قوية ، فعند تقدم ذلك الأكل يكون تناول الخل لذيذاً لمثل هذه المعدة. فعلى هذا لا نقول : إن الموجب لتلك اللذة ذات الخل ، بل الموجب مجموع هذه الأحوال والشرائط
وأيضاً : الإنسان يرغب في تناول الدواء المخصوص ، وليس المقتضى لحصول تلك الرغبة هو ذات ذلك الدواء ، وإنما المقتضى له مجموع أمور منها : حصول أخلاط مخصوصة في البدن. ومنها : أن لا توجد سائر الأدوية ومنها : ظهور آثار المرض في البدن فتصير مجموع هذه الأحوال موجباً لحصول الرغبة في
And know that a great group of the followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari - may God have mercy on him - argued for invalidating the saying that reason judges good and evil. They said: If killing were evil in its essence, then every killing would have to be evil, and killing by way of retaliation would have to be evil. And if benefit were good in its essence, then every benefit and pleasure would be good, so fornication and sodomy would have to be good. Since it is not so, we know that the statement that good is good in its essence and evil is evil in its essence is false. The Mu'tazila said: This speech does not apply to us, because we do not say that good is good in its essence and evil is evil in its essence. Rather we say that good is good due to aspects (wujuh) around it, and evil is evil due to aspects returning to it. Thereupon the Ash'ariyya said: explaining by aspects is an analysis of something unintelligible and unreasonable. And we will mention an interpretation of these aspects such that the intended meaning becomes understood, and we say: There is no doubt that a person may derive pleasure from consuming vinegar, but the cause for obtaining that pleasure is not merely consuming vinegar, but rather the cause is a combination of things, such as having previously eaten fatty, pleasant food and the stomach being strong. When that prior eating occurs, consuming vinegar becomes pleasurable for such a stomach. So we do not say that the cause of that pleasure is the essence of vinegar, but the cause is the combination of these conditions and circumstances. Also, a person desires to take a particular medicine, and the cause for obtaining that desire is not the essence of that medicine, but rather the cause is a combination of things, including: the presence of specific humors in the body, and the absence of other medicines, and the appearance of symptoms of illness in the body. So the combination of these conditions becomes the cause for the desire (to take that medicine).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument against intrinsic moral values and the Mu'tazila response, followed by Razi's own explanation. First, the Ash'ari argue: if killing were evil by essence, then every killing (including lawful retaliation) would be evil; if pleasure were good by essence, then all pleasures (including fornication) would be good. Since these are not the case, intrinsic values are false. The Mu'tazila reply: they do not claim intrinsic values; rather, good and evil depend on aspects (wujuh) that relate to the action. The Ash'ari counter that explaining with aspects is incoherent. Razi then provides an interpretation: just as the pleasure of vinegar depends on prior food and stomach condition, and the desire for medicine depends on bodily humors and the absence of alternatives, so good and evil depend on circumstances, not essence. The passage ends mid-sentence, continuing with further examples.

ذات
وجوه
تحسين وتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris deny all moral values and that actions have no moral significance.
Correction: Ash'aris deny that moral values are intrinsic to actions, but they affirm that actions can be good or evil based on divine command or contextual aspects. The passage shows that Ash'aris are open to explaining moral value through circumstances, not essence.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila position on good and evil as presented in this passage?
A) Good and evil are intrinsic to actions
B) Good and evil depend on aspects (wujuh) related to the action
C) Good and evil are determined solely by God's command
D) There is no good or evil, only convention

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari–Mu'tazila debate shows that moral values are not intrinsic but depend on aspects or circumstances, as illustrated by Razi's examples of vinegar and medicine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How might the examples of retaliation or medicine in this lesson challenge your absolutism? What would it take for you to reconsider a deeply held moral conviction?

The Ash'ari Argument Against Intrinsic Good and Evil
Ash'ari reductio argument and Mu'tazila initial response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core Ash'ari objection to the Mu'tazilite doctrine of rational good and evil, and the initial Mu'tazilite defense that good and evil are not intrinsic but due to aspects.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not intrinsic to acts; the Mu'tazila argue they depend on attendant aspects.

This passage presents the opening salvo in a major theological debate. The Ash'aris attack the idea that the intellect can judge actions as good or evil in themselves, using a reductio ad absurdum. The Mu'tazila respond by clarifying that they never claimed intrinsic moral values—rather, values arise from contextual aspects. The author then notes the Ash'ari charge that these aspects are unintelligible, setting up his explanation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari argument
Read the Ash'ari reductio: if killing were evil in itself, then all killing (including lawful retaliation) would be evil; if pleasure were good in itself, then all pleasures (including illicit ones) would be good. This is absurd, so the doctrine is false.
Notice the logical structure: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
2
Read the Mu'tazila response
The Mu'tazila immediately deny that they hold intrinsic values. They say good and evil are due to aspects (wujūh) that accrue to the act.
This is a key clarification: they are not committed to the Ash'ari's strawman.
3
Read the Ash'ari counter-objection
The Ash'aris retort that 'aspects' are unintelligible – an analysis that leads to something not understood.
This sets up the author's upcoming explanation.
4
Reflect on the debate structure
Consider how each side's position is presented: the Ash'aris attack a version of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, the Mu'tazila correct the misrepresentation, and the Ash'aris then question the alternative.
This back-and-forth is typical of kalām debates.
